The Red Page
Little Verses by Nettie Palmer
THE MOTHER.
In the sorrow and the terror of the nations,
In a world shaken through with lamentations,
Shall I dare know happiness
That I stitch a baby’s dress?
So: for I shall be a mother with the mothers,
I shall know the mother’s anguish like the
others,
Present joy must surely start
For the life beneath my heart.
Gods and men, ye know a woman’s glad un-
reason,
How she cannot bend and weep but in her
season,
Let my hours with rapture .glow
As the seams and stitches grow.
And I cannot hear the word of fire and
slaughter.
Do men die? Then live my child, my son,
my daughter!
Into realms of pain I bring
You for joy’s own offering.
A SEAFARONG.
To icalee this summer morning
And feel the boat move lightly,
The sea a silken wonder,
The far cliffs rising whitely,
A stretch of sea to cover
Before I meet my lover.
To catch the light of sunrise
Flung on the seaward ranges,
To see above Kiama
The heights all gleams and changes,
To guess their forests gleaming,
Their fern hung cataracts streaming.
To move along this coastland
Where silver gulls are wheeling,
The prow with rush and ripple
Still to the northward stealing
That when the daylight’s over
I there may meet my lover.
To hear our voices faintly
Because the sea has held us,
To feel the glory bringing
Strange comradeship to weld us,
As happy pilgrims faring
To some great joy for sharing.
To see far breakers flashing
By league-long cliffs and beaches.
Until, the coastline breaking
In countless radiant reaches,
My heart will rise and hover
With joy to meet my lover.
From “Shadowy Paths,”
by Nettie Palmer.
Nettie Palmer’s muse is a modest one,
Australian-born. She has something femin-
ine to say, and her voice is pleasing, deli-
cately modulated, and lyrical. Shadowy Paths
(Euston Press, Euston Buildings, London,
N.W.) strikes no new note in our Austra-
lian gamut, but it is a singing note, worth
hearing. Unfortunately this volume shows
no deepening of emotion or widening of
thought since her first essay in verse, but the
delicate standard set is maintained.
The Australian Attitude
It used to be a comfortably leaning one —
against a verandah-post. There was a
reason for that Australian attitude: the
Australian had a verandah-post to lean
against. The verandah is not a character-
istic portion of the flora or fauna of other
races. The outside peoples put down (and
pull up) sun-blinds. These are very gay and
picturesque when the sun comes out. But it
mostly doesn't come out. And there are no
verandah-posts to support them. But in
Australia a permanent iron verandah is
necessary because of our permanent iron
sun. And before the Australian could lean
against his verandah-post he had to dig the
hole and erect that post. And having pro-
vided the verandali-post the Australian had
a right to lean against it.
But, wait! It was usually the Aus-
tralian’s father or grandfather who dug the
necessary hole and planted the necessary
post. And it kept him too busy, providing
verandah-posts, to allow him any leisure to
lean against his own handiwork. But the
son or grandson found the landscape so
planted with verandah-posts that he saw no
use in attempting to erect any more. So he
leant against the nearest post. He was not
exactly lazy; really he was accumulating
energy, for which he found no immediate
vent.
For the Australian was living in a world
that did not suit his temperament at all.
You see, the modern Australian is The True-
Born Englishman—the only one extant. He
is the direct descendant of the Elizabethan
Englishman—the Englishman who made
England. That ancestor was pirate and
freebooter, with no nice sense' about playing
the game with scrupulous fairness to the
other bloke. He was out to win. Reckless
and carelessly courageous he mopped up the
world, for himself —and England. He
boasted, and proved his boast. He was un-
tamed, extravagant, unexpurgated; he was
the Immortal scallywag of the world, with a
humorous touch of the blackguard In him.
And he builded England for Elizabeth, and
us.
He builded England—too well. He made
her too secure. He bred from his proud
loins a race of shopkeepers, and set them up
in a little shop—to breed and in-breed other
shopkeepers. Nobody dared molest his de-
cendants; and even in their shop-keeping
there was the strain of adventure and world-
wandering that he had put so profitably
into his piracy. The shopkeepers, in their
turn, mopped up the world. They brought
their captured galleons of groceries tri-
umphantly home from all the seven seas.
And though something was stirring in the
world outside, wars and revolutions, they
cared not. secure in their island shops,
bringing their captured galleons of a world-
trade home.
Yet, here and there, an Elder Son was
born who refused to serve behind that high-
piled counter. He ran away, scornfully
leaving his inheritance to the youngsters.
He ventured far, urged ever on by that itch
of conquest in his Elizabethan forefathers’
blood. And so he came to Australia, and
found a new continent to conquer. It kept
him busy—but not at shopkeeping. He
planted a new race in an empty continent;
he planted verandah-posts in the sand. He
was too busy to afford the time to lean
against them. He left his sons, his grand-
sons, the ease and the leisure to lean. They
leaned because, though there was plenty of
hard work for them to do, all the exciting
work of the pioneers had been done. And
there was in the veins of this direct de-
scendant of the Elizabethan Englishman a
distaste for shopkeeping, a dislike to mere
uninspiriting labor. He wanted to ravage his
continent as his ancestor had ravaged the
oceans. And he found his continent full of
barbed-wire fences, and his cities thick with
verandah-posts. So, dissatisfied, he leaned,
waiting for something exciting, something
Elizabethan, to turn up.
And, thanks to an equally dissatisfied
bloke in Germany, it came. The invasion of
Belgium swung back this tame world into
the spacious days of Good Queen Bess.
Upon the seven seas the sinister black flag
was once more unfurled. The Australian left
his verandah-post; he had found something
congenial to do. He found it in Gallipoli,
and he did it with the ancestral delight in
facing tremendous odds. He was not doing
his duty: he was out for fun. Here were
real things to be done: battle, murder and
sudden death; and he tackled his stupendous
task with an Elizabethan light-heartedness
and an Elizabethan zest.
He found himself fighting beside his
younger brethren, the shopkeepers that he
had somewhat contemptuously left behind in
the shop. And they could fight, too. The
long, almost ignoble, security had tamed
their souls; but at the call, the old smoulder-
ing fire thrilled their veins. Their courage
was equal to his, but different. They had
an element of dour doggedness foreign to the
Australian’s reckless indifference. They
didn’t growl: their vision was more limited;
they were content with less. Give Tommy
his cigarette, his girl’s photograph; his
music-hall song, his lollies and his tea, and
Tommy would go anywhere, do anything;
it was all in the day’s work. But the Aus-
tralian brought to his job an element of
artistry, a deep, untroubled joy, a careless-
ness about discipline, an often fatal reck-
lessness, a “be-damned about everything”
attitude, and a facility in curses as pic-
turesque as that of the Elizabethan free-
booters. This was what he had dreamed
about when he was leaning against that
verandah-post; this is what he would dis-
cuss when he came back to lean against
that familiar post again. This was the life.
It wasn’t his duty; it was his joy. It was
the game for which he, of all races, had
been prepared and tempered and fitted —the
game of the True-Born Englishman.
And Gallipoli Betty, in the intervals of
writing to Gallipoli Bill, took to the game
with the same joyous spirit. She knitted
socks with the same delight that she had felt
when shooting the breakers. She slaved
in the Red Cross depots with the persistency
with which, afore-time, she had hunted for
dress-material bargains and made her own
frocks.
And those of us who couldn’t go took to
the new game with the same reckless light-
heartedness. Australia had to prepare for
her wounded, and how did she do it? With
a doleful foreboding? No, with a laugh!
This race made of Australia Day—one
State contributing three-quarters of a mil-
lion pounds by one supreme effort —a car-
nival and a f§te. Had any stranger visited
Sydney on that day would he, by any pos-
sibility, have guessed that here was a city
engaged in raising funds for her maimed
and shattered and desolate soldiers? No;
he would have found a great city en fete,
half a million enjoying a gigantic carnival,
showering sovereigns like confetti. One
would have thought these joyous, excited
citizens were celebrating a national victory
or indulging in a mad orgie: there was no
shadow, of war upon their gaiety. They
were throwing their money into the hat,
and in the act they found a reckless joy.
It was all such a lark!
And so, thanks to the Australian atti-
tude, Australia goes light-heartedly along,
the one happy country in a world sub-
merged in gloom. The heavy clouds of
sorrow' have obscured our sky; there are
thousands of weeping mothers and mourning
brides: but the racial cheerfulness, like our
sun, is high above all personal griefs. It is
incurable, our optimism. And it is idle to
deny that its manifestations are sometimes
foolish. We talk of economy, and spend
as lavishly as ever; Premiers still keep car-
nival with pawn-shops; High Commissioners
gaily ride out on camel-back into the
interior; new storeys are placed on new
public buildings; we shut the pubs one short
hour earlier and establish new beer-gardens
at the gates of Government House; we im-
port famous musicians and, as solemnly as
we can, we discuss the absorbing question of
altering our musical instruments to the Con-
tinental pitch—forgetting that very soon
that continent might not be sufficiently in-
tact to possess a pitch at all; and on Allies’
Day our girls masquerade in fancy costumes,
we flag our city as for a victory, we build a
bazaar in our main thoroughfare and crown
a queen of carnival!
J*
The younger son never knows when he is
beaten; the elder son would know—and
win. Both attitudes are of inestimable
racial value; both will win this war.
The War
The value of J’Accuse, by a German, pub-
lished by Dr. Anton Suter, of Lausanne,
does not lie in the alleged nationality of its
anonymous author, but in its brilliant indict-
ment of Germany as the criminal-in-chief
responsible for the present trouble. The
English translation (Ilodder and Stoughton,
per Dymock’s Library) is a closely reasoned
work of over 400 pages. The author’s patri-
otic purpose is to bring back the German
nation to a condition of national sobriety, to
awaken his compatriots to the discomforting
realisation of their crime and their blood-
guilt. In the preparation of his damning
brief the author proffers evidence “written
by Germans in the German language.” In
no other work on the war has the ground
been so carefully covered: the slightest
detail. becomes tremendously significant, and
even in the omissions in the Austrian and
German official excuses the author finds the
finger-prints of the murderer. He even finds
humor in the mailed fist. He points out that
for days after Germany had declared war
upon Russia —days during which Russia
and Germany were actually at war —Aus-
tria and Russia remained at peace. “The
Austrian and Russian Ambassadors remained
quietly in the capital of their enemies, and,
who knows, may even have continued nego-
tiations. The mobilised condition of their
armies did not disturb them in the enjoy-
ment of the comforts of peace. Surely noth-
ing equally insane has ever occurred in dip-
lomatic history. The two chief duellists
have not yet crossed swords, but the second
of one party has already attacked the other.
If in the six days between August 1 and
August 0 Austria and Russia had after all
succeeded in arriving at an agreement—a
consummation.... which could easily have
been realised but for the provocative inter-
vention of Germany—where in that case
would have been the sense of Germany’s war
against Russia? It would have been a sort
of war in the air, a tilting against wind-
mills, a war without any substantial
ground.”
It is as impossible to summarise within
the limits of our space the terms of this
tremendous indictment of Germany as it is
to count the number of times that the author
proves the German Chancellor guilty of delib-
erate lies. And for every item the author
has his circumstantial evidence, usually
written or spoken by one of the criminal
gang. The reader may doubt the nationality
of the author, but he cannot doubt the facts
nor dissent from the sentence of the court.
d*
In a remarkable article in October Fort-
nightly, A. E. P. B. Weigall diagnoses the
Hun mind. He proves the German to be,
in his method of thinking, as remote 'from
-the rest of civilisation as an inhabitant of the
planet Mars. The actions of the Huns are
governed by a code which does not, even
distantly, resemble our code. The Hun is a
slave to the cancer of logic—sheer, inhuman
logic. “The Germans see things as they
are; we see things as they ought to be. The
Germans are inspired by the doctrines of
unqualified intellect; we are inspired by the
dictates of what is called ‘heart.’ ” The
German admits the horrible facts of life;
we discreetly cover them up. Behind all
our food-supply, for instance, stands a hor-
rible fact, the slaughter-house. We try to
forget the slaughter-house. We disguise our
food. We call pig’s flesh pork: the Huns
call it pig’s flesh. “We enjoy our beefsteak
because we shut our eyes to the manner in
which it was obtained ; the Germans enjoy it
because they have learnt to look at the
slaughter-house with complacency.” So
they call a spade a spade, and a stomach a
stomach. “There is no people in Europe
which refers in such undisguised terms to
the functions of the body which we are wont
to hide. Human beings, they declare, are
but animals ; and a really civilised people can-
not any longer refuse to face facts because
they happen to be nasty... .There is no na-
tion... .which is inure boldly bestial... .Man,
say the Germans, is born of human passions;
then surely it is logical to admit their exist-
ence and their important rOle in life.” Hence
43 cases of rape recorded in Germany to
every one case recorded in England, 12 cases
of incest to each one in England, and the fre-
quency of “Eulenburg” scandals. Hence,
too, the subjects that the German artist de-
lights to paint: “Pregnant women; hairy,
amorous men; fat matrons suckling their
babies; sweating laborers.”
Our civilisation tends toward an increasing
regard for human life; but Nature does not
regard individual lives as precious, so why
should the Germans? Death is not a
tragedy; it is an inevitable part of Nature’s
process—then why worry over death? Hence,
340 murderous assaults in Germany to every
one in England, hence the attack in massed
formation. Human lives—their own lives,
still less Belgian lives, do not matter. Hence
the atrocities.'
• Honestly, the Huns consider our objections
to the sinking of passenger ships without
warning as due to our conservative method
of reasoning. They honestly laugh at us'
as the world's old fogies. “To them we are
like the early Victorian rural classes who
objected to the introduction of steam loco-
motion. Modern warfare, they contend, is
essentially the imposing of every kind of
horror on the enemy until he is so morally
outraged and dazed that his physical powers
of resistance are destroyed.” The idea of
a fair fight, of fair play, smacks of primitive
barbarism. The Huns indignantly repudi-
ated the English calling a certain chivalrous
Ilun officer a “sportsman.” A Ilun paper
thanked God that the German army did not
contain such an abortion as a sportsman on
its rolls. No German officer could be such
a fool as to subordinate the scientific busi-
ness of his profession to sentimental consid-
erations. So we are fighting against a type
of cold, logical intellect that does not admit
the existence of a heart. We are fighting
against beings as revolting as the octopi of
Wells’s Martians. According to the Huns,
it is we who are the barbarians: we still
attach a meaning to such terms as “sports-
man” and “man of honor.” They are a
people without a soul —and proud of that
fact. They honestly believe they are fight-
ing for the good of the world and the benefit
of the human race. “Logic told them that
our civilisation was bound to go under before
the onslaught of theirs; and they find that
after all materialism is likely to break itself
against the mighty walls of an ideal. They
hate us because just as we think their point
of view degrading, so they think our atti-
tude toward life obsolete, and they are irri-
tated by the solidarity of an Empire united
merely by a mere bond of heart.”
The Kaiser, quite honestly, is always call-
ing upon his God. “the good old German
God”; but, and significantly, neither Wil-
helm nor anybody else in Germany ever men-
tions a man called Jesus.
«
Australian caricature, the one department
in art in which this continent stands on
equal terms with the world-masters, receives
a welcome recruit in David Low. Hop, Phil
May, Will Dyson, Vincent and Low ; a record
equalled by few papers in the world; and
though The Bulletin does not claim that
it taught this quartette their art, it has had
the honor to give them their chance to
develop their talent. Caricatures by Low,
collected from The Bulletin and other
sources (Tyrrell’s, Limited, Castlereagh-
stieet, Sydney), is a limited edition at one
guinea—and worth it. It contains nearly
400 caricatures of Australasians, ranging
from State politicians to the mysterious per-
sonage, supposed to be the Emppror of Aus-
tria in retirement, who sells bootlaces on a
vacant block in George-street. There are
also four colored insets, one of which, that
of Henry Lawson, will henceforth deco-
rate the bare brick wall of this ofiice.
Naturally mere politicians bulk largely in
this collection, but temporary vice-regal
visitors, assorted bishops, judges, Gents
One, aldermen and members of the Inky
Way, the public services and sport make up
a survey of this continent and Maoriland
remarkable for its comprehensiveness. Every-
body who is anybody, and lots who aren’t,
will find themselves genially flayed herein.
Low puts no malice into his portraits: any
victim who cannot laugh over his idiosyn-
crasies thrown on the screen is as humorless
as Kultur. And his art is remarkably
varied: he escapes monotony by varying
from perfectly “straight” portraits to essays
in farce. Indeed, his art is so young yet
that he has not developed a line that could
be always identified as a “Low” without his
signature. For his subjects determine his
style. Here and there he has horribly
failed; but the majority of the caricatures
will be instantly recognised—-by everybody
but the victim. There is only one fault that
this page finds. Low has caricatured nearly
400 persons, and his edition is limited to
250 copies. What are the other 150 subjects
going to do about it?
Madeline E. King, whose black-and-white
work is well known in Sydney, forwards a
charming book of pen sketches entitled
Sydney. There is strength in these draw-
ings, and though her drawing of foliage is
occasionally niggling, this is but a slight
defect in a series of pictures of Sydney
buildings evidencing artistic power and sin-
cerity. Such a volume is valuable as a
Sydney souvenir. (Printed by J. Andrew,
Ph ill in-street. Svdney.)

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gallipoli, 1915.
The sun flares down,
And comes the night with its deep sleep
And sweet forgetfulness.
It matters not how went the fight,
Or if they won or lost;
But here and there, lit by sad stars,
A sleeping boy stirs restlessly,
And smiles, and dreams again
Of rain-green bills.
Under the burning sun they fight and fight.
The struggling line strains ceaselessly. It
breaks....
And forms again.
And all the time, along that valiant lino,
Too-wearied boys drop down like flics.
They lie there in the sun.
And underneath soft southern skies,
Where half the world seeks peace in sleep,
Now and again a mother starts
And wonders why she woke.
MAOBILAND.
LAURLE FOSTER.
When the Door Was Opened!
The Kaiser stepped up to the door.
Pulled hack the bolts- and out came War!
War looked about him. "Now,” said he,
“Who shall I take along with me?”
lie saw a gallant regiment—
He beckoned them, and off they went.
lie saw some citizens at work.
“Come on !” said War. ‘'You mustn’t shirk!
“If you would die, as you’ve free.
Take up your guns.and follow me!”
He met a sailor, home in port.
“Aha!” said War. “Come on, old sport!”
The sailor promptly gave three cheers:
"I've been expecting you for years!'’
War saw an old man in his shop,
Who begged to be allowed to stop ;
And to his plea War made reply :
“I’ll come back for you by-and-bye.”
War found a widow with her son:
She pleaded : “He’s my only one !”
War cried: “T can’t help that, you know;
For I am War, and he must go!”
And next War saw a child at play.
He stooped and snatched its life away.
He found a maiden fair and sweet.
And trampled her beneath his feet.
He saw a horse of noble breed,
And left it on the ground to bleed.
He saw some cattle, sheep and goats,
And drew bis blade across their throats.
Tie found a church with spire,
..And left it in a cloak of .ire.
He came upon a fair-built town,
And with his heel he stamped it down.
He strode across the country-side,
And where he trod all green things died.
A splendid bridge the river spanned ;
War broke it underneath his hand.
Then, turning from the land again,
He worked stark ruin on the main.
He saw a lofty ship that drave,
And straightway sank it in the wave.
He saw a little, humble bark,
And overwhelmed it in the dark.
A raving fiend with fury blind,
His monstrous bulk oppressed mankind,
Which yet must groan beneath his sway,
Until once more lie’s locked away.
Meantime, unnumbered curses pour
Upon the wretch wjio let out War!
n.s.w.
EDWARD NEWTON MAcCULLOCH.
By the Way.
You’ll forget in a day,
Though our kisses were more than sweet;
Ere the gold of the gold be grey,
Ere the poppies wilt in the wheat,
We’ll break from the ropes of sand,
Nor reck of the puny smart,
And I’ll go back to the dear grey-land
And my own sweetheart!
The red of the rose is blown.
Shall we weep for the perished musk?
The soul of the day is flown.
Shall we tryst with Death in the dusk?
Shall we fret for the faiths that stand?
Nay, dreamer and dream depart—
And I’ll go back to the dear grey land
And my own sweetheart!
You’ll forget in a day
That yesterday’s rose was born.
Lo! she'rides with the wind away
To the call of an elfin horn '
Maskers at Fate’s command,
’Tis finished —so. .. .smile and part—
And I’ll go back to the dear grey land
And my own sweetheart!
VICTORIA.
RIMEA.
The River.
Somewhere in the dark the river goes,
Feeling its way by the quiet streets,
Catching the gold that a blown lamp throws,
Sucking a garden's rooted sweets,
Gurgling down by a dreary lane,
Beneath a culvert and out again ;
Trapped, some squalid quarter in,
Flowing broken and sick as sin;
Then out of the town with a silver rush
To the star-still sweeps of the onen bush !
QTIEENSI-AND.
M. FORREST.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 2, 1915.
A DRAIN WITH A LEAK IN IT.
Ir takes the Australian States no more than
live years to get rid of £20,000,000 of public
money on “education, science and art.” An
immense sum is also contributed annually
by the population in fees to private schools ;
the Universities suck up a large revenue
from students and the investment of bene-
factions ; and the denominational schools
involve their supporters in further outlay.
Lumping it all, Australia one way and
another spends somewhere between five and
six millions a year to “educate” the child,
llelatively to population the bill is, perhaps,
without parallel anywhere in the world—a
matter of no particular moment, though,
because the Australian youngster requires
all the education the Old Man can give
him, for the youngster is going to have a
big job on hand when he gets into trousers,
and unless he has a well-trained brain and
hand he will find it mighty hard work.
His share of the reversionary interest under
the will is a large item called Debt; also a
partnership in a whole continent, sparsely
peopled, of which the undeveloped resources
of wealth and national strength can only
be measured by the degree of courage, skill
and enlightenment that may be brought to
bear upon their utilisation. Australia is
fertile, rich in minerals and climatically
one of the most favored spots on this poor
old earth, possessing all the elements neces-
sary to make it the home of a vigorous
race. Yet there are other places in the
world that are fertile and highly mineral-
ised and have endurable climates, and in
some of them the human race has
rotted to decay. And there are places that
are bleak, sterile miseries where men and
women have flourished and grown in
strength and done the things that really
matter. It is not the place that counts
most in this business, but the kind of place
the people make it, the direction ol: energy
into the right channels, the recognition
of opportunity, (lie education of the local
youngster to grapple with his environment
and stand up on both feet.
Five or six millions a year spent on the
education of about i oo,ooo young Austra-
lians is a big thing. Does the country get
full value for it? No one can tell. It is
conceivable that it might get equally good
results from half the expenditure or it might
get such tremendously better results by
spending an extra £1726 15s. Od. that this
Commonwealth would be a great, shining
example to the rest of the world. But as it
is impossible to take a footrule or a clinical
thermometer or a column of figures to
measure or reckon or cast up the. value of
the moral and material harvest derived from
all this ploughing and harrowing and fer-
tilising in the educational field, the problem
can only be handed on to posterity, along
with the statute book, the Murray waters
and the multitudinous p.n. But there is no
particular reason why the Australian citizen
of to-day should not make an effort to dis-
cover whether he is going on the right lines.
Tip to a certain point education merely con-
sists of acquiring a mass of raw information
which forms the foundation of all know-
ledge. A lawyer, a surgeon, a farmer, a
teamster, a miner, even a member of Par-
liament—all must first learn to read and
write and use figures. But that is, ot
course, not education. It is. so to speak,
merely the set of tools with which the indi-
vidual will work to gain real knowledge.
The test of a national system of educa-
tion is certainly not to be found in the
illiteracy tables, but is rather to be looked for
in the degree of thoroughness with which it
fits the youth of the country for the prac-
tical work of life. It is, for instance, no
sort of use laboriously turning thousands of
kids every vear out of an educational sausage
mill on the off-chance that they will get a
job in which their preliminary instruction
will prove useful. Such happy-go-lucky
principles lead straight to wood-and-water-
joeyism. If the Australian child is not to
be trained to get out of the rut, then lie
might just as well —perhaps better —-spend
most of his time playing cricket. It is the
business of Australian statesmanship to so
direct public policy that Australia may get
the utmost good out of the five or six million
a year spent on education —or 10 millions
if it is necessary. Up to the present this
is just what it has neglected to do. It
seems content to piously regard the expen-
diture on schools as bread cast on .the waters.
That there is an industrial side to the
matter is apparently not considered worth
attention.
* * * *
Now what will the Australia of to-morrow
want most? Clerks and shop-assistants or
engineers and chemists? Cart-drivers and
stevedores, or men skilled in industrial pro-
cesses relating to wool and metal? Handy
men ready to take any job that comes then-
way, or men trained to do the work which
Australia now pays foreigners at the other
end of the world to do? Farmers with
knowledge of cultural methods and the eco-
nomical use of land, or hayseeds, with
copious whiskers who are mostly soil-and-
seed-wasters ? Any intelligent golitlnna
could answer these questions on sight, and
his friend the native bear could explain that,
unless the Australian system of haphazard
education is made to minister to Australia s
requirements, the Australian kid is going, to
see, like his elder brother, nearly every job
in the Commonwealth requiring special skill
and education mopped up by individuals
hurriedly imported from somewhere ovei the
sea. To what extent, for instance, did the
expenditure of nearly six millions last veai
on “education, science and art, ’ strengthen
Australia’s capacity to capture the trade ot
Germany or even hang on to the trade the
Iluh had hurriedly dropped i It led the
young Australian mostly to a dead-end. and
the remaining kid was pushed to universities
where the principal industry is the turning
out of lawyers and M.A.'s and B.A/s, and
producing an occasional scientist who more
often than not has to take a Government job
at wages a laborer would reject. Meanwhile
the failure to apply scientific methods to
manv Australian industries is the cause ot
immense economic loss to the nation and
of downright waste of useful materials.
A SCHEME OF VOLUNTARY
COMPULSION.
Conscription masquerading as a volunteer
movement has at last arrived. Afraid to
face the one bitter word of contention, the
British Government has adopted Lord
Derby’s scheme, which in essence is forced
military service, with the pressure applied
by strange devices and through devious chan-
nels. instead of in the direct and simple
fashion in which real democrats are sup-
posed to prefer to do their business. Aus-
tralia, closely following the British lead, has
undertaken to raise an additional 50,000
men by a similar scheme, and the squeeze of
the new recruiting machinery may be ex
nected to be felt almost immediately by
those of military age. Briefly put, the plan
of operations is to divide the community into
sections according to age and fitness, and
then to invite it section by section, as re-
quired, to join the ranks of the army. If a
man responds to the invitation at once he
is welcomed; if he offers to do so at a futuie
date that date is noted and he is required to
keep his promise; if lie offers an excuse for
declining service that excuse is looked into
inquisitorially and may be either accepted or
brushed aside as insufficient; if he refuses
altogether life at home is apparently to be
made as little worth living as possible, the
State then taking the place of the flapper who
now hands out white feathers. At the same
time the outer door is barred and no person
of military age is allowed to leave the coun-
try without a passport. T In' difference
between such a scheme and straight-out.con-
scription is. on the face of it, one of Tweedle-
dum and Tweedledee; but in reality it is a
difference that counts much, for the new
plan is tricky, underhand and evasive, while
real conscription goes straight ahead to its
honest goal without subterfuge or pretence.
ft was always the pride of the British race
that it scorned evasion and sham, and sought
to do all its work in the light of day; and
what is true of British tradition in this
respect is still more true of Australian senti-
ment. W. M. Hughes is following a
British lead in announcing the method of (he
new recruiting campaign, but he knows, as
■ill men know, that the plan in its essence is
entirely un-British, as well as subversive ot
all Australian ideals. The bonfire of Lurope
is a big one; the position of the Allies is
insecure; the recent failures of English arms
are conspicuous. And for that vei.v leason
it is the more necessary that m the call foi
new and greater armies all things should be
done openly and above-board and in accord
with the feeling of the people who must bear
the burden. Conscription Australians could
understand, and, despite a small but noisj
section who curse it in the name of democ-
racy, failing to understand that it is the
most democratic of all military arrangements,
there is little doubt that as conscripts called
to service young Australians would accept
their duty cheerfully and do it efficiently. But
that men should be asked to volunteer undei
compulsion—for that is the paradoxical idea
of the new scheme —is sure to cause a sense
of irritation which is helpful neither to the
soldier nor to the cause he fights for. Beyond
all doubt the straight and open course would
have been the best in point of effectiveness
as well as the safest and most pleasant.
Britons never thought much of the rack and
thumbscrew methods, and are not likely to
do so now.
* * * *
Among the worst features of the new plan
is the suspicion it casts upon the whole
military system, and the openings it leaves
for favoritism and wire-pulling. The com-
munications to the possible recruits under
the scheme must be of a semi-private nature,
and their replies and excuses are for that
reason open to differential treatment.
Already, rightly or wrongly, there is a wide-
spread idea that military qualifications
and services and proffers of service are
not judged severely on their merits;
and this feeling is bound to be intensi-
fied when pressure of the sort indicated
begins to be applied. While the might-
be recruit is nominally free to go into camp
or stay outside as he pleases, there are
bound to be inequalities and injustices that
will gall and rankle. What is accepted as
a valid excuse in the case of Percy may be
dismissed as of no importance in the case of
Bill ; and Bill and his relatives will have
a grievance which will not be easily for-
gotten. Under straight-out conscription the
chances of that sort of thing would be dimin-
ished. Then Percy and Bill would both
be under compulsion to serve in the army of
their country, and any plea for non-service
would have to be considered openly and on
its merits. In plain fact, the Derby-
Hughes Devil-take-the-hindmost plan is nega-
tive, while conscription is simple, positive
and direct. The former denies the right
of an able-bodied man to loaf while the house
is on fire, and says that he shall be coerced
into action; the latter asserts his duty to
help in the protection of his own property,
and calls upon him in plain terms to perform
it. In the negative scheme there is scope
for malingering and evasion ; in the positive
scheme there is practically none.
* * * *
Still, the new plan is coming into opera-
tion, and the best must be made of it. The
point which the war has reached is so criti-
cal, and the need of men so great, that
almost any means of filling the ranks are
better than none. The pity is that, after
so may failures, due mostly to the disinclina-
tion of leaders to look facts squarely in the
face, the recruiting reserve of Australian
fighters should be exploited in an unneces-
sarily clumsy and unsatisfactory way.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Vague Invitation to Arise and Do
Nothing.
The old “Imperial” commonplaces still
come rumbling over the cables. One day it
is Rosebery speaking. The next, Milner.
The day after, Curzon. And the sound of
it all is very fine and large, and the mean-
ing of it all is very vague and nebulous. There
are many sentences about the time having
arrived, or being likely to put in an appear-
ance presently, for reconstructing the Empire
and welding the scattered communities closer
together. Then the language tapers away to
feeble platitude about nothing in particular,
and the problem supposed to be under review
falls down where it was before. But we
know quite well that it won’t be abandoned;
by-and-bye other people will hurry along
and pick it up and make more remarks
about it in a solemn tone, and others will
say “Hear, hear,” and great bewilderment
will prevail as usual. It would be a very
acceptable relief if the individuals who
periodically make noises in reference to this
business would say what they want —or, at
any rate, give a reasonably coherent indica-
tion of it. Schemes without number for
achieving “Imperial unity” have been put
forward and hurriedly pushed into a pigeon-
hole, because examination in detail have
shown them to be mainly projects for acquir-
ing a little limelight and a lot of chaos.
A few place-hunters and posers, most of
them alarmed at the spread of democratic
thought, have trifled with the business for a
day or two. and after raising a few super-
fluous cheers have faded into obscurity with-
out having explained themselves or their
alleged mission in terms anybody could
understand. Therefore this paper would
again suggest to Rosebery and co. that in
Australia there is no sort of enthusiasm for
mere words about the Empire, or any
anxietv to be stampeded into relationships
that would affect the status of Australia as
a free, self-governing country. There have
never been many Australians who looked
forward to the Commonwealth becoming part
of an organisation such as full-blooded Im-
perialists yearn to create, and the number
will probably be considerably less at the end
of a year or so. Wild talk about “Australia
henceforth taking her place in the counsels
of nations” is without point, if by this it is
meant that Australia has in . future to
become interested in the carving up ot
Africa and preserving the balance of power
in Macedonia. The war has done no single
thing to alter the fact that job
is here. But it has done this other thing :
it has shown that the present connection is
quite sufficient for all practical purposes.
So far from the Great Disturbance being an
invitation to “Imperialists” to meddle, Aus-
tralia's and Maorilaud s and Canadas pait
in it is really a command to them to let
well enough aione.
The Pickpocket Patriots.
Maoiuland was asked to lend the Govern-
ment a couple of millions the other day, and
rushed forward with offers of double the
amount. Whereupon the cheerful lou
Ward announced that lie would play this up
in “public works” because John Bull was
providing half a million a month to meet
local war outlay. Even if the stout little
baronet cannot see the indecency ot this,
surely the Dominion is not equally demora-
lised. This war is not over yet by a long
way. Already it is shaking the finance ot
every belligerent country to the. boots. The
s’de that can hold out longest will wm. h or
his part John Bull is faced with the stag-
o-ering job of finding over five millions a (lay.
And Ward, while he is tapping the hard-
pressed old man for a half-million a month, is
actually floating local loans to build a cul-
vert at Ngatgapunaiawalii and a bridge at
Maungaparihawiki. Ward and Massey
and the rest of them are great warriors
when they see a chance of shouting their
patriotism, but their frequent yells to that
effect would sound better in John Bulls
ear if the vellers were not at the same
moment pulling at his pocket so shamelessly.
Toasted Cheese.
When turning down the offer of a job as
N.S.W. Commissioner for Irrigation m
order to get into khaki. ex-Miuister for
Education Carmichael said: —
I do not believe those people are right who sav
that members of Parliament should not be eligible
for public appointments.
Probably not; but The Bulletin has no
doubt whatever that the fewer individuals
who are pushed or dropped or thrown from
active politics into jobs for which the tax-
payers find the cash, the better for the coun-
try. Nor has it even a shadow ot doubt
that the appointment of party heelers and
suckers and hangers-on, merely because they
may be of a certain unclean use to the
politician, is a scandal on the name of
democratic government. Take the case ot
Carmichael. Why was this job ottered
him? Because of his outstanding qualifica-
tions, or because the persons in whose power
it rested to make an appointment felt that
it was politically desirable for Carmichael
not to be on the premises? The answer is
easy. There are probably 500 men
in Australia better fitted for the job than
Carmichael. But that didn’t matter. They
were not looked for. They were not
wanted. The main thing on hand was to
side-track a restless, adroit, ambitious indi-
vidual who might at any moment become
dangerous. Therefore he was offered the
toasted cheese. He might have made a good
Commissioner. The Bulletin expresses
no opinion on that point. What it does say,
however, is that most decent men will think
better of Carmichael for having turned his
back on the cheese, and less of the wire-
pullers who were using the public funds to
buy the bait.

The Wasters.
During last week 7432 persons leaped to
their feet in Australia and made loud re-
marks about “economy,” the jangle of words
including a sermon from llolman on the
great nourishment to be derived from “loans
and a deluge of nonsense from other quarters
about the virtue or immorality of “spending.
Most of these people appear to. have been
afflicted with the delusion that in speaking
about economy they were addressing them-
selves to some esoteric problem of great pro-
fundity. Yet it is all simple enough. Aus-
tralia lias been for years and years on a wild
jamboree with borrowed cash, frantically
importing huge quantities of goods with the
proceeds and not producing sufficient to pay
the bill. Now when most of the cash in the
world is wanted for the big shooting match.
Australia is called upon to think about get-
ting sober, for that is what economy means
—that Australia shall live on her production
instead of the production of other countries,
secured on tick. Talk about balancing pu
vate economy by public expenditure, and
about the duty of everyone to keep on buy-
ing or to knock off beer or riding in a tram-
car, or to live on bandicoot —it is mere
blither. The only kind of economy that is ot
any value is the brand that will force the
host of parasites who have flourished on the
great national borrow and bloat to turn to
honest toil; that will shut the door on the
flood of imports still pouring in; that will
produce the commodities required by the
people of Australia and their creditors. I his
is a job for the politicians; but they are in
such a tremendous hurry to tackle it that
most of them have gone into hiding, or are
busy talking a cloud of words about some-
thing else. In the meantime their own reck-
less extravagance is an outrageous offence
against the nation, and their whining cadge
to Britain a shameful spectacle. Here they
are, while snivelling about economy, spent
ing money more furiously than they e\e
did before; wringing more and more trom
the public pocket; giving sanction to fulthei
huge additions to the cost ot government
an hour when the burden of a gigantic "
debt is being added to the load .already
accumulated, and when the countiy is i -
rapidly stripped of its manhood. In me
“Buried at Sea.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Golden clouds on Samothrake;
Brazen cliffs, apd purple wave.
And one' trawler creeping slowly
With its cargo <|i the brave,
With the best and bravest freighted,
Stark and sheeted, iron-weighted,
Slipping swiftly, bending lowly,
To their ocean grave !
Starlight on dim Samothrake ;
Lambent shrapnel on the shore.
Snarling Maxims vigil-keeping
And the Dreadnought’s deeper roar!
Deep they sleep who won our glory,
But their names shall live in story,
When the last great gun is sleeping—
Live for evermore!

Cost o’ Living and a Few Other Things.
Two years ago Justice Heydon, of the
X. S. Wales Arbitration Court, came to the
conclusion that £2 Ss. was the living wage
and should be the basis of awards. The
other day he discovered that the cost of
existence was £3 35., and his Honor was so
staggered by the figures that he decided it
would be dangerous to fix wages accordingly.
Therefore, so to speak, he threw his job up
iu despair, and “handed the problem on to
the Government.” And the natural impulse
of the Government will be to rush round and
see if it can borrow a few millions some-
where to advance to copper-mining com-
panies, or to use in putting up some new
buildings or tearing down old ones. This
would keep a certain number of men em-
ployed for a while; and as thousands of
other workers are leaving the State for the
trenches, the remainder eouhl probably get
along somehow. Meanwhile the time seems
to he about ripe for considering Justice
lleydon. If it was a fair thing for the cost
of living to he a factor in fixing wages a
year ago, what are the circumstances which
now justify the Court in saying that to give
men enough wages to live on is dangerous?
It seems to this paper a lot more danger-
ous to give them less than enough, anyhow.
And if the Court is going to take up this
attitude of fearfulness about the statute it
has to administer, then the whole business
collapses; for with workers going out on
strike every day, and the Arbitration Court
publicly washing its hands, the arbitration
system merely becomes an expensive joke.
Meanwhile there is at least some hope ; for
the Government has a wild-eyed scheme for
borrowing half a million to buy out all the
retail bakers in Sydney and (after borrow-
ing some more to patch up the bakehouses
and ovens) starting to sell bread at a
ha’penny a loaf cheaper than Private Enter-
prise. As this would result in the average
worker’s family saving about (id. a week on
bread, it will be seen that the hiatus between
£2 Bs. and £3 3s. is receiving close attention.
And as the aforesaid worker’s family will
certainly be taxed another shilling a week
to meet the loan interest on the current
year’s borrowings, everyone will be greatly
dissatisfied and nothing will have been
accomplished. Which is the end of a quarter
of a century of borrowing and bloating and
developing our great natural resources!
The Forest Timbers of Tasmania.
Consider once more the conservation and
development of our great natural resources.
The Tasmanian Government has sold at
£l, at 10s., and sometimes less per acre many
thousands of acres of land covered with mag-
nificent timber. The selector has ringbarked
the timber and sent it up in smoke. In
many places the game still goes on: in
others the Government has pulled up to have
a think. One of the latter places is Tyenna,
less than 50 miles from Hobart as the crow
crawls. Several thousands of acres have
there been practically given away, and now
two experts have estimated, in an official
report to the Minister, that there are over
15.000 acres left, which carry an average of
50.000 superficial feet of commercial milling
timber per acre. Allowing 20 per cent, for
waste, this leaves over 000,000,000 feet of
timber, of which much is “Tasmanian oak”
and other valuable cabinet-making wood. At
the ridiculously low price of ss. per 100 ft.
the lot would be worth £1,500,000, or £lOO per
acre. If the Government could net Is. per
100 feet as profit or royalty, it would rake
in £300,000, or £2O per acre, and even a
penny per 100 feet would bring in nearly £2
per acre. The figures as to the quantity
of timber seem steep, but the estimate was
made after a careful investigation by the
Chief Inspector of Timber and a practical
sawmiller. The Government’s present idea
of dealing with this asset is to put in a two-
penny-halfpenny mill to cut 25,000 feet of
timber per week, at which rate the forest will
be cut out. in 500 years or a little less. Of
course there is an agitation for “opening up
the land to the settler,” aud if someone does
not wake up this forest will go the way of
others just as good, and politicians will talk
about the progress of the country. Mean-
while Australia is importing timber by the
shipload from Puget Sound and various other
places on the far side of the world, and the
State Government of the Speck which con-
tains this forest and scores of bigger ones is
drawing from its timber lands the absurd
revenue of £4500 per "year in timber licenses
and leases, and is wondering why it lias
such a job to make both ends meet.
Attorney-General David Hall’s notion of
abolishing Private Enterprise in the baking
of Sydney’s bread has roused the usual oppo-
sition in the usual quarters. Sosh is held by
interested persons to he a beast of prey
whether his paws are dipped in flour or en-
gaged scratching out bricks, and the old cry
that the Government can’t manage things as
well as the individual is heard again. There
is, of course, no reason why a big State
bakery to feed all Sydney should not be a
financial success. Theoretically it ought to
be. The saving in carts and horseshoes and
drivers ought to ho very large. But the
present Government has exhibited such a
genius for wasting money that nobody can
hand over a cherished theory with aught but
fear that the poor thing will be butchered to
buy another few votes. And there is a fur-
ther doubt. At present the bakery business
is of mixed merit. Some very inferior bread
is sold and some very excellent. Some people
like the bad bread best; others prefer the
good. If the bread we get is not to our lik-
ing, or the baker’s boy has dirty hands (as
he usually has, to the shame of his
employer), or the cart arrives too bite, or
persistent mistakes are made in the bill, we
can sack the baker and get another. But we
couldn’t sack David Hall even if the dough
was delivered raw in old coal sacks. If the
Government would make mock of its
theories and start about ten bakeries under
different managements with special bonuses
for the one that secured most business, and
penal servitude for all connected with the one
which came lowest on the list, we might get
good bread and get it cheaper; but the
scheme which the discredited N. S. Wales
Labor party is supposed to be discussing in
whispers in some dark cellar, though in
theory it is all right, looks its though it might
be full of indigestion in practice.
»
A sort of half-promise has been given that
the habits of debt-collecting agencies in X. S.
Wales will he legislatively restricted. The
need for some action was instanced by a
recent case, in which an agency in pursuit
of a substantial “collecting fee,” as well as
the amount of the debt, carried its bluff to
the extent of getting a summons out against
the defaulter. As a matter of fact he had
only defaulted to the extent of refusing to
succumb to the agency’s disguised robbery ;
for when it had refused to accept the bare
debt, he had gone direct to his creditor and
handed over the money. The bluffing agency
has of course no right to add any charges as
against the debtor, its reward being solely
the commission that its client, the creditor,
has agreed to pay. Nevertheless “cost of
collection” or “interest and expenses” is gen-
erally added, in a desire to collect a double
commission, and in the not altogether ground-
less hope that the person who is influenced to
pay a debt by a threatening letter will like-
wise be influenced to _ pay “fees.” The
agency in. the case mentioned did not appear
in court, and nothing would have been heard
of the circumstances if the solicitor who had
been engaged by the defendant, anxious to
earn his costs, had not inquired bitterly if
there was no redress against the firm that
had made his presence necessary. The Court
made some remarks, but said there wasn’t.
A year or so ago the Law sat heavily on
the practice of sending out blue papers that
purported to be processes of Court as a final
threat to recalcitrant debtors: and if the Law
cannot sit on other bad habits, the Legisla-
ture should help it to find a seat.

The X. S. Wales prospecting vote has this
year been cut down from £15,000 to £lO.OOO.
Whether or not it is intended as such, it is
a belated acknowledgment of the failure of
the system. The principle of giving sub-
stantial aid for the encouragement of legiti-
mate mining enterprise may he commended :
at any rate it has as much to commend it
as has the granting of assistance to other
industries by bonuses, etc. But the X. S.
Wales system of apportioning the assistance
is wrong. The Board controlling the vote
has to keep within strictly-defined lines,
which do not embrace any large project. It
is a perpetuation of the old-time plan of
doling out £5O here and £5O there —a system
invented by politicians for vote-buying pur-
poses. The ancient prospector whom it
might have benefited to some extent no
longer exists, or if he exists he does not
wield pick and shovel. He may still indulge
irx the fascinating dreams of the fabulous
richness of the extension of the old lead,
but the beneficent old-age pension relieves
him of the necessity to delve in quest of it.
All the other States apply the principle on a
broader basis. In Victoria there is appro-
priated annually £25.000 for companies and
may be granted a loan up to £lO,OOO with
or without security. In the former case a
first mortgage is given over the mining
lease, a bill of sale over the machinery, and
an agreement to pay within three years ; it
granted without security, only an agreement
t j repay is given. A co-operative party may
be granted up to £250, an undertaking being
given to repay before dividing any profits.
It is not claimed that this system returns
any direct profit to the State; it was never
intended that it should. An aggregate bal-
ance-sheet over a series of years would show
a big deficit; yet the timely assistance which
would enable a struggling company to tide
over a temporary difficulty and work up to
success might show more than a counter-
balancing indirect advantage. Until Hol-
man's recent plunge as a State mining
speculator in the Cobar deal this had been
persistently ignored by X. S. Wales politi-
cians. Whatever may be said about that
particular case the principle underlying the
grant is far more defensible than that on
which the prospecting vote has hitherto been
doled out.

“The Old Soldier’s” verdict:-
General Mimio was probably right when he
recommended that military operations in Gallipoli
be discontinued during the winter, llis opinion is
certainly worth more than that of the politicians
at Westminster, and now that operations have been
begun against Bulgaria from the Salonica base it
would be a breach of all the rules to keep two
armies so near together under independent com-
manders. They ought to join their forces, and
mutually support each other. Circumstances have
so completely changed since Bulgaria took a hand
that both the Dardanelles and Constantinople arc
now of secondary importance. The argument for a
readjustment of positions is strengthened by the
fact that the Gallipoli Peninsula is so narrow that
it offers no facilities for offensive operations, while
its rugged surface and the small front of operations
it allows are all in favor of the Turks and their
German allies.
The Wages Board lately constituted to
deal with the conditions of N. S. Wales clerks
has already granted a number of exemp-
tions, and there will probably he more. The
most notable so far apply to banks and in-
surance offices. This will not, however, pre-
vent a further application for a separate
Board to deal with the employees in such
places; or their conditions may be dealt
with through the Federal Arbitration
Court —which, considering that banks and
insurance offices are mostly Federal in their
business, would be more satisfactory.
Solicitors’ clerks and architects’ clerks are
excluded by the Act from jurisdiction. Some
very bad sweating, of a systematic sort,
exists in all the excluded employments; and
though it is therefore unfortunate that
these are beyond jurisdiction, the Act
at least gives a very large number of
hungry citizens a chance of getting their
conditions improved, as the shop assistants,
who were still more sweated, did years ago
through the same benevolent agency.

A MINOR evil of the voluntary military ser-
vice system is that it breeds prigs. A cer-
tain type of young man as soon as he enlists
is filled with a tremendous self-righteousness
and exudes contempt for those who exercise
the right given them by the voluntary sys-
tem . of staying at home and making the
nation’s songs, boots or porous plasters.
Nobody knows better than those who have
been through the mill that everybody who
enlists is not necessarily the noblest of
beings, any more than every male of mili-
tary age who fails to do so is of necessity
a cur and a blight upon the landscape. The
fit man “without encumbrances” who joins
the forces is doing no more than, in a pro-
perly-constituted democracy, he would be
required by law to do. and for doing which
he would have no more excuse to “swank”
than a man has who pays any other tax. It is
to the credit of the average Billjim that
he sees nothing specially noble in enlisting,
and he doesn't feel disposed to preach to
those who hang back. But there is a class
which, as soon as it gets into uniform, is
overcome with admiration of the nobility
of the course it has taken, and points to
itself as a splendid example for the nation
to gaze on and applaud. Such a man may
bo a hero, but even heroes can be objection-
able. If we had universal service the chance
for him to show his heroism would remain,
but the opportunity to be objectionable about
it would no longer exist.
From an Australian at work in the Panama
Canal Zone:—
State-ownership, being well-directed, is panning
out all right here. It got a fair start to begin
with, as Vested Interests were unable to get their
beaks into the dish while the canal was in process
of formation, everything in the Zone being strictly in
the hands of United States Government officials. The
Zone authority collects canal dues, attends to
pilotage, provides bunker coal, runs an enormous
ship-chandlery business (supplying everything that
a ship requires, from frozen mutton to a patent
log), maintains a hospital, publishes a weekly paper,
the Cax.Uj Uncori>, and even takes on laundry work.
Canal employees are supplied with all they require
—at a cheaper rate, by the way, than is charged to
ships passing through. Prices even to the ships
compare very favorably with those charged in other
ports of the world. It is one of the most compre-
hensive experiments in State ownership at present
on trial. Private Enterprise finds that it is alto-
gether unable to compete, so steamship agents, coal
agents and other parasites are getting very lean.
Recently these unnecessary evils have been raising
their voices to high heaven (otherwise Washington,
D.C.), declaring in tearful -tones that it is impossi-
ble for them to pay their laundry expenses, to say
nothing of meeting tucker and tobacco bills, and
calling loudly on high heaven (meaning Washing-
ton, D.C.) to put an end to the monstrous monopoly
of the Panama Canal (which is really the United
States Government). Up to date high heaven (which
is to say Washington, D.C.) has turned a deaf ear
to the would-be fat-man’s supplication. It has done
more. Gko. W. Coethals, Governor of the Canal
Zone for the U.S., has intimated to shipowners and
shipmasters that “the employment of agents on the
isthmus is not only unnecessary but frequently results
in delays which might otherwise be avoided.” In
view of this warning, with freights up in the sky
as they are at present, and ships’ time corres-
pondingly valuable, the chances of Private Enter-
prise doing much or any business are exceedingly
meagre.
In Victoria, where over 500 cases of menin-
gitis, resulting in 200-odd deaths, have been
recorded since the disease was made a noti-
fiable one in August last, the Health De-
partment has at last decided to do something
terribly drastic. On the recommendation of
the newly-formed Medical Committee it is
distributing a leaflet! Briefly, this sheet
states that the symptoms of meningitis are
“sliiverings, fever, headache and vomiting,
with the head drawn rigidly back, and often
a dark, measly rash.” Hospital treatment
consists in the “use of special serum.” Pre-
ventive treatment involves the isolation of
contacts (so widely neglected by the power-
less Health Department), "the general prac-
tice of disinfection of the nose and throat,”
with good ventilation and “the avoidance of
overcrowding, especially in ill-ventilated
places.” For the throat-disinfecting “an
ounce of permanganate of potash crystals in
a pint of water makes a strong solution,”
and a teaspoonful of this in a tumbler of
water is the correct thing for use with a
spray-producer, or as a gargle, night and
morning. “Every cold,” furthermore and
particularly, “should be looked upon with
suspicion and cured as quickly as possible. ’
All of which is no doubt excellent; but the
people who spread meningitis around don t
bother about such recommendations. “The
disease in August,” it is stated, “did not
spread nearly so widely among civilians, but
if has persisted much more obstinately
among them because the measures adopted
by the military authorities have not been
adopted generally by civilians.” Exactly !
The horror has persisted for the simple
reason that the civil authorities haven’t the
power to make civilians adopt the measures
effectively employed among soldiers. Ihe
State Parliament is too busy drawing its
£4OO per annum, or wrangling about Minis-
terial jobs, to attend to a little matter like
this.

“Rifleman” : With the blazing eye of pro-
phecy, I can see the stay-at-home Australian
of some months or years hence standing
abashed before regiments of hard-bitten sol-
dier-travellers, who have been seeing things
for themselves from the inside and growing
more and more fiercely patriotic as their
education progressed. They will return to
this rich, isolated and industrially backward
country with new ideas and ideals, and the
old. slack order of things won’t satisfy them.
The stay-at-home who neither fought nor
planned for the future will seem to them the
most hopeless kind of person, and they will
convict him of laziness and incompetence
every time they encounter him in a tram or
see him coming out of a bar. It will be
impressed on him at least twice a day that
he had a great opportunity and missed it,
that his civilian garb is the badge of shame,
and that the raggedest scarecrow in war-
worn khaki has a perfect right to shoulder
him off the footpath. It really won’t be
worth while being a civilian in those times.
In the angry days to be, the mere possession
of a wife and family won't be sufficient ex-
cuse for the shirker of national responsibili-
ties. Domestic ties don’t prevent a real live
community getting ready to practise the arts
of peace.

Was <T. D. Fitzgerald, of the Holman
Government, expounding to the Leg. Coun-
cil the new ethics of Labor when in refer-
ence to the early closing of pubs lie observed
that “we must not punish individuals who
have built up businesses under the protec-
tion of the law”? Perhaps it doesn't matter
much; but even coming from a member of
the Holman Ministry the words are ominous
reminders of the decadence of the Labor
party. Labor made no such snuffling
appeals when the early closing of.
shops and factories was up for debate;
nor did it ever show itself parti-
cularly solicitous about individuals and
their pockets when it approached reform in
A Song of Famine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WAS not made where the hammers leap
And the white-hot steel in the furnace
glows;
Out of tlie dark of the primal deep
Through naked shudders of death I rose.
Stark in the desert I reared my flag.
With a vulture croak for my battle-cry;
And still from the world's green peace I drag
My tortured victims afar to die.
Proudly they marched in their strong array,
Banner and lance and plume and gun.
What to me was the shot-torn fray?
Bloodless my triumphs were fought and
won.
Bose through the years the pride of men.
Tilth and vineyard and home and mart.
Glory of song and might of pen.
While swathed in my sands I dwelt apart.
Laughter borne on the desert wind
Scorn on my throne and my kingdom set.
“Laugh,” I cried, “in your wisdom blind.
But your wisest children shall call me
yet!”
Bose a. horror of blood and tears,
Bed death trampled the world at will;
And the trembling kings in their doubts
and fears
Forged the death that the death might
kill.
Wailing, borne on the desert wind.
Crept and murmured about my throne.
Death from your forges, ye people blind.
Is it all the war-craft your years have
known ?
Suddenly out of the dark they called—
“Come to us. King, in our hour of need !
The place of the foeman is thunder-walled.
And under his lightnings our people bleed.”
Stern I rose at their shuddering cry.
Mighty in arms abroad I strode ;
What to me were the bolts that fly?
What were the trenches that barred my
road ?
Hard at the throat of their foe I gripped.
White grew his face where the war-clouds
rolled,
Cold the sweat from his forehead dripped
As he strove to break from my close-locked
hold.
Master am I where your strong arms failed ;
I have heai-d your bidding, the strife is
done.
Where your legions halted and leaders paled
My ancient arms at your call have won.
What is my guerdon in years to he?
Will ye pay me toil for my faith and toil :
Oh. haggard nations that called to me.
I claim my share of the victor’s spoil.
n.s.w.

regard to industrial conditions. Fitz-

The Long, Long Wrestle.
Winter has leaped into the trenches from
the Baltic to Gallipoli, and the soldier is
now as busy wrestling with wet feet and a
damp shirt as he is with the enemy. In the
Balkans the mercury crawls to unusual
depths, and someone has remarked with an
air of authority that this is a good thing
for the Allies. It may be so; but it strikes
this paper that it would have been a long
way better if the Allies had arrived on the
scene ahead of the frozen rain, and had
made themselves comfortable before the Hun
got his thump in on Servia. And a terrible
thump it has been—-a ghastly illustration of
what war means to a nation under the har-
row. Apparently the army has been broken
into fragments and the population driven, a
starving, wretched herd, into the mountains
and southward towards Greece. Belgium,
Boland, Servia —what a trinity of misery
and woe! And while Kultur is thus glut-
ting itself, the Turk is apparently letting
himself go in Armenia, so that in some dis-
tricts human life has been there almost
exterminated. Yet with all this infamy de-
manding that justice be handed to the per-
petrators on the end of a bayonet, with the
enemy still presenting an unbroken front
and still powerful for evil, there are people
talking comfortably of peace and dron-
ing about the struggle for ascendancy being
almost over. The Yankee mountebank
Ford, who was lately howling advice to the
United States people to “tie a tin-can on the
tail of the Anglo-French Financial Commis-
sion, and hunt it back home,” and is now
embarking on a circus voyage to
Europe for the alleged purpose of “getting
(he boys out of the trenches before Christ-
mas,” is, of course, only advertising him-
self according to his old methods But there
are apparently in all countries, Britain as
well as Germany, influences at work to get
the towel thrown in. That the Hun, who
started at his top, should be feeling that
way, is not surprising; but that Britain,
Russia and France, who haven’t yet reached
their top, should even consider Teuton terms,
is unthinkable. To beat him flat will be a
tremendous. task, probably involving every-
thing that is meant by a war of attrition;
but to spare him any part of what this
implies, for the sake of sparing ourselves,
would be the madness of suicide. In spite
of what has happened in the Balkans the
Allies have their hand on the brute’s throat,
and if they hang to him long enough they
will surely stifle him. The German knows
it, too. The chief factors in determining the
time it will take us consist of our courage
and resolution, and their offspring, recruits.
.
In a signed letter F. E. Strangward, mana-
ger of the Commonwealth Pottery and In-
sulator Works at Blackburn (Vic.), makes
charges that ought to set P.M.G. Webster
moving. Strangward states that early
this year his company got an order from the
Post Office for 150,000 insulators at 3Jd.
each, on condition that the company’s books
were inspected by a departmental auditor.
The goods were duly supplied in four months,
at a cost of 3.674 d. each, less per cent,
deduction on account of “defects.” None of
the “defects,” however, were returned, and
Strangward says the Department used
them with the others. Next, a tender for
200,000 was accepted, at 3fd. each f.o.b. Mel-
bourne. The co. supplied and paid carriage
on them, but about 7 per cent, were rejected
on account of chips and other faults. These
however the Department re-packed and sent
to other States, then carefully deducted
their value from the account. Of another
lot supplied at 2.55 d. (German price) the
Department deducted 23,500 as defective, yet
afterwards coolly put them in use. Tenders
were called in October for 100,000 insulators,
and as the.ico.’s works are now idle it lately
inquired about this lot, only to learn that
the “Department has a year’s supply in
hand.” What the Department’s game is
Heaven knows, but there is a suspicion
that it is buying Japanese insulators at the
tendered figure—2-kl. each. This may suit
the Jap. manufacturers, but it is up to
Webster, P.M.G., to explain wherein lies
the advantage to Australia, seeing that the
Jap. co.’s books aren’t audited, neither does
it pay war tax, income tax, land tax, muni-
cipal rates nor minimum wages, while the
Commonwealth Insulator Co. does all those
things.
Dear Bulletin,— My friend Smithson is
having more trouble with the men he engaged
to build his new house. It is a large man-
sion, to be constructed regardless of expense,
and he has about 100 alleged tradesmen em-
ployed under a foreman and seven or eight
sub-foremen. The edifice is progressing with
a gradualness that has driven him hopeless;
but he, unfortunately, engaged the men for
three years and can’t sack them till then.
His present trouble is that some of his
youngsters have contracted typhoid, and
the doctor has ordered him to leave his
present abode immediately. He inter-
viewed his foreman and desired him to
hurry up and put the roof on the new house
so that he might get into it. The foreman
consulted the sub-foremen, and particularly
the foreman plumber, and then assured
Smithson that there was no time to put
the roof on before, the Christmas recess, but
that the whole staff would take off its coat
and bog into the task of putting one
sheet of iron over a corner of a bed-
room. Smithson tells me that this is really
a great favor, and that he is expected to be
duly thankful and to consider himself hon-
ored. Seeing that these foremen get over
£lOOO a year apiece, and the tradesmen £0
a week each, I consider Smithson is ex-
pected to be grateful for dashed little. I am
reminded of this by reading that Victoria’s
Health Minister McLeod is introducing a
short Bill to amend the Health Act, so as
to cope with the meningitis outbreak.
McLeod says the Health Act is notoriously
useless, but there is no time at present to
amend it in the many directions needed. —
Yours, F. C.

“Alamon”: At Pine Creek (N.T.), while
the hotel girls were on strike for better
treatment and higher wages, unionists were
picketing the State pub to prevent meals or
drink being served —and incidentally were
obtaining their own supplies at the Chinese
restaurant, a place run by Chow labor at
Chow wages. The unionist of the N.T., who
would not be seen dead in the same street
with a non-unionist white man, employs
Chinese tailor, Chinese laundryman, Chinese
storekeeper, Chinese cook and Chinese sly-
grog-seller. He also employs native labor
and howls like a poisoned dingo when the
Government orders that the blacks should
receive higher wages and better treatment
in . the way of accommodation. In short,
unionism in the Territory is a perfect hog
for consistency.
“M.A.”: Sydney. ’Varsity Senate has
bowed its conservative old head and decided
to abolish those archaic relics of the educa-
tional system, the Junior and Senior exami-
nations. Ex-Minister George Beeby passed
death on them when he inaugurated the
public-school system of Intermediate and
Leaving Certificates. The secondary public
schools, and more particularly certain pri-
vate schools, objected to send their pupils to
the University through the same channel
as the common or garden public schools,
and they managed to persuade the Senate
to cling to the ancient Junior and Senior
as an alternative route. Whatever chance this
mistaken policy had of success was spoilt
by the common-sense of teachers and parents,
who failed to see the use of compelling
pupils to go through a curriculum which
the public schools escaped. Something like
a passive strike took place, and realising
the foolishness of it all the Senate has wisely
discarded the useless system.
( J. T. Whitsitt, the stormy petrel of the
Speck’s politics, has been stirring up the
Defence Department on the subject of
returned Tasmanian soldiers and their
treatment. He alleges that they are kicked
about from pillar to post, have a hard job
to collect their pay, and get very little help
from the various patriotic funds to which
the public has subscribed. He also alleged
that when the public sent fruit and beer on
board the transport for the troops
the soldiers didn’t see the fruit and had the
privilege of paying for the beer. Of
course the official reply is that everything
is all right. It always is.

George Titheradge’s health has been far
from satisfactory of late, and the theatrical
people are going to tender him a compli-
mentary souvenir performance at tier
Majesty’s, Sydney, on Friday afternoon,
December 10. This year of war is the
fiftieth of good old “Tith’s” connection with
the stage, and everyone concerned wants to
make his jubilee and farewell a memorable
artistic and financial success. The generous
Australian playgoer, who has time-and-again
exulted in "Tith’s” art, may be relied upon
to attend to the financial side.

Guard” is timed to march into Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, on Saturday, so "Paul Jones” will
have to scuttle the ship on the preceding
evening.
TIVOLI FOLLIES, SYDNEY.
Eileen Watson and Alex. Yakovlenko in a
Pierrot Dance.
“Kick-in” seems to be playing a game of
theatrical baseball around Sydney. It
started at Her Majesty’s, moved on to the
Criterion, and Saturday will witness a dash
to the Royal. Its pitch at the Cri. will be
taken by Muriel Starr in “Nobody’s
Widow.”
At last! “The Waybacks” leave Sydney
Royal this week (last night Friday) and
move on towards Brisbane.
The Tivoli Follies (1015-10 vintage), with
lI.M. Vera Pearce in the lead and Arch-
duchess Thelma Raye close handy, came back
to the Sydney house last week-end. The
show they provide is light and bright, and
blows past like verbal thistledown. The
shortest turn lasts about 35 seconds, and is
practically an acted two-line joke; and there
are others that arrive and depart within two
minutes by the producer’s stop-watch. No
one performer has a mortgage or holds a sea-
son ticket. Each arrives, leaves his
or her sample of entertainment, and -'departs
smartly, carefully ignoring the clamor for
more—if there happens - to be any clamor.
There is plenty of good comedy, and the
performers are all young enough to enjoy
their featherweight jobs, dresses and stage
setting.

of testimonials from ex-sufferers of that
tired feeling.
“The Price of Thomas Scott,” played by
the Repertory Co. at Melbourne Athemeum
on Saturday night, is the tragedy of ,a small
man driven by wowserism to shut the finer
instincts out of _ his life. Thomas Scott
is a draper with a moribund business
and a couple of offspring whose feelings are
all against the harmonium recitals which the
old man regards as suitable pleasures for
the young. Leonard, the boy, has j.n idea
of acquiring the higher education in the
hope of beating his chances of becoming a
draper and drowsing thus to the end of his
days. Annie, the girl, wants to be a fashion-
able milliner. Knowing these things,
when T. Scott is offered £5OO for
his decayed residence by a Dance Hall
Syndicate, he has a generous impulse
to sell and satisfy his children’s de-
sires for a wider existence. But Scott’s
Nonconformist conscience gets to work over-
night, and he becomes appalled at the pro-
spect of a site long devoted to the service of
the Lord resounding to waltz music and the
giggles of cuddled girls. So he refuses the
offer and decides to hang on and be a semi-
bankrupt to the grave. Elizabeth Baker,
who furnished a more interesting drama in
“Chains,” celebrates Scott’s dull triumph
in three acts of plain and desultory dialogue.
Barring Scott, the characters are only just
vital enough to appear as details in a genre
picture with Thomas as the central figure.
Thomas’s morality, of course, is only a
bundle of “Don’ts,” but under Gregan
McMahon’s care he represents the artistic
mean between imbecility and heroism. Miss
Isabel Handley as Annie Scott is the only
one in the play who shows herself dutifully
conscious of the fact that her dad’s sacrifice
is akin to martyrdom. The other members
of the family and their friends are appar-
ently of the opinion that the old man’s
refusal of 500 golden goblins is merely a
bit of puritanical posturing. Those parti-
cular characters are played passably enough
by Mrs. Fanny Fowler, Miss Doris Fitton,
11. E. McMurtrie, Harry Lindgren and Ber-
nard Bradley; while Frank Keon is a red-
faced outsider who appears as a sort of
human pendulum, swinging, ethically speak-
ing, between the circus and the church.

AT POVERTY POINT.
“Peewit” : There has been much talk and
writing about an occurrence at the Little
Theatre (Sydney), and as to the meaning
and origin of the term “getting the bird” ;
and not one who has gaily pranced in with
his little lot has given the right definition.
The expression is very old. It was originally
“getting the goose.” I )oes not the poor old
acrobatic clown in Dickens’s “Hard Times”
say despairingly: “I was goosed last night”?
lie meant that he was hissed. Everybody
knows, or should know, that geese are star
hissers, and the phrase probably originated
in some unsuccessful performer alluding to
a hostile audience as “a mob of hissing
geese.” Gradually “goose” was changed
into “bird,” and lias stuck at that.
