AT HER MAJ-
ESTY’S MEL-
BOURNE.
Colonel Slaughter.
Died after a very short illness, Arthur
Francis Russell, the well-known Melbourne
dentist, and governing director of Hoyt’s
Picture Theatres, aged 51. Mr. Russell was
known to every “pro.” in Melbourne. For
years he ran side-shows in Melbourne and
took companies to country towns. Then,
reckoning that dentistry would flourish, he
went to the U.S.A. and returned with a
diploma which enabled him to achieve a very
big business. Next he scented big profits in
pictures and got in early there too, building
up an amusement business which must be
well up among the first half-dozen in Aus-
tralia.
“Oh Oh”: Seems to be something in
“11.0.’5” contention that the ’Murkan plays
we see here are mostly elaborated sketches
' —°ue good act and the rest padding. The
latest, “Kick-In,” now being done in Syd-
ney, when put on at Proctor’s, Noo Yark,
was a single-aeter with only four characters.
The. author, Williard Mack, played in it.
Finishing early in the evening he had plenty
of time to go and have a look at “Within
the Law.” One can suspect, after sitting
out his present elongated version, that he
took a notebook with him.
“Rab Cilbup” : At this time, when Billjim
is setting new standards of heroism for his-
tory, and the young Australian is proving
himself the finest fighter in the whole men-
agerie, the presentation of Young Australia
in “The Waybacks” amounts to a calamity.
Here is a family outback, the type of the
majority of our recruits, and the only char-
acters that are worthy of respect are Dad
and Mum. These are, despite their farcical
exaggerations, real, honest Australians. But
their family ! Not one of them is of any
use- outside an asylum. One girl is a gaping
fool; the eldest son is a thin weed whose in-
tellect is as weak as his body ; and the only
other son who attracts notice is obviously
“wanting.” Farce is all very well; but
farce that is provided by individuals who
ought to be in Callan Park is not only pain-
ful but a slight upon Australia. An Eng-
lishman wandering into the theatre and see-
ing an Australian audience shrieking with
delight over this representation of a typical
bush family would naturally wonder where
Australia gets its heroes from.
“W.A.R.”: What is the matter with the
Firm? Long ago it imported from the land
of Crook Melodrama a play that had a long
run, “Within the Law.” Since then these
people have been trying to discover another
play with similar possibilities, and naturally
they looked for it. in the same place. But
none of the long succession of TJ.S. dramas
has succeeded in staying anything like that
distance. At the present moment one of
the heads of the Firm is returning from the
neutral country of his birth with a suit-case
packed with this fleeting sort of play. Yet
if Mr. Ward had merely looked nearer his
adopted home lie would have found a play
that, following the example of “On Our
Selection,” looks like running for ever. Dur-
ing the stay of “The Waybacks” in Sydney,
the following imported plays died, or were
born and died Her Majesty’s: “Potash,”
“The Man who Stayed at Home” (revival),
“Paul Jones” (revival). Criterion: “Stop
Thief,” “A Pair of Sixes.” and the decease
of “Kick-in” is announced. Only one play,
“Potash,” had a decent run; but “The
Waybacks” is still coining money. I
am not saying that “The Waybacks”
is a great play; but what manager
cares whether a play is great as
long as the box-office receipts are big. and he
is saved the expense and trouble of putting
on something in its place? Evidently Aus-
tralian audiences like Australian play's, even
when they are as awful as “The Waybacks.”
Possibly, after the war, the Firm might dis-
cover this fact. If not, what about discov-
ering a new Firm?
“Mid-Channel”: Please allow me to add a
soft note to the concerto about high and low
pitch. There isn’t much doubt who should
throw the towel in, “E.N.M.” or “R.F.” The
artificial effect produced by the high pitch
has had a fair innings and been clean bowled.
There is no need to add a list of further
dissenters to that already furnished by
“R.F.,-” but the case of G. Grossmith .and
“I Have a Song to Sing O” will suffice.
The low pitch with which Australia is yet
unfortunately not acquainted has a-distinct
charm in itself, while the other is something
like a speaker who has been “tuned up” to
liis task with sundry nips. The vendor of"
the cheap piano grins behind his hand when
some innocent enthusiast asks for an instru-
ment “with a high pitch,” for he knows that
when the said pitch has climbed down the
instrument is scrap-iron. Vocalists are
trained to the low pitch because of the ex-
treme delicacy of their organs and the differ-
ence a semitone can make in the range of
their voices, and brass instruments must be
tuned accordingly to minimise their harsh-
ness.
“R.F.” starts to wind up the musical-pitch
debate P
A final word on my side, leaving- the opposition to
have the last one, if so inclined. “E.N.M.” reminds
me of the song, “If the Missus Says It’s Black—
Well, It’s Black!” Undeniable facts and incontro-
vertible arguments are alike of no avail against the
impregnability of his arrogant dogmatism. “I
heard, 1 maintain ; therefore it is so. and everybody
else is wrong.” In talking about “dead celebrities”
and “a legion of ghosts” he displays the same
sublime disregard of actualities, inasmuch as out
of the dozen persons I named nine are still alive;
and, anyhow, living or dead, the opinion of any one
of them would carry more weight than that of a
dozen “E.N.M.’s.” Now for “W.J.5.,” whose imper-
fect knowledge of the English language is plainly
responsible for his lofty assumption that I don’t
know what I’m talking about, and am guilty of
a grievous error. The word “virtually,” which I
used advisedly, means “in essence or effect, although
not in actual fact” ; and how many people would be
capable of perceiving any difference between the key
of C at the low pitch and the key of B half a tone
higher? Or between the notes C sharp and D
flat, virtually, though not actually, the same? Not
one in a thousand, “W.J.S.” included! I quite
agree with him that Sir 11. J. Wood, being a prac-
tical musician and not a scientific theorist, has pro-
bably never studied on the lines laid down by
“W.J.S.” The same shameful ignorance can also be
attributed to Puccini, Verdi, Gounod, Mozart, Handel
and sundry other benighted souls who unfortunately
did not have “W.J.S.” for a mentor.
“Van Eck”: King, of Brisbane, will need
a trickster to trump him. For a couple of
years, more or less, he has been proving that
stock drama is a profitable investment. In
fact, he is so sure of it that after having
possession of the Theatre Royal for the
aforesaid period he now takes over the
Brisbane Tivoli for a long season, and is
going to run drama against himself, and,
incidentally, shut out opposition from the
best-equipped theatre in Australia.
“C. Ockney”: In its latest Sydney pro-
duction, “Paul Jones,” as well as having a
woman “leading man,” boasts, I note, a
woman producer. Well, and why not?
Lovely woman can manage most things
when she sets about it, and stage-manage-
ment and producing should be well within
her compass. Lena Ashwell in London
—always associated with “advanced” ideas
—is running a new play at her theatre, the
Kingsway (“Iris Intervenes”), for which
she has a staff largely composed of mem-
bers of the sex to which I have the honor
not to belong. No man is employed as a
mummer or on the business side of the house
who is eligible for active service, also there
is a woman stage-manager, the limelights are
worked by women (oh, to be the handsome
juvenile gent.!), and women are employed
to do the lighter part of the scene-shifting.

Australian black-and-white artist Corporal
Cecil Hartt has relieved the anxiety of
friends as to the extent of the injury he
received on Gallipoli. Writing from Lon-
don he reports that, although he still has a
limp, he is improving so rapidly that he
expects to be back in the battle smoke very
soon. Meanwhile he finds the attentions
showered upon Australian soldiers in Eng-
land almost embarrassing.
The Women Workers’ Union in Sydney
lias started a promising little activity iii
conjunction with the war. holding a con-
tract for supplying the Defence Department
with shirts for soldiers. The purpose of
the Union is. to provide employment to
women who, either through age or disability,
would lie sweated on the ordinary factory
rates. Requiring no profit, the union on a
4oi hours’ week manages the business well
and has the good word of the Department,
particularly so from the fact that no article
has yet had to be rejected. Mrs. Kate
Dwyer is the godmother of the scheme.

f/• Pittman, permanent head of the
JN.S.W. Mines Department, goes out at the
end of the year. He has been an officer for
over 30 years, and is said to be retiring in
protest against some recent Ministerial
actions, which have been taken in direct
opposition to official advice. Pittman has
proved himself past-master in the art of
drafting non-committal reports. The only
thing that he ever wrote very definitely
about was the Milburn Creek copper mine,
which, for the purpose of adjusting compen-
sation, he valued at a big figure. Whether
or not. that figure was justified was not
determined, as the mine was neveiY again
worked as a producer. Although his name
was not in the slightest degree involved in
the political scandal the episode made him
wary—almost absurdly so.

Lord Jersey, who died last May, leaving
property valued at £790,000, apparently
died with bitterness in his soul against
the impertinence of a democracy which
dares to tax the property of its betters. He
left instructions to his heir to deduct 10 per
cent, from all legacies to employees, on
account of “the heavy duties which will fall
as a burden on my estate ; and I desire that
it shall be impressed upon each person that
the burden imposed by Government renders
this deduction necessary.” After which
vinegary thrust at the nation which had
been fat-headed enough to allow him to hold
so much that he never earned, the noble
earl haughtily climbed the golden stairs, and
claimed a martyr’s crown or half-crown, as
the case may be.
THE UNAPPRECIATED PRO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tlie situations are tremendous, the scenery is im-
mense, and as for the business it is magnificent ; but
one i/iisses the applause.— Extract from a letter from
an rotor well known to Melbourne and Sydney, now
at the Front.
’Tis well to wear a uniform,
And bear a bonzer gun,
To pose amid the battle storm,
And see the foenuin run :
But when one’s done his little bit,
And made a most decided hit
’Tis vain to pause
For the applause.
I stand upon a giant rock,
The smoke is in my hair,
And valiantly I make a mock
Of Germany’s despair.
I shake a bayonet in the breeze.
No more such things the high gods please.
I'm sad because
There’s no applause.
They say I play a noble part,
And 1 don’t doubt it’s that ;
’Tis one to touch the vulgar heart,
And I have lots of fat.
But vain is all the business, vain
My graceful posing ’mid the slain.
It' never draws
The least applause!
VICTORIA.

THE FALLING MAN.

HEBE: “Circus or aeroplane?”
An old ship with a record is now a coal-
hulk at Cockatoo Island l Sydney Harbor).
On her second baptism she was the Kal-
goorlie, but. in her palmy days she was the
Melrose Castle, trading between Britain and
Cape Town. Thirty-five years ago, while
nearing South Africa, she was the scene of
a historic shooting, which ended in a hang-
ing at Newgate. Town Councillor James
Carey, a building contractor, of Dublin, had
organised the conspiracy which ended in the
murder of Lord Frederick and
Mr. T. IJ. Burke in Phoenix Park. Under
an assumed name he took passage for the
Cape on board the Melrose Castle. The
"Irish Invincibles.” however, scented him
out, and Patrick O’Donnell was deputed “to
do the job.” Pat hanged for it. after trial
at the Old Bailey.
“The Old Soldier”: So the War Office has
definitely abandoned the issue of puttees
for wear in the trenches. I am not sur-
prised. I remember how they came into use.
We used to wear them as a protection
against leeches in the marshy districts ol
Lower Bengal and Assam. The original
puttee was a long, narrow strip of Bhotea
blanketing woven from the wool of the
Himalaya sheep. It was quite well known
to the liillmen (who invented them) that
puttees wound tightly round the leg and
kept on all day, interfered with the circula-
tion of the blood. That seems to have been
only just found out at the War Office. There
was another sort of puttee made of the same
sort of wool, but felted ; this was for wear
among the snow on the high passes during
winter. It was worn with a very thick felt
boot, which was by far the best thing _ (n.
the kind I have ever known for crossing
sncwfiekls. Even the best leather very soon
gets saturated when walking in deep
snow, while the felt retains (he heat and
prevents frost-bite. By the way, I doubt
very much whether thick wool-felt is not a
better protection against cold than sheep-
skin or fur, of which we are now making
winter vests.

After a special effort by the Victorian
police, through female agents, the hoof ol:
the Law has descended upon three Mel-
bourne fortune-tellers, all women. One ot
them, a well-patronised, rather expensive
practitioner, has a neat little suite, where
tlii- wallpaper, both in the ante-room and
the ante-up room, is up-to-date and grease-
less. Everybody knows this one. Another
of the soothsayers keeps a registry office in
the neighborhood of the Argus’s den. Both
of these have been in their present locations
for many years. The third, catering for a
different class, was discovered in Bourke-
street. Each of the prophecy-mongers was
fined a fiver, with five guineas costs; and, in
performing his sad duty. P.M. (loldsmilh .
mildly remarked that “in future the
penalties would be more severe.” That s
what the P.M. always says, before
proceeding to let the vulture down with a
fine like a feather. What’s a fiver to a
future-reader with a fashionable stand.' 1

“Legionary” : The remark of the \ ictorian
brigadier McCay, that “the Australian boys
seem to like it,” reminds me. After the tup-
penny-ha’penny S’African trouble, many of
the adventurous irregulars loathed the idea of
coming back to the led-
gers and counters they
had left behind them in
Australia. Hence, when
they reached the S’Afri-
can ports on their way
home, they eagerly
sought news of wars or
revolutions in other parts
of the world. At that
time the most promising
area of trouble was in
South America, and the
Consul for the troubled
State was soon besieged
by mobs of tall, war-bit-
ten strangers who wanted
to fight for his Govern-
ment if the wages were
high enough. When the
proud representative of
the Andes told them how
many dollars he could
offer, there was a flight
of broad-brimmed bats
skyward, and all was
congratulations and re-
jo ic i n g—u nt i 1 some
trooper with a turn for
finance harshly an-
nounced that the blanky
Andes dollar was worth
just about one-third of
the ordinary, respectable
dollar. That burst the
dream, and soon they
were all aboard their troopships, going home
like lambs to the jobs and the girls they’d
left behind them.
John Carey, master of the Union Co.’s
Mapourika. has convinced a Maoriland
nautical Court that the Almighty was to
blame when the ship struck Clay Point, near
the treacherous French Pass, where Pelorus
Jack used to live, on November 6. The
inquiry hinged on the possibility of light-
ning affecting the compass. At the time of
the grounding a severe electrical storm was
raging. The course set was one which should
have put the Mapourika clear of trouble, hut
the actual course was IT points off the
one which the skipper believed he was
following. This was attributed by an expert
to a deviation in the compass caused by the
vessel’s'unusually high iron masts collecting
large quantities of electricity from the atmo-
sphere. The Court adopted that view and
held that Carey’s compass had been rendered
temporarily a very useful thing for getting
into trouble with.

“Jobber,” from a hospital in Egypt:—•
It, is much the same here as it seems to be every-,
where else—nurses are expected to pig in anyhow.
At one Australian hospital the sisters had mentioned
to the matron that they had no place other than
their bedrooms (and, as far as I know, no sister has
a room to herself) in which they could entertain
visitors, or spend any time , before going on duty.
They were reminded that when they volunteered
they knew they must be prepared to “rough ’ it,
and a lot of other guyvcr. Matron was certain that
the sisters wouldn’t take one of the rooms for their
own use when there might be a rush of patients
and the poor soldiers would have to sleep in the
passages, and so on. That was- the end of the
matter as far as the sisters were concerned. But
a few days after this oration all the beds and
patients were moved out of one of the brightest
wards and bunged in elsewhere ill order that the
ward might be turned into a dining-room for
officers! Those same officers have a fine sitting-room,
and some of them have a bedroom each. 1 know of
other hospitals where each doctor has his own
room. I also know of a hospital where the sisters
have had to sleep in passages on account of lack of
space, while almost elaborate comforts were supplied
to male officials. I'm glad to say that most of us
feel uncomfortable about it. Our drawing-room
manners are nothing to speak of, maybe, but the
hardest Australian “hard case ” at any rate knows
what’s a fair thing where women are concerned.
“Bill O’Costs”: In these dull clays when
clients are shy I sit, on the hard benches of
the criminal court and study the faces of
those twelve Bulwarks of Popular Liberty,
the dozen good men and But sit with
me and observe them get to work. They
have listened to the evidence, to the Crown
Prosecutor and to the prisoner’s counsel and
are filled with the north wind. Now his
Honor is going to explain it all. This
type of Judge has a set formula and never
departs from it: —
Gentlemen, the prisoner is charged with larceny—-
that is, the taking and carrying away of the pro-
perty in question without the consent of the true
owner and with intent to deprive him of the pro-
perty therein or possession thereof. [Pause to let
this, soak in.] You will consider the evidence with
a view of satisfying yourselves whether it conforms
to this definition'. Now it is my duty to review that
evidence, and direct you for your guidance in your
determination. [“Now it's coming,” thinks the
earnest juror, all attention.] The evidence may be
considered in two divisions. Upon the one side you
have the evidence of the prosecution and on the
other the testimony for the defence. If. on the one
hand, you believe the Crown witnesses, you will have
no hesitation in finding the accused guilty, but if,
on the other hand, you credit the evidence of the
accused you will, with equal assurance, bring in a
verdict of not guilty. The prosecutor says the
prisoner stole his purse. The prisoner says he
found it. Which of these statements is the truth?
It. on the one hand ——
And so on the gentle see-saw rocks through
all the evidence, and the jury, being fat.
elderly and full of dinner, goes quickly to
sleep. That judicial method has, at least,
the merit of not being irritating. But listen
to another Judge. lie is SO or so and
brimming over with misdirected energy, but
diffusiveness and irrelevant parentheses dog
his footsteps. After an elaborate re-
arrangement of his seat and his wraps and
a. tussle with his notebook be finds the
starting post:- — ;
Ah —gentlemen of the jury, the prisoner is charged
with —eh —er —the indictment —yes, the indictment
filed —er —laid bv—ah'—the indictment of the Hon-
orable the Attorney-General [he is always particular
about titles] is one of—charges the prisoner with—
with [wild scrambling; with the note-book and
papers]"—-where is the indictment? [He has had it
in his hand all the time, hut that’s nothing*.] A
very distinguished Judge, the late Lord Hiatus, once
observed that an indictment —[yarn of no particulai
interest sandwiched in] —Eh? Oh, yes, here it is.
The indictment charges the prisoner—urn- urn—-
[laborious perusal of the document for arf a mol
—urn —ah —with receiving the same knowing it to
have been stol —Eh? What? Oh, yes, that s the
second count.
And so on and so forth. These luminously-
befogging “directions” to juries make me
think with admiration of a summing-up of
old Judge Boucaut. now retired. It is the
only one I ever beard worth reporting m
full :—-
Gentlemen, tlie c|iiestion is: Did he steal the watch?
Consider your verdict.

“FIOHTISU SOLDIKRS FIRST!"
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the transport Marquette was torpedoed, the
Maoriland nurses on board refused to take prece-
dence in the rescue boats, crying, “lake lighting
soldiers first!”
In unheroic days before
'('lie splendor and the shame of war,
With stoic calm ourselves we bore
When danger on us burst;
When some might live, but more must die,
The men stood resolutely by,
And came the good old British cry,
“Women and children first! ’
When death was close men played the game;
No thought of self, no panic-shame!
But steadily the order came
Instinctive, unrehearsed ;
When men the dreadful choice must make
Whom death should spare and whom should take,
They shouted, for their manhood s sake,
“Women and children first!”
The women and the children went
To rescue-boats, but ill-content —•
Many a woman’s heart was rent
That she was thus accursed ;
Her sex and weakness to her gave
The right to reach a living grave,
While son or husband died to save
Women and children first.
But now in war, in danger’s face
Ready is she to take her place,
The daughter of a fighting race,
No more in sex immersed.
When the Marquette sank she stood free,
Proud of her new-found chivalry,
And gave this cry to history,
“Take fighting soldiers first!”
x.s.w.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

Judge Murray, of Papua, has discovered
a village up yonder where the local nigger
follows the narrow path of righteousness
behind the missionary with much agility:—
There is no crime at Boianai ; no child ever
dreams of staying away from school. The houses are
well built, the people are clean, the village streets
and roads are kept scrupulously in order, no rub-
bish is seen lying about, everyone has plenty of
food, everyone seems contented, and everyone seems
to have work to do and to enjoy doing it.
The Bulletin knows another place:—
There is any amount of crime there, about a dozen
beaks sitting every day to keep the law in motion.
Thousands of kids play the wag and roll naked on
the sands by the sad sea waves. Most of the houses
were erected by Jerry Builder, Ltd. ; the people are
sprinkled from head to iieel with filth swept up by
the wind from the streets; rubbish blows about all
over the place; everyone howls about the high price
of food, is violently discontented, and enjoys work so
much that he is generally going’ out on strike. Also
the atmosphere is violently assailed every ten yards
by 17 assorted smells leaping from stekandeoyst
emporiums, pursued by 46 other aromas. The
traffic is under such good control that when a citizen
wants to cross a road he springs six times from under
the wheels of sudden death before reaching the oppo-
site kerbstone, where he slips on a banana skin. Fur-
ther, the citizen is hourly pursued by dishevelled
people for the loan of tuppence and has his ears bom-
barded by brass bands, barrel organs, conqertinas
and lone-hand cornets that have nearly learned “Tip-
perary.” When at night he wanders into an open
space to commune with the stars the first thing he
sees is a fiery exhortation to try a fresh sort of pill,
and when in anguish he looks the other way flaming
letters 10ft. high tell him that his king and country
want him. Whereupon he rushes into a picture-
palace and gazes at a Yank drama, falls into a pub
and consumes a liquid of foreign origin, kicks him-
self into bed, and doesn’t get any sleep until the
mosquitoes have finished supper.
Ana yet if it came to a choice between living
in that blameless village of Papua and loaf-
ing round in the other place—well. The
Bulletin reckons it would stay at home in
sin.

There are still a few excuses left. To
the lady’s usual unpleasant accusations in
the Melbourne Divorce Court the other day
the gentleman replied that he had put up
with a greyhound in the connubial bed for
six years; and then he got another lady to
help him in his rebellion. The Court ex-
pressed no adverse opinion about the rebel-
lion, but decided that it ought to have been a
single-handed affair, and granted a decree.
* * * ,-X-
-“J.R.”: “Chug” talks doubtful history
about the Huns (B. 11/11/T5). It’s a
question whether they came from Asia at
all. The Magyar language-'which it is sup-
posed they spoke is very like those the Fins
and Basques speak now. Attila, their king,
whom Kaiser Bill likes to call “Etgell,” was
really king of that part of the Hungarian
plain between the Dan-
ube and the Theiss, and
it was as likely as not
pressure from the Mon-
gols that set him and his
followers in motion
against Western Europe.
They must have been a
curious mixture of races,
largely made up of Mon-
gols and wild Slavs from
Pagan Poland and Pagan
Prussia. As for Gor-
mans, how many real
Germans are there in the
German Empire of the
present day? There are
none living east of the
Elbe, and if you come to
analyse the pedigrees of
the German princes you
will find that most of
them are Vendisli, which
means Sclavonic, just
like their people. The
reigning families of the
two Macklenburgs are
certainly so; and as for
the population, only
one Prussian province—
Westphalia - is really
German. The rest, Pom-
erania, Brandenburg, Sil-
esia, are pretty plainly
Sclavonic, while the two
Prussias and Posen are so thoroughly Polish
that the very school children have quite
lately gone on strike against getting their
lessons in German. The people talk Polish
in their homes, but the Prussian steam-roller
is being driven over them, even to the extent
of forcing them to sell their lands whether
they like it or not into German hands.
* * * *
A returned traveller says that the Austra-
lian War Contingent Association in England
gets eucalyptus leaves from Ireland, and puts
them under the pillows of wounded Kan-
garoos “who cannot hope to recover, so that
as they pass into another world they may
have at least the aroma of the homeland from
which they came, and to which they will not
return.” This paper gives the Association
(of which George Reid is president) due
credit for good intentions, but wishes it
would make a more sensible use of
the gum-leaves. When men who are not
hopeless are in such need of bucking up. ii.
seems plain foolishness to make Australia’s
most characteristic scent stand for a kind of
death warrant.
“Carfax” : That war-census card on which
the National Council of Women is taking
a census of the Ma State is evidently the
result of deep thought. For instance, among
all the questions asked the least space for
reply is left opposite to the query: “Have
you had any training or previous ex-
perience in any work?” For Mrs. Carfax
there is about Mn. to write; and what I
want to know is where she is going to put
this: —
Yes I have. I had a thorough college-training
in the theory of domestic economy and hygiene, and
quitted school with full knowledge of the rationale
of scrubbing, the flesh-forming value of certain pro-
teids and the warmth-producing values of sugar and
starch. Since then I have gone through a 27 years’
practical course in the following subjects—cleaning
windows, washing floors, cooking meals for seven
people out of enough food for three, hanging pic-
tures, rearing babies, ironing shirts, curing colds,
patching old clothes, turning last year’s winter cos-
tume into a summer blouse, feeding the fowls, keep-
ing an eye on the gas stove, dealing with Chows,
mending a husband’s socks, putting children to bed
(also getting them out of bed), engaging washer-
women, removing fruit stains from tablecloths, ex-
tracting splinters, renovating straw hats and enter-
taining relations. I can make jam, pickles, lemon
syrup, ox-tail soup out of chops, also slip-bodices.
1 can make a log of mutton last three days and a
pair of shoes six months and a currant cake as long
as I keep it locked up. In answer to the question
whether my time is fully occupied I beg to state
that it is only occupied from G a.m. until 10 p.m.,
and that during the remainder of the evening the
time hangs so heavily on my hands that with the
exception of having a bath and brushing my hair
and reading the paper and putting the cat out. and
placing the jug ready for the milkman and tucking
the kids in and making a man shift over a bit, I’ve
got absolutely nothing whatever to do. If I could
be of any use in driving a tramcar or in the fire
brigade please let me know any evening after 10.
P.S.: Any evening after 10 I won’t be in.
* * * *
Front somewhere in Gallipoli:—
The Colonel had scarcely put his whistle to his lips
to order the charge when the first of our lot sprang
over the parapet. He was a mere boy, and grinned
back at the others. “Last out, lousy!” he cried, and
swinging round, went laughing on. In 20 yards he
was down, drilled clean through. By God, it made
me weep, the sight of that kid playing in battle
with men as if he were romping at school with
children.
A picture for the National Art Gallery:
“Last out, lousy !” Of course, it won’t be
tackled ; but none will better express the way
of Australia.

Maoriland is getting fairly close to tlie
edge with its circus tricks for raising cash
to help the I [empire. It was on one of the
mail trains running down from Palmerston
to Wellington. Accompanied by an elderly
man who looked as if he knew better long-
ago, a flapper went from carriage to car-
riage. She was fair and fresh and plump,
with glad, diaphanous rags, and her escort
invited all and sundry to kiss the vision in
the sacred name of patriotism. The oscu-
lation was quite reciprocal, too, and the
dear girl had pulled in about £l2 when she
got to the last carriage. Then she gathered
up her petties and ran back through the
train and gave everyone a free show —of
riding pants and putties. Her name was
Bilk
That ancient abomination, the Hobart
Gaol, part of which dates hack beyond 1830,
is now the subject of inquiry by a Parlia-
mentary committee, which wants to find
out whether it would be better to tinker it
up, to reconstruct it, or to shift the pris-
oners into the country. Home of the evi-
dence ought to be a pretty effective warning
against crime. The drainage runs down
open gutters when it does not soak into the
floors, and what is left of it goes into an
open drain outside for tile benefit of
citizens generally. One witness deposed that
prisoners were kept for 20 or 22 hours at a
stretch in cells with 300 cubic feet of air
space, equal to 10 x G x 5, and that by the
end of that time ’‘the stink in the passages
was enough to knock a man down.” Appar-
ently he wasn’t imaginative enough to speak
of what it was like inside. The workshops
are saturated with filth and practically
without ventilation. Then the old thing is
so insecure that the more active guests
have to be put in chains to keep
them from climbing out into fresh
air, What is most staggering is that
according to the evidence everyone has
been agreed for about 25 years that con-
ditions were absolutely rotten, but nothing
worth mentioning has been done to improve
them.
* * * *
The Law brayed more loudly than usual
in Adelaide Insolvency Court the other day.
A bankrupt had been examined at great
L-ngth in chambers, and when the proceed-
ings came out into open court it was found
absolutely necessary to read aloud all evi-
dence previously taken. There were 700
type-written folios ; and Judge, two counsel
and the Official Receiver took turns at the
gabble. The farce lasted the whole of the
working day, and nobody was a bit the wiser
for it all, though a few people were a little
richer and a great many a trifle poorer.
A SLIGHT BALLAD RE THE UNDESIRABILITY
OF LOVE-LETTERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dearest, let not your raptures flow
In ink o’er any writing-pad,
Tear up the scribbled lines that show
What joys have made your bosom glad ;
One cannot always hide away
The reams where love rhapsodic lingers,
And I’d not have them fall a prey
To mocking eyes and prying fingers.
While our romance hath other scope,
Let not the bliss Dan Cupid doubles
Embark in any envelope
To sail upon life’s sea of troubles,
To strike, perchance, some alien coast,
The savage haunt of hoary sinners
Who’d make unseemly haste to roast
Our tender hearts to grace their dinners.
Ah, if the screed that throbs and burns
Kept love unfaded for all season!
But Time’s a nasty wretch who turns
On such the cold, white light of reason;
And in its glare the baby word,
The pet name sweet as garden honey,
To critic eyes will seem absurd,
Or, frightful thought!—extremely funny.
Old letters tied with lilac bands
You crave, to stir emotions gentle:
But sentiment like this demands
A reader duly sentimental.
And, sweetheart, though of placid mood,
’Twould irk my wandering spirit after
If strangers read our scrawls with rude
Remarks and loud, derisive laughter.
Nor does this sum the bitter end
Of lavish use of pen and pencil;
Since first Egyptian lovers penned
(In pre-impressionistic stencil)
Long yarns about “the tie that binds
Twin soul to soul with bonds immortal,”
Men have been prone to change their minds
And veer away from Hymen’s portal.
Who shall record his words profane,
His blind, unmitigated fury,
AVho hears his passion bleat again
To edify a judge and jury?
And many a maid, with vain regret
For foolish letters nigh demented,
tlas sorrowed long, with lashes wet,
That pens and ink were e’er invented.
And oft the phrases passing fine,
The verbal rockets that we utter,
Miss fire within the written line,
And, tongue-tied, Cupid seems to stutter.
So, darling, let the love we prize
Be ne'er to scented notes committed
The “sonnet shining in the eves’’
Is just as nice—and never writted.
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Luck? Fate? Sergeant Fred Fergus, a
N. S. Wales soldier, passed tlie tests of the
First Battalion in Egypt and the trenches
of Gallipoli, escaping from even Lonesome
Pine with but the loss of an eye. Then
reaching Melbourne from the base hospital
at Weymouth (Eng.) he sat down on the
sill of a lift-well in MeKillop-street to rest.
Two minutes later they took a mangled
corpse from underneath.
ARTIST FRED MeCUBBIN discourses on
atmosphere ( Vic. ).
Luke Murphy, who died lately at St.
Kilda (Vic.), aged 82, was one of the oldest
settlers in Gippsland. He arrived in Victoria
60 years ago, and went to Port xilbert, where
he met and married an Irish girl named
Margaret McCaffrey. The pair moved opt to
the place now called Sale, on the Thomson
River, and Luke Avent in for farming of all
sorts. He has left a remarkable family—-a
crowd of sons: three of them barristers, tw T o
of them surgeons, one a dentist, another a
grazier, the eighth a merchant. There
is just one daughter.
When the war commenced Dr. George
Cuscaden, principal medical officer of the
3rd Military District, Avas given the job of
sorting out the Victorian doctors who vol-
unteered for active service abroad and of
looking after medical arrangements gener-
ally in his State. Until lately the P.M.O.’s
billet has permitted private practice; but
owing to the piling-up of work th* Defence
Department recently advertised for ail
officer to give his Avhole time to the business
until the end of the war. There were
several applicants; but, as Colonel Cusca-
den, able and affable, had proved himself
one of the best salary-earners in the service,
he remained in his own shoes. _ Cuscaden is
a Collins-street surgeon of ancient repute, a
member of the Faculty of Medicine at Mel-
bourne University, and likes to have the
accent on the second syllable.
ADELA PANKHURST TALKS PEACE.
Died in Sydney a few days back, H. C.
Deliner, brother of The Bulletin’s old con-
tributor F. S. Delmer. Born in the Fly-
si eck 41 years ago, Deimer graduated at
Melbourne Uni, and for some years was a
schoolmaster at Geelong College and Sydney
Grammar School. About nine years back
he joined his brother, then professor of
English at Berlin University, and became
English lecturer at the German Military
Staff College, where he had as his pupils
many of the younger Hun officers who
exhibited their kultur in Belgium lately. Del-
mer resigned his position before the out-
break of war, and returned to Australia as
master of modern langiiag.es in the Stab'
High School, North Sydney. Kidney and
heart trouble closed the story. The elder
brother is at present interned as a prisoner
of war in Germany. Another brother is
master of a steamer on the Australian coast.
The Bulletin regrets that it won’t hear
anything for a while from its friend “Avis,”
the man who under that name or no name
at all has written so many good pars from
the Persian Gulf —including the delightful
story of the performance of Shakspeare with
variations at Busra. “Avis” —otherwise
Captain Thomas W. White (he never minded
anyone knowing it) —was reported missing
in Mesopotamia early in November; and the
Arabs “presume he is a prisoner of Avar.”
This paper knows no more enthusiastic
young soldier. Catching the complaint Avhen
a junior cadet, he joined the Garrison Artil-
lery at North Melbourne as soon as he could,
(nd got his crown and flags for signalling.
Then he moved to the Signalling Corps;
then in thirst of more experience he trans-
ferred to the infantry, and got his commis-
sion. Later, he \\ r as area-officer at Bendigo
and at Carlton, and filled in spare moments
learning to fly at Point Cook, being one of
the pioneer pupils. White left for the neigh-
borhood of the Garden of Eden with the
Australian Flying Corps about nine months
ago. His father —long in business as a lock-
smith in Little Collins-street, Melbourne,
died recently.
Of all the Duntroon military college boys
who went with the first four infantry bri-
gades only one is still in action: all the
others have been killed, wounded or in-
valided. The survivor is G. F. Wootten, a
captain at 21, and twice mentioned in dis-
patches.
Australia owes a special and personal
grudge to the Hun air-murderers now. In
a recent raid they caught two Kangaroos
as they came out of a London theatre
between the acts. The men in question were
Pte. Rogers, of Orange, and Pte. Rowell, of
Adelaide, who had both mended nicely after
their Gallipoli smasli-up and were out on
furlough. The bomb injured Rogers’s left
hand so badly that it had to be amputated,
and Rowell was so damaged that he had to
be returned to hospital.
Adelaide is recalling that the late Robert
Barr Smith, besides founding Elder, Smith
and Co., had a big hand in the start of the
Adelaide Steamship Co., the Bank of Adel-
aide, and both Moonta and Wallaroo copper
cos., and did a lot towards building up the
pastoral industry in every mainland State
except Westralia. Among his more striking
benefactions were £9OOO to the Adelaide Uni-
versity library (and £lO,OOO is on offer for a
University residential college), £lO,OOO to
build the" spires of the Anglican; Cathedral,
and £2OOO towards the new bishopric of Wil-
lochra; £2300 to clear the Trades Hall from
debt; a steam lifeboat for Government ser-
vice on the south-east coast; Clement
Wragge’s observatory on the summit of
Kosciusko; and minor gifts innumerable and
generally unknown to the public. Mr. Barr
Smith started the cremation movement in
South Australia and was a steady sup-
porter of it; so the great crowd that
gathered round the family vault at Mitcham
saw only the urn containing his ashes, car-
ried by his only surviving son.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” reads the ser-
vice :
John Macpherson has gone over, at 87, the result
of a fall out of a Sydney tram-car. John was Scotch,
and years ago was book-keeper to J. 1!. Holdsworth,
wholesale ironmonger, when he had his store at the
corner of Market and George streets. Old Tom
Puck land was his neighbor. When J. 11. Ilolds-
worth died, Edward, his son, succeeded to the busi-
ness, and took into partnership his uncle Henry
Holdsworth and John Macpherson, the firm being
then Holdsworth, Macpherson and Co. Mr. Mac-
pherson and his family, have been connected with
it ever .since. In a manner of speaking, the
Waverley Cemetery was part of the family, for John
was mayor of the borough when' the municipal coun-
cil projected it. The burial-place was designed as
a source of revenue for the municipality; but the
scheme went agley, and the Government insisted on
all the income being expended within the grounds.
In recent years the old man had a seat, with fees,
upon the Water and .Sewerage Hoard as representa-
tive of the Government. Probably he hadn’t made
a fortune. Comparatively few men do in his line.
It" isn't like the fluff trade where there are no
standard prices. The ironmonger can’t-charge 2000
per cent, on cost price for a pot on the ground that
the pot has a handle just’a sensation different from
all other pots.
LAUGHTER IN GERMANY.
The flag-waving and other patriotic demonstrations
which invariably take place all over the country
when the German staff reports a new “victory” are
by no means spontaneous on the part of the public.
It is, in fact, a standing order in most German
cities that on such occasions every householder must
display bunting.— Cable.
His Nibs : “Smile, you ——, smile.”
Maorilaud heroically resisted the tempta-
tion to import something the other day when
it handed Percy Muter the job of actuary
to the State Life Insurance Department in
succession to the late Morris Fox. Muter
was born at Invercargill in 1868. and joined
the office as a cadet at Christchurch. He
began to climb steadily with his eye on the
job he now holds, and in four successive
years passed the four stiff tests necessary to
qualify for fellowship of the English Insti-
tute of Actuaries. He was the first to get
the F.I.A. degree by exam, in Australasia.
The first man to take a horse and cart to
the top of Kosciusko passed out recently ar
Sydney. E. 11. Taylor, who did it, came to
Australia as a lad in 1850 from his native
Worcestershire, and for some time snatched
type in the S. M. Herald office. After mining
at Araluen (N.S.W.) and elsewhere he
joined the N.S.W. Trigonometrical Survey
in 1880, and had a hand in the triangulation
LOW, whose hook of caricatures has just
been published, on the way to his favorite
w ickedness —afternoon-tea.
of practically the whole State. His marks
still stand on the summits of the best-known
hills on the Blue Mountains, and the afore-
mentioned trip up Kosciusko was made Tor
the purpose of placing a cairn on the highest
point of 'this continent.

G. S. Kingsell, who used to he in the
N. S. Wales Justice Department and after-
wards went to Fiji and wrote some good
stuff from there, recently left London for
France as a lieutenant in the Army Service
Corps. He will work with Indian troops, hav-
ing acquired some of Brown Brother’s tongue
while growing coolie sugar. Kingsell reached
England on October 6, and the things he
had learned in Fiji gained him a commission
inside a fortnight.
A THOUSAND FRIESOS AND NONE.
[F OR TH E BUL LETIN.]
I saw him march in the line to-day,
And never a girl had he;
And he sailed to the Great Grim AVar away,
The clown of his company.
They’d crowd his tent in the camp at night
AVhen the long day’s drill was done:
For the ready .jest on his lips was light
Who’d a thousand friends and none,
I heard him laugh in a bar last week,
A laugh that the footlights know
AVhon the limelight glares on the painted cheek
Through the tinsel sham and show.
I saw him stand by a grave last year—
lie thought that be stood alone—
And the under world through his eyes looked sheer
From a face like a face of stone.
I saw him smile as he passed me by—
For he saw me and went his way—
And how should ho wear for such as I
The smile that he wears to-day ?
For my face was “plain” for a brave man’s bride—
He left me as brave men dare—
And the other girl lived and the other girl died
Because her face was fair.
T saw his face when the moonlight shone
One night by the wind-tossed wood,
And once ’neath the stars when the moon was gone,
And I thought that the lines were good.
And once when the skies were weeping sad
To his sick-room door I crept,
And the lines were good, and the lines were bad,
In tiie lamplight while he slept.
I saw him march through the streets to-day
And on through the wharf’s wide gates
(There’s a face for the woman that loves, they say.
And a face for the woman that hates),
And the memory of the dead past stood
In the living present’s stead:
And I wonder now will the lines be good
On his face when he is dead.
I saw him march in the line to-day,
And never a girl had lie;
I saw him sail to the war away,
The clown of his company,
The saddest man where many are sad,
With the good-bye cheerings done.
In the Legion Lost of the Good-and-Bad
AA'ith a thousand friends and one.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

There isn’t much to spare (here. Still, if
the management has decent inner reserves, it
does tip' right thing in keeping up the old
rate. Westralia lias been having such a
wretched time that when a concern can pay
the old dividend, it would be pretty cruel to
button up its pocket. Fortunately the com-
ing harvest promises very well, and if wheat
isn’t going to bring a phenomenal price, it
will at least be profitable.
Shareholders who have been getting _ a
20 p.c. div. would certainly not be actually
drowned in sympathy if 2 p.c. w r as cut off.
But though there are a few people who may
he getting 20 p.c. out of this investment,
there are three times as many who are
getting only about 7 p.c. In 1890, when
present general-manager Holmes left the
family’s pet donkey behind in S. Australia
and went across to take charge, the paid
capital was £60.000 and the formal reserve
fund £64.620. To-day the paid capital is
£250,000 and the formal reserve fund
£680,000; and the bulk of that large addi-
tional reserve has been provided by pre-
miums paid on new capital issues. The
20 p.c. is therefore an entirely deceptive
figure.
No changes of serious consequence have
occurred during the year : —•

The policy appears to have been to merely
stick to old customers; and that was right
when resources had to be conserved. Cur-
rent-account money has naturally been
drawn on; just as naturally fixed deposits
have increased a trifle, because so many
people reckon that the safest thing to do
with money just now is to let somebody else
take the risk of investing it. Practically all
the money this bank has thus accepted is in
hand; liquid assets represent just over 40
p.c. of the liabilities. The depositor is even
better protected by the large size of the
capital and reserves in proportion to lia-
bilities. Put it this way: the liquid
assets are £1,061,030, and the bank owes
£2,618,399; so it owes £1,557,369 w’hich is
not represented by liquid assets. And to pay
that £1,557,369 it has £2,456,956 of ad-
vances and £79,500 of premises. A West-
ralian security demands rather harder look-
ing at than most, 'partly because mining
districts are uncertain cattle, and partly
because agriculture isn’t a proved success
iu many parts of Westralia where it is
being tried by people who are borrowing to
make the experiment. But giving all that
in, this bank’s depositors are protected by a
quite unusual margin.
Henry Tones Co-operative. Ltd., which

turing and fruit cos. and timber mills” :

The shareholder opens his mouth and shuts
his eyes and trusts the directors with the
spoon. What’s in it he doesn’t know;
what’s behind it he doesn’t know.. It isn’t
intended that he should know. This co.
doesn’t make jam. It merely owns all. or
virtually all. the shares in numerous cos.
which do make jam. The shareholder who
really owns the jam has thus no right to
push his finger into it.
The co. was a perfectly legitimate—in
fact, wise—attempt to cut out a lot of
unnecessary expense, and thus save waste.
Of course, there was a hit of a row about
it, and a noise has been made now and then
since. But this is certain: the basis of the
thing is right. Neither the producer of the
raw materials nor the consumer of the fin-
ished goods can be benefited by waste. If
there are half a dozen buyers’ agents chasing
for fruit which one could comfortably
handle, and half a dozen travellers trying to
sell jam which one could comfortably sell,
somebody has to pay the wages and the
exes, of the superfluous 10—either the grow-
ers or the eaters. And there were other
things. N. 8- Wales was flooded, say. with
apricots while apricots couldn't be had in
Tasmania; and vice versa as to, say, rasp-
berries. Instead of the N.S.W. jam co.
carting up raspberries from Tasmania to be
presently perhaps carted back to Tasmania
and offered as jam. it is now possible for the
fruit to be used lip where it; is freshest, and
then distributed, with the result that not
only is money saved, but a more wholesome
article is produced. The public ought to
benefit. Whether it does benefit depends
upon the management and the respect it
lias for the country’s anti-trust law’s. So
far, there is no doubt this co. has had a
very healthy respect for those law’s. lii
fact, Henry Jones, the chairman, is almost
“nervy” on the subject.
This co., as before remarked, isn't a jam
co. : it is an investment co. But that
sort of evasion doesn’t count for much. If
the co. were really suspected of using its
powers to unduly sweat either producer or
consumer, such little subterfuges w’ouldn’t
avail for very long. Meantime the arrange-
ment has this result: it prevents the public
(including competitors and shareholders
alike) knowing anything worth knowing
about the concern. The investments are in
“at cost” ; and the assets consist wholly of
“shares and interests” in these cos. and
amounts owing, by them —dividends and ad-
vances. On the surface, no goodwill is dis-
closed. But this co. may have paid £50,000
for 50,000 £1 shares in a subsidiary co.
which lias only £25.000 worth of assets. As
a matter of fact, in the first £400,000 of
capital issued £91,500 w’as goodwill. The
capital is now £605,000. Goodwill may have
been increased by subsequent purchases;
or it may have been reduced out of profits:
or large reserves may have been ..accumulated
by subsidiary cos. It is, by the way, signi-
ficant that the directors say: “The profits
of the South African business havfe fully
confirmed anticipations, and enable a contin-
uance of the policy of building lip a sub-
stantial reserve in that country.” It may
be handy some day to have a business in
S’Africa. You can almost smell it coming:
“In consequence of the profits from the
S’African business, the directors are able to
distribute a bonus of” so-and-so. Two
conclusions, then, seem inevitable: (1) As
to capital, nothing of any value regarding
the assets can be gathered from the balance-
sheet. (2) As to the profits, they are no
doubt very substantial; but the-management
will strike trouble if it attempts to pay any-
thing but moderate dividends, though much
may be done, no doubt, by timely bonuses.

That last line is very good, considering (1)
the war, which so disturbed the market
early in the season that large quantities of
wool usually sold in Sydney were sent to
London, to be on hand in case of a spurt;
(2) the disturbance of the frozen-meat
trade, through the commandeering of all
available mutton; (3) the drought, which
has affected the offerings in both wool and
stock markets, and is going to affect them
for some time to come. Fortunately from
the co.’s point of view’, fat-stock sale com-
missions have been very juicy on account of
the phenomenal prices, so the. profit for the
year has been fairly well maintained. There
w’as actually less of a fall than in the year
before; and the recent shrinkage, seeing
that we. know what il is due to, is less dis-
turbing than the persistent and distinctly
unpleasant fall in profits which bad occurred
previously.
The most comfortable feature of the whole
business has been the wide margin of profit
in excess of the dividends. In 10 years
the shareholders have collected £89,000 ; and,
besides £13,000 that has been written off,
£70,187 has been put by. Thus very nearly
as much has been left in the business to
strengthen it as has been taken out. What
has been done with the undivided profit the
next table shows :

Compare the position with that of In years
ago, and you will see that, while advances,
etc., are only about £9OOO less, and though
over £20,000 has been put into store and other
premises, no less than £74,000 of debt has
been paid off. And while that by itself w’ould
he a very excellent performance, no doubt
something still more has been done; for it
is pretty certain that some of the advances,
etc., in 1900 have been written down. In
other words, they have been realised at a
loss, and the loss has been made good out of
current profits. This paper isn’t going to
swear that all the £164,131 at present on the
books would fetch 20s. in the £ if the securi-
ties had to be quickly realised. Apart from
the war. the drought in the North and Nor-
W’est and in Queensland is a terribly serious
business, from w’liich the losses are going to
be enormous; and it is quite possible that
some of the £164.131 is represented by dry
bones bleaching alongside a drier waterhole.
But no depreciation that is at all conceiv-
able could wipe out the reserves, and reach
the capital. And all of that capital is repre-
sented by tangible things: none is repre-
sented by goodwill, though nobody would be
foolish enough to say that the co.’s business
isn't w T orth a lot of money. For tw’o or
three or even more years profits may be
depressed; beyond that the future looks
cheerful enough, always provided that there
is a reasonable explanation of the fall in
profits from 1910 onwards.

The figures for the year are a shade better,
hut there is not the improvement you might
assume from the fact that the deficit for the
year was only £1436 as against £4584 in
the preceding year. In this matter the actu-
aries, who were asked to make a sort of a
report without making an actuarial examina-
tion, don’t put things quite as explicitly as
they might. They say :
The total cost of management has fallen not only
in actmil amount blit ulso in proportion to income.
The amount of shareholders’ capital spent in excess
of all the insurance revenue during the past three
years has been: 1913. £5200; 1914, £4584; 1915,
£1430. The movement is in the right direction and
rapid.
Now'', that whole £1436 might have been
wiped out (his year and still the movement
might not have been in the right direction
at all. Before you know whether the £1436
is a bhiter figure than the £4584 you must
know the amount of new business secured in
those tw r o years respectively. As a matter
of fact, while new-business (Ordinary) pre-
miums in .1914 were £4454, in the past year
they were only £2878. Further, so- many
lapses have occurred during the year that,
according to the. actuaries, “the amount at
risk is slightly less now than one year ago."
Further, though capital has been drawn
upon to the extent of only £1436 during the
year, it must be remembered that liabilities
have been accumulating on the policy con-
tracts. These liabilities have never yet been
valued, hut will be at the end of the current
year. This paper has. no basis on which to
estimate the amount that should have been
accumulated to meet the claims and matur-
ities. Instead of a total deficit of £26,508,
the co. has probably thus far gone to lee-
ward to the extent of at least £40,000. That
would mean that it ought to have £14,000 in
hand towards meeting -its obligations. For-
tunately, owing to its considerable share
capital, it has that much and a good deal
more. Loans on mortgage and cash repre-
sent over £28,000, This paper repeals that
it has ho idea what sum the co. ought to
have accumulated; but the amount can
hardly yet be £28,000. So far, therefore, the
policy-holders are quite safe. Whether they
will be safe later on. depends upon future
results. And whether shareholders will lose
their money or get fat depends upon the
same thing. They have put up £56,894, of
which probably £40.000 or more has gone.
That may be recovered, and big profits made
by-and-bye—we do not know’. We can only
say more or less positively how things stand
at present.
These young life societies put the post-
mortem-ist in an awkward corner. It seems
like baby-murder to kill them: it goes
against the grain—of this paper, anyway —
to speak kindly only of the corpulent old
affair, and not give a youngster which may
by-and-bye turn out better than any one of
them a chance. But in a matter of life in-
surance how can a writer take the risk of
recommending anybody to have anything to
do with a concern which hasn’t proved itself,
which has all its fighting to do, which may
possibly go hung, which certainly won’t be
able to work cheaply and pay good bonuses
for many years? A man may unquestion-
ably be a public benefactor by taking out a
policy in one of these new concerns, seeing
that he may thus be helping to establish
something better than the present best. But
if he looks at the matter from the purely
selfish point of view, the struggling infant
must have a cold time.

These M.L. woollen mills have had the
time of their lives. One of them explained
the other day that they really were not
charging more for their wares, but were
now’ able to keep going hard and make the
increased profit by reason of greatly-in-
creased turnover. Apart from military re-
quirements. which had been heavy, it was
explained that the disturbance of shipping
had given the local product a better chance
than it ever had before in the local
market. This co. is silent about the causes
of the extraordinary profit, and merely ven-
tures the opinion that “the directors believe
shareholders will consider the results satis-
factory.” Well, even the Mosgiel Co. can
do with a thick slice of good luck. In the
past 13 years the dividends have averaged
only ?. p.c. • p.a., and the reserves are not
£lOOO more now’ than they were then. A
fair amount of profit has of course been
written off; but depreciation of woollen-
mill plant is rapid, not merely that the
machinery wears out, but it lias to be
s.‘rapped or a more modern competitor gets
the business. Still the eo. has done what
some others haven't been able to do —it has
at least maintained its mill, and apparently
increased its business a bit, without going
into debt. Twelve years ago it owed
£24,281. To-day it owes only £12,346, and
has approximately the same value in assets
apart from the mill plant. The co. seems
thus to have been rather more than holding
its own after distributing the dividend
before mentioned: and, though profits have
been uneven, it has plainly been, on the
average, doing much better during the last
half-dozen years than in the preceding six.

The war-price of copper was responsible
for the half-year’s remarkable profit. Six
months ago there were 1294 tons on hand.
This, taken into account at £6O per ton,
actually averaged £BO Bs. Id.; so over
£34,000 of the profit credited to the past
half-year came from the surplus realised
on the previous term’s output. Of the past
six-months’ production 2072 tons were sold
at £B7 13s. 10d., and that meant another
£40,000 more than an average market would
have yielded. The current half-year should
show equally good results, because 1937
tons remained on hand, and have been taken
into account at the usual £6O, whereas elec-
trolytic copper is now up to about £95.
If tilings go smoothly at the mine, the cur-
rent half-year, helped by the surplus from
last half-year’s copper, should show profit
in the neighborhood of £200,000, or 3s. per
share.
Meantime Ihe mines are developing splen-
didly, and the prospects for the co.’s side-
line, superphosphates, are excellent owing
to the bountiful wheat harvest which will
be reaped in four States. Last season,
following upon the general drought, thou-
sands of farmers sowed without fertilisers.
Next Season they will feel themselves in a
position to use them. There no doubt is a
good profit in this business; hut the co. has
never shown it separately. As for ore develop-
ments, no estimate of reserves has been made
for the present report, and the former half-
year’s figures are repeated, with the recent
North Lyell extractions deducted: —Mt.
Lyell, 1,791,479 tons; North Lyell,
973,210 tons. During the past 12 years,
during which 2,900,016 tons have been
treated from Lyell and 1.366,989 tons from
the North, 325. 9d. has been paid in divi-
dends ; and on Sept. 30 there were assets
valued at £409,031 in excess of liabilities,
equal to (say) 6s. 6d. per share. Lyell
ore is of lower metal values than the aver-
age hitherto treated ; but North ore contents
have varied hardly at all. Costs are more
than they were ; hut the increase has been
checked by the Mt. Margaret electric-power
scheme, which is giving great satisfaction.
The dividends paid have averaged nearly
10s. per ton; the profits for the past 12
months have exceeded 12s. At 10s. per ton
the reserves would be worth 21s. 6d. pet-
share ; and to that must be added the value
of the ore proved in the bottom level of the
North during the past half-year and the
liquid assets. The market values the
shares at about 235. 9d.; and it has often
put a higher price upon smaller reserves and
poorer prospects.

Block 10 didn’t have enough ore left to go
into the co. which was formed to take over
the B. 11. Prop.’s smelters; and as that
plant can’t cope with the output even of the
four interested cos., of course Block 10
hasn’t been able to get its ore iu there. Nor,
in spite of all the talk about the development
of new markets and so on, has it been able to
find a buyer for either its lead concentrates
or its zincy tailings. Hence mine and mill
have been shut down all the half-year, and
the loss show’ll above is made up as follows:
Repairs and maintenance, £4329; manage-
ment. £1216; depreciation. £4083; income
tax. £10S0; less interest and sundries, £Bl6.
This loss is exclusive of any that may (or
may not) be made out of the Misima
(Papuan) option which general-manager
Ward is now testing. (He, by the w’ay,
fell down one of the workings the other day
and damaged himself considerably.). So far
£4853 spent on options is taken in as an
asset. The new property looks more promis-
ing than it did at the start, and two or three
months should decide whether it is worth
the money the option syndicate wants for it.

Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will bo as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.

Meantime, though the co. has pretty well got
to the bottom of the mine in Broken Hill,
there is still a comfortable surplus of £59,824
in the office, chiefly fixed deposits.
The directors of Arthur Rickard and Co.
Extended. Ltd. (Sydney), are finding out a
few things about published balance-sheets.
For many years The Bulletin scoffed every
six months or so at the accounts of the Hay-
market Land, Building and Investment Co.,
whose 40s. shares were mostly quoted at a
trifle over a pound. The Rickard Co. about
a year ago bought the other co., and, in
spite of warnings, “relied on the balance-
sheets and on the certificates of that co.’s
auditors that such balance-sheets were true
statements of the affairs and position of the
co.'' Now, long after the completion of the
purchase, a special audit “discloses the fact
that for a number of years past the Hay-
market Co. has been carrying on its books
secured balances which should long since
have been written off and in respect of which
it holds no security. Such writing-down will
entail a loss of £20,074” —which of course
the Rickard Co. will have to bear. As a
matter of fact, a published balance-sheet
doesn’t as a rule set out the true position of
a co., and rarely pretends to. All that audi-
tors usually certify lo is that tlje summary
is in accordance with the books*; and from
the books nobody can tell whether a property
alleged there to be worth £lOOO is really
worth that or something else. The pub-
lished explanation is therefore a poor excuse
for what looks like a bad bargain; and there
ought to be a strict inquiry into the whole
transaction.

( F>. H. Block 14 is the president of the
Gleaners’ Union. Ten years ago the mine
was so nearly worked out that it wasn’t
worth giving it a sulphide plant, yet the co.
has been paying dividends ever since, though
it has wasted a lot of money on other shows.
Last half-year, though it wasn’t able to sell
any of its small reserves of sulphides, it
made £4358 net. That wasn't enough to pay
the usual sixpenny dividend and the interest
on pref. shares (they carry 10 p.c. p.a. and
come in for dividends along with the others ) :
but there is still over £42,000 in the bank to
draw on for help, and other more or less
liquid assets worth £15,000 in excess of lia-
bilities. The co. has all these years been
poking around (lie upper levels looking for
little lots of carbonate ore—which the smelt-
ers snap up—and finding enough to keep the
pot boiling, though it lives from hand to
mouth. But it is steadily costing more money
to find the good stuff; last half-year total
exes, were £2 Is. Id. per ton as against
£1 17s. 2d. the previous half, notwithstand-
ing that “exploration work was kept as light
as possible.” For years the co. hunted around
for another mine; but it has given up the
search. There is £30,000 pref. capital, be-
sides 100,000 ordinary shares.
The consistent Yerranderie (N.S.W.)
silver field is to have another producing
mine. The Peaks, after years of prospect-
ing, has located the main lode at a depth
of over 700 ft. on the underlie. The solid
mineral is 28in. wide (a big lode for Yerran-
derie), and assays over 200 oz. of silver and
11 P to 40 p.c. lead. The great drawback of
the field is the high cost of cartage. The
fertile Burragorang Valley in itself ought
to justify the construction of a light rail-
way from Picton, and connection with the
mines would not be a costly undertaking
and would probably lead to the establish-
ment of a central smelting works on the
field. Coal is abundant in the locality, and
unlimited supplies of limestone and iron-
stone for flux could be obtained from the
Mittagong district.

Insufficient votes were recorded at the
B. 11. Junction North’s meeting to give the
directors increased borrowing powers, so a
9d. call has been made. But the market is
holding up fairly well, because there are at
last sensible negotiations to sell the lease.

Mount Baring, in the Oberon district
(N.S.W.), has again closed down, except for
a couple of men underground. This show,
which is an exceedingly patchy one, occurs at
the junction of the clay slate and felsite
country; but its nature is such that if it did
not occur at all the mineral wealth of N. S.
Wales would not be materially affected.
Its auriferous patches being no good, and its
more numerous other patches considerably
worse, while its gold is largely silver, its
proprietors invariably end up with much
more knowledge and a good deal less cash.
Enough money has been spent there to make
a mine of it if Nature had not been so
nearly out of gold when she constructed it.
It is, however, a constant starter.
lodide (N.S.W.) presents a more hopeful
prospect in the shallow ground. The rich
gold-silver vein discovered by tributors in
No. 1 level is still holding out, and what is
considered an important development is that,
instead of lying on a horizontal floor x it is
now pitching at 45deg., indicating a chance
of its living down. A shoot of gold-bearing
ore has been located at the 90ft. level. Over
a width of 30in. this assays load 13 p.c.,
silver 300 z., and tgold 3oz. 18dwt.
Raker’s Creek, the S.A.-owned Hillgrove
property which still holds the record as the
largest dividend-producer among N.S.W.
gold-mines, exists now practically as only a
golden memory. The mine is being worked
in a desultory way by tributors and by the
company in old levels. No further deep ex-
ploration seems to be contemplated. During
the past half-year the company crushed 43
tons for 2900 z. gold, equal to (ioz. 15dwt. per
ton, and tributors got 7780 z. from 180 tons,
or 4oz. Bdwt. per ton. The operations showed
a loss to the co.
Great Fingall (W.A.) has loaned portion
of its closed-down plant for the manufacture
of munitions, and intends to devote the next
six months or so to deep development and
adding to ore reserves. So far, about 45,000
tons have been extracted from what is
known as the new shoot below No. 14 level,
and returns have aggregated over £70,000.
Long-distance trucking and double haulage
have, however, proved so expensive that in-
stead of making a profit the co. has shown
a loss of £7OOO. Between the Nos. 18 and
19 levels prospects are fairly good, and in
some sections first-rate; and by the time
the mill gets going again the management
hopes with the aid of increased reserves and
improved working conditions to. once more
make a profit.
Fremantle Trading Co., formerly Fre-
mantle Smelters, Ltd., is about to resume
operations on its Northampton lead mines.
It also proposes, with Government backing,
to purchase ore from surrounding leasehold-
ers—an arrangement which will get over
that difficulty of finding a ready market
which has hampered local lead-production
since the war began. The co. has not yet
reached the div.-paying stage, but still has
hopes. An inflated metal market will of
course help.
* . •* * * •
“Bullant” Mclntyre's great gold hunt
(W.A.) has turned out poorly, and
Christmas will probably see the end of it.
The idea was all right, but the trouble has
been to get the men comprising the expedi-
tion to work systematically. Ilad each
couple of prospectors been, sent out to test
the wilderness in their own little: tinpot Way,
perhaps the result would have beeti different,
but in its present form the scheme is un-
workable. Meantime the promoter sits on a
black stump outside of his humpy and
dreams, of what may still be.
Mount Morgan’s blister is becoming more
gilt-edged than ever. The latest return
shows G2l tons copper and 10,2590 z. gold.
So a ton of copper contains £7O worth of
gold. But it isn’t anything to rejoice over,
for it means that the copper contents of the
ore have fallen while the gold contents have
been maintained. The last return shows
18,G5G tons smelted and 14,046 tons milled :
total, 32,702 tons. That means well under
2 p.c. of copper.
The Queensland Government is giving
attention to the Great Fitzroy, near Rock-
hampton. The mine is low-grade, averaging
less than 24 p.c. copper, but with the metal
at £BO that means 40s. per ton. Add 10s.
per ton for precious metals and the total
value is 50s. But deducting concentrating
and smelting losses, the actual yield is back
to 40s. again; and costs must be screwed
down if the mine is to pay. Fitzroy is, how-
ever, capable of producing from 150 to 200
tons of blister copper per month; and the
State can’t now afford to let £15,000 a
month go to waste if ends can possibly be
made to meet.
* ' -X- * *
Three of the Power family, helped by
Percy Pigott and Frank Powell, run the
Mount Cuthbert, Cloncurry. Powell, sen.,
is on the mine as general manager, and
W. H. Corbould is consulting engineer. The
co v having got its railway, has begun to
smelt at Mount Elliott’s Selwyn plant.; but
want of timber for underground use has
forced the co. so far to rely on dump
material for the smelter. It is said that
this isn’t averaging much over 5 p.c., partly
on account of leaching. Directly levels are
fixed up the grade of ore shipped should
improve, and then things will move pro-
perly. The co. on August 31 had a cash
balance of £14,50G and sundry stores £6972.
It owed £59,G00 on debentures. Now the co.
has to face the cost of its own smelting plant
as designed by W. H. Corbould. This will
result in a long bill. Hence the need to ship
as much rich stuff as possible to Selwyn.
Not satisfied with already holding a dozen
separate properties, the co. has taken an
option over the Orphan lease, east of Mount
Elliott’s Dobbyn claim.
The second big wash-up is in hand at
Cock’s Pioneer, Eldorado (Vic.). Starting
in mid-July and finishing on November 20, a
paddock of 240,000 cub. yds. of drift was
This is a higher plant efficiency
than was counted on, and the yield looks
like a tonic for Arthur Moline, who has
been bullocking so hard at his job that, he
needs one. Roughly 240,000 cub. yds. gave
up 23250 z. gold and 38 tons tin oxide, and
there are four tons of gold-bearing concen-
trates to be treated. Already the mathe-
maticians are figuring out costs and profits.
Gold at £4 per ounce gives £9300, and tin
oxide at the safe figure of £75 per ton
means £2850. The total value of products
recovered to date is therefore £12,150, or a
shade over Is. per cub. yard. With the
four tons of concentrates thrown in, the
dirt will go just a shade over Is., against
10kl. for 385,000 cub. yds. for the four
previous clean-ups. As there has been less
timber to worry over in the bottom, costs
should not be above 7d. Supposing the
profit to be sd. per cub. yd., that works out
at £5OOO netted for a 34 months’ campaign.
A year’s working profit on that basis would
be over £17,000. As administration costs
oughtn’t to be £2OOO annually, the div.
could comfortably be over 3s. a year. Should
the four tons of stuff on hand yield propor-
tionately, the gold return for the run just
ended will be 2500 z. more, or another Id.
per cub. yd.
It is still a tough job to find out exactly
where the New Langi Logan stands. Toole’s
half-yearly confession seems to switch the
wash off the 2oz. rails and send it down on
the loz. siding. So far as plans show, he
hasn’t rushed out to the east side of the lead
but lias kept prodding away on the western
reef. Late samplings shape up to 25dwt.,
and Toole isn’t such an ass as to move off
a run of gold when he strikes it in a lead
like the Langi. llis best foot is put forward
on the Cathcart gutter, where he has a
sampling record of 2foz. per fathom against
174dwt. on the Langi lead. There, too,
water is light, and the gutter of the right size
to collect profits from quickly. Toole’s
trouble is not that his income for the half-
year was made up of calls £12,713 and gold
only £1241, but that he caju’t yet shove into
the wash. If lie could get properly at the
Cathcart gutter he ought not only to hunt
the co. off the call-list, but be able to get
the east reef drive off No. 2 shaft out to the
Langi gutter. With that going and the main
south drive carried farther along from No. 1
shaft he could have the water under com-
mand, sample a block of the big lead and
discover the real worth of that gutter.
* * * -jf
Pernatty, the big S. Aus. copper-leaching
proposition, reports its first consignment of
precipitate to Wallaroo. There seems to
be no question as to the magnitude of the
ore-deposit; but, despite occasional reports
of faces assaying up to 20 p.c. copper, bulk
quality is comparatively low. It is a metal-
lurgical problem—how to get a maximum
extraction at a minimum, cost. In this re-
spect the management is to be credited with
a policy of hasten slowly. The existing
plant is purely experimental, but is so
designed that should it be successful it will
be a unit of a large installation. The prac-
tical men assert their satisfaction with re-
sults, but this satisfaction does not seem to
be reflected in Adelaide, judging by the life-
less market for the stock. Adelaide is
nothing if not a boom market; but apart
from the preliminary flutter it has shown
no disposition to excite itself over Pernatty.
* * * *
At last Tom Husband has been allowed to
print the tragedy of. the Briseis in 1914.
Against profits of £37,728 shown in 1913, a
loss of £4266 has to be exposed ; so the co.
stepped back in the year nearly £42,000.
That may not be all, for unless the invest-
ments of the London directors were fluked on
to the market before the war there will pro-
bably be a pretty stiff shrinkage in the re-
serve investments of £50,000. The year’s
results were, due to drought, war and smash-
up of the metal market for months after the
Huns attacked the Allies. Half-time had to
bo worked everywhere on the mine, and with
less work done costs ran up out of recogni-
tion. The big job ahead is to get rid of the
basalt crown on the Ringarooma Hill, Some
of this stuff costs 2s. 6d. a cub. yard to shift.
Still the nozzles are slowly wearing the mass
down, and with the Krushka Flat again
giving fair returns,'finances can’t be so drab-
colored as when the curtain rang down on
1914.
* * * *
From the people who own it: —•
Speaking- of the Papuan oil-field Tite Bulletin
(2/9/’l5) said the Petroleum World is “published
ia the interests of the oil rings.” This is the exact
opposite of the truth. The Petroleum World, with
its weekly and daily, has a policy distinguished by
its rigorous opposition to any aggrandisement of
the large oil corporations at the expense of others.
Two examples maj' here be briefly mentioned: —
(1) When a cosmopolitan combination of some of
the largest oil interests secured privileges in Trini-
dad inimical to independent concerns, we strongly
attacked the Home Government for the grant of
those privileges; and but for the fact that, without
breaking faith, the Government could not go back
on its decision, those privileges would undoubtedly
have been curtailed.
(2) The Standard Oil interests, the largest “oil
ring” of all, engaged in a public controversy with
us a year ago—the only instance on record where
they adopted such a course, at all events in the
United Kingdom. They retired considerably the
worse for the encounter.
It was with the desire of issuing a warning before
it was too late, and of saving the great oil wealth
of Papua from the fate (say) of the Trinidad oil
industry, that the article in the Petroleum World
was written.
Then Heaven save the Papuan field from
its disinterested friends! The Common-
wealth’s policy was to keep the oil rings
out, and work this field itself. The
Petroleum World, however, said: “Only
the .Standard Oil, the Dutch Shell Co., Lord
Cowdray’s group or the Burmah Oil Co.
can possibly properly develop this great oil-
bearing area.” From this we are to assume
that (lie Petroleum World is unfriendly to
the great oil rings. The Bulletin accepts
its friend’s statement that it is so. But it
shows how the English language varies in
shades of meaning with latitude and longi-
tude.
* * * *
Among recent cables' is a statement that
the Anaconda is producing zinc by an elec-
trolytic process. The first serious Broken
Hill attempt to make spelter was by that
method; in fact, the Sulphide Corporation
was primarily formed to work Ashcroft’s
Process, and the Central mine at Broken
Hill was merely a side-line. But it turned
out to be far too expensive, and after it had
cost the co. well on to quarter of a million
one way and another, it was abandoned.
Ashcroft himself, however, never lost hope,
and was recently working on it in England.
