Sydney, Novum beb 21), 1915.
My Dear Ahini, —
The jubilee of the Parliamentary Labor
party tailed off in a concert at the Toavu
Hall. Empty galleries and a thin attend-
ance on the ground floor gave the performers
a hearty reception from the echoes that lie
in wait for such occasions. Premier Holman
and J. D. Fitzgerald trod softly to their
seats as the National Rifle Reserve band Avas
about to blare Auber’s overture, “Masa-
niello,” which it handled with spirit. Mrs.
Slapoffski supplied samples of her top notes,
Dick Ryan tried the “Heroes of the Darda-
nelles” on an audience that wanted more,
organist Arlom thundered on the big instru-
ment, and a. ucav soprano, Miss Evans,
shoAved promise of better things.
Labor’s effort to attract a crowd at the
Agricultural Grounds on Saturday after-
noon fell flat. War funds come so far first
now that trying to gather in coin for other
purposes is a forlorn hope. Buxom Lady
Doris Blackwood, who is staying Avith the
Stricklands, was Avith the State Governor’s
small party at the carnival.
Papuan’s long Excellency Murray is re-
covering from a badly-sprained ankle. He
met the mishap in the Backblocks, and had
to be carried for miles till the cavalcade
came to a horse and the fringe of civilisa-
tion. His Ex.’s eldest son Terence, by the avu.v,
has just been gazetted Captain. Young
Murray has seen a good deal of service for
his 24 years. Noav he’s back in France after
convalescing in England. As I told you
before, he gave Philippa Kitchener, a niece
of K. of K., an engagement ring a Avhile
ago. The wedding is to take place Avhen the
youth gets his first leave.
Mrs. Murray is likely to come this way
any day from Hobart. She Avants to help
a small son, Pat, spend his Christmas holi-
days.
Some day something good may happen to
the old barn knoAvn as the Blackfriars
Training College for Teachers. It may be
burnt down or bToAvn away. Meanwhile the
students swelter under its iron roof in sum-
mer and shiver in its tireless rabbit-warren
in winter. Occasionally they have a drop-
tools afternoon, when the temperature gets
high in the nineties, and they take tram to
town to there meditate on the palace that
houses their Education bosses.
In this unlovely old Blackfriars shed there
was a first-rate exhibition last week. The
Art work, mostly of the bold poster style,
that hung round one burrow, chiefly
done by pupils of J. C. Wright, was
an unusually strong exhibit for a
students’ show. Two young artists,
Misses Young and Bennett, were both
humorous and decorative in their dash-
ing color friezes—“ The Forty Thieves” and
a comic Bunny family sketch. Art needle-
work, in which the early Victorian child’s
handiwork —often embroidered with tears —
was imitated, proved the deathless fame of
cross-stitch. Miss Wark, the slim young
schoolmarm in charge of this department,
which dovetails in with that under Miss
Marsden, is keen to get Australian bird-life
into silken stitches. Furniture made by
the male students formed solid islands in
a small ocean of kindergarten cardboard,
backed up by Montessori relief.
The Teachers’ Training College, by the
way, has a grievance. It can get no official
assurance that students AA T ho are specially
adapted to teach one subject Avill not be
dumped into a school that does not seem to
have ever heard of their extra qualifications.
An exceptionally clever Art student may
Avilt amongst the three R’s, and a profound
scholar in algebra may be anchored to pupils
Avho dote on fine needlework.
To the list of wounded ex-A.D.C.’s has to
be added the name of Pollock, Avho was on
Lord Denman’s staff for a while.
The suggested memorial to Nurse Cavell
in Sydney xvi 11 probably be designed by
Theo CoAvan. At present, though, the idea
is not whipped into form.
A Mecca of Avomen last Aveek Avas the
“Baby’s Kit” exhibition held in a building
in Jamieson-street. It displayed complete
sets of garments to be forwarded to the impe-
cunious baby of our Allies in furrin’ parts.
Really a Hordern grandchild or any other
gilded Pink Toes couldn’t Avish for anything
more complete or comfortable. There must
be some exquisite needleAA’orkers and knitters
and designers amongst our voluntary
Avorkers. Most of the outer garments Avere
fashioned from scraps and remnants given
by Red Cross or the shops, but the piecing
work was done with such skill that you’d
think the workers had a limitless choice of
the best. And as for accessories for Baby !
One lady who had gifted her old evening coat
now saw it as a beautiful cot-quilt and lined
with blanket. Selvedges of flannel were
crocheted with giant needles into mats for
baby feet. Truly woman in wartime is
desperately designing and hath sought out
many inventions.
Colonel Bob Owen came home the other
day to a vociferous welcome. The eldest son of
the late Percy Owen, of Wollongong, Colonel
“Bob” has always been popular in the
South Coast. Years ago he went into the
British army as a youngster, then retired
and filled a soldiering billet in Maoriland.
In the intervals he used to trek home to
see dad and the cabbage-tree palms.
Wounded at Gallipoli, he is now convales-
cent after a civic reception in his native
town.
The Bush Nursing Association, which
Lady Dudley made such a good fight for, has
just Avon the D.S.O. at far-away White
Cliffs. The lonely opal-mining camp hasn’t
had a doctor since the war nearly killed the
gem trade, and diphtheria played a coward’s
trick and fell on the town when it had no
red lamp burning. All it had Avas Nurse
Collins, one of the bush sisterhood. But she
Avas as good as an army corps and a mile of
trenches; and Avhen a doctor, posted out from
Broken Hill, arrived, she had already saved
eight lives and Avas beating the enemy across
the Momba Plains. Three other patients
Avere lost after the Government doctor came ;
but that, it seems, Avas the end of the epi-
demic.
Perth’s Anglican shepherd, Archbishop
Riley, arrived from Melbourne the other
morn. The long, wiry-lookirig cleric, who
is a friend of the vice-regal Stricklands, was
pounced on by Private Secretary Finn when
lie shoAved his gaiters on Central Station.
Asked if he’d rather rest and have a snack or
go AA r ith his Ex. to the Red Cross depots, his
Reverence chose “socks” for a start. The
Perth visitor is known as a good sport in the
West, and is on quite friendly terms with
race-club secretaries and other sinners.
In Melbourne one notices the heavily-
embroidered veils worn by the comparatively
smart, and the very short skirts of the ultra-
fashionable. The latter vagary is about the
only sartorial excitement a Sydneysider can
pick out in Collins-street. Evening duds of
the fresh-air cut of peace-time have been re-
modelled. Fairly high at the back, the
theatre gowns that float in from the Best
Suburbs are mostly meek enough to pass
muster at a Y.W.C.A. party. Arms still
gleam through a film of transparency, so
the sacrifice has some compensations.
The death of Cecil Lamb, formerly of the
Permanent Force here, removes a once-jaunty
personality. He was killed by pneumonia in
England, whither he had gone to try and. get
into the firing-line. His widoAV, who lives
in N. S. Wales, is a sister of Dr. Stewart
McKay.
“Bertie” Adams, of Chatswood, is making
a London success in the name part of the
frisky comedy “Mademoiselle Champagne.”
The Adams family have a turn for musical
fluff. A brother of Bertie’s wrote the pre-
ceding lively “Scarlet Rhapsody” Avith satis-
factory results.
Lieut.-Colonel Robertson, lately at Thurs-
day Island, notes a considerable drop in the
thermometer. He was recently promoted
and takes the command of the R.A.G.A. in
Victoria. In consequence, the tall soldier’s
headquarters are at Queenscliff, where a
wintry wind races round the fort eight
months in the year. War-time having
thinned out the subaltern staff, the big
house that used to be the officers’ mess, on
the crest of the cliffs, is being spring-cleaned
for the C.O. and his family. A bungalow
cot, facing the Rip, houses the two lone
subs, Mr. Kelly and Mr. Brown, who occa-
sionally trip this Avay on duty.
On December 11 Joan of Arc will toss her
plumes at the Theatre Royal. The proceeds
of this matinee Of Mrs. Boult’s drama go
towards Christmas sugar-plums for the kid-
dies of fallen soldiers. Gifts in kind will
be Avelcomed vociferously at Siddeley Cham-
bers, Hunter-street.
By the Christmas Eve mail craft Mrs.
Reggie Davis and family, from Scone, will
float to France to join Dr. Reggie and his
car. Mrs. “Banjo” Paterson’s small sister,
Miss Walker —one of the Tenterfield crowd —
goes as company, Avith a hope of somewhere
seeing one of her three soldier brothers Avho
is invalided to England.
A sprig of the Limerick family-tree was
cut off at the battle of Loos after doing some-
thing very gallant. He Avas a Pery—Syd-
ney and Brisbane knew him as “Laddie”
,—and when in these parts, where he mar-
ried, mostly drove a pen for the Bank of
North Queensland. Kitchener sent the
mother his admiration and regrets in the
same envelope.
lima Elliott, the Sydney girl Avho had a
long innings as pupil of Madame Christian,
is said to have as many engagements at con-
cert work as she can get through in England.
And Carrie Lancely, the Gore Hill soprano,
is rushing through a dizzy round of patriotic
concerts in London before packing up for
home and gum-trees.
Cement, sugar, chaff or any similar hessian
bag will be accepted by the Sandbag Commit-
tee at4s3Pitt-street. Alter them first to sched-
ule size, 14 inches by 33 inches. And for good-
ness sake make the seams overlap an inch,
so that the Front won’t be disturbed by bags
on the burst. Leave six inches open down
the top seam. Then ask the boy of the
household, who always knows where the
string goes, to spare you about two feet of it
for each bag. It isn’t necessary to fill
them with soil.
Alice Currie, a Sydney girl with a voice,
is singing at drawing-room entertainments in
London. She camps with Ruth Bannister,
the wood sculptor. Ruth’s mamma pours out
the tea.
Lieutenant Maurice Lenehan, who left
with an artillery lot, put the usual question
to Zelma Robertson before he went. The
girl is the only daughter of the George
Robertsons, of Woolwich. The young man’s
brother Eric, who did his bit at the Front,
is getting well in Randwick Hospital. The
youths are sons of Colonel Bob, who divides
his time between the law and eighteen-
pounders.
The woolly Bloxhams, of the Western
Plains, are threatened with an artist in
their family. Cecilia is a promising young
student, whose brushwork added to the
receipts of the English stall in Martin Place
on Allies Day.
“Tryphena” Doughty, once on The
Bulletin staff, is again in the Inky Way.
This time it is as one of the owners of a
Grimsby paper in the Cold Land. A step-
son, Wilfred, edits the journal.
The Last Post sounded on Gallipoli for
Frank, eldest son of the Frank Coffees, of
Chatswood, Avho are on the Wouldn’trow
Wilson side of the Pacific at present. Frank,
junior, who supported the twin stars of a
lieutenant, was attached to one of the Vic-
torian battalions. In peaceful times he was
on the writing staff of one of the Melbourne
dailies.
The robust Major Stanley Ryrie and his
missus came over here from Melbourne on
Friday. The pair feel kind of dislocated;
they had made final fixings for a trip abroad,
he to the war, she to Egypt, when the
authorities called everything off. They told
the Major he was most wanted to train men
nearabouts. Mrs. Ityrie’s cousin, one-time
fluent Hilda Flood of Sydney, is matron of
a private-house hospital in England, while
her husband, Colonel Percy Owen, does six
months in India on some engineering ex-
cursion.
The Peace Society this week had Dr.
Strong, the Melbourne theologian, to preside
over its cooing. “Business as usual” is the
motto of this society, which still offers a
bun and a glass of soda to the mad bull of
"Europe.
The Mosman Macleod girls have laid aside
their steel sock-spikes for a spell and taken
on the spinning of the yarn instead, with the
real thing in wheels, same as the chorus
sings to in “The Flying Dutchman.” The
covering of Mary’s lamb when it reaches
their hands is really snow-white, but before
getting into circulation it is spun, skeined
and dyed khaki with the juice of our own
gum-leaves. There’s pure Australian for you.
Gordon Inglis, a former Sydney press-
man, is now a lieutenant in the Grenadier
Guards, “somewhere in France.” Boyd
Cable, another Australian writer, who has
had over 12 months in the trenches, has at
last been invalided to England with a gas-
tric trouble. He wears his two stars with
an artillery lot.
The Free Kindergartens are dodging the
measles and working up their light infantry
for the usual pro Christmas show days.
Before getting back to Melbourne Lady
Helen had a look at the Training College in
Liverpool-street and the Surry Hills school.
The Training College, by the way, has an
interest of its own in its new quarters. The
big, old-fashioned house that now shelters it
used to be the home of the late John Rae.
To this old public servant’s facile pencil
Sydney owes many sketches of what it used
to look like when Darlinghurst was “the
bush,” and its few residents found it o’
nights by the light of lanterns.
Nurse Hay, who matroned at Clarmore
private hospital, is bound for active service
in one of the war-area hospitals. She goes
as a plain “sister,” but if some of the big
surgical guns at the halting place discover
her identity, it’s even money they’ll promote
her on the field, so to speak.
The Federal Income Tax Department in
Sydney is good for the liver but bad for the
temper. The box into which the fateful re-
turn may be dropped is at the top of in-
numerable stairs. The lift can take about
12 out of a possible 50 who make the pil-
grimage. It might seem a simpler arrange-
ment to put the box at the street-level, b«t
that’s merely a woman’s stupid idea.
Literature isn’t written these days—it is
dictated to typistes by stout business men
who want to persuade some little “general”
to pay 12s. 6d. and get her hair made glossy
or her tallow skin converted in one week to
blush rose. A friend who has temporarily
given up housekeeping and sent the maid
home for a holiday has been engaged weed-
ing out of her employee’s mail the obvious
shark-circulars. The Yankee shark with the
miraculous hair-raiser was the most raven-
ous with his demand for one week’s wages
for his rubbish ; but there were also jewel-
lers and drapers—particularly drapers. One
firm of very eminent ragmen sent an envelope
full of tears and sobs begging to know why
Miss Broom liadn t favored them with her
esteemed orders, and promising the sack to
any employee who hadn’t guessed what she
wanted and leaped to get it. a a hat a howl
there would be it the liquor-sellers chased
custom into every suburban hole and corner
in this way! But the temperance movement
hasn’t yet touched the drapery trade,
Maudie Telfer, who went away from Syd-
ney with the Quinlan Opera Co., has gone
into vaudeville. So has the Westralian
soprano Lulu Benstead, the times being out
of joint for high-class trills. Pianist Carl
Budden Morris, a' former pupil of Yvonne
Charvin, is making a crust at teaching iu
London, with occasional appearances at the
keyboard.
Dorothy Gumming, the Sydney girl v is to
play Anne of Austria in the Julius Knigln
revival of “The Three Musketeers” at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, with Olive Wilton as
Miladi.
Adela Panlckurst’s recent explosion hero
on mueb the same lines as the familiar
shrieks of a year or two ago. Then the
attack was on man in the abstract—par-
ticularly if he wore a policeman’s hat. Now
the oratory foams over man in the con-
crete—the man with the gun who is fighting
for you and me and the Pankhursts. Some-
how the voice of Adela, which races like a
boarding-house bell, can’t convince a Peace
Army to materialise. But she might pick
up a wilted brigade at the street corners.

m
Lipton’s
Tea.
By Special Appointment to Ilis Majesty the
King, Purveyors to the late Queen Victoria
and King Edward the Seventh, the King and
Queen of Italy, the King and Queen of Spain ,
and to every Viceroy of India for the past
23 years.
THERE can be no better recom-
mendation to the virtues of Lip-
ton’s Tea than the fact that in
India, which is the greatest Tea-pro-
ducing country in the world, Lipton’s
Tea has reigned supreme in the Viee-
Regal Court for nearly an ordinary
working lifetime.
Lipton’s colossal organisation has
been described as “The business on
which the sun never sets,” and Lip-
ton’s Tea as “The Pride of Five Con-
tinents.”
A Medical Opinion of
Lipton’s Tea.
Read what Dr. N. E. Yorke-Davies, the
Famous Nerve Specialist says regarding
Lipton’s Tea:—

COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, 24th November, 1915.
Sale of Trade and Customs Launch,
“Golden Fleece.”
TENDERS will be received until noon
on Wednesday, Bth December. 1915,
for the purchase of the Trade and Customs
Steam Launch “Golden Fleece,” as she now
lies at the port of Sydney.
Full particulars regarding the launch can
be obtained from the Collector of Customs.
Customs House, Sydney, and arrangements
for the inspection of the Launch can also be
made.
Tenders are to be endorsed “Tender for
Launch ‘Golden Fleece’ ” on top left-hand
corner of envelope, and addressed to the
Director of Navy Contracts, Navy Office,
Melbourne; or to the Director of Navy Con-
tracts, care Naval Staff Office, Edgecliffe,
Sydney.
The highest or any tender not necessarily
accepted.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, November 29, 1915.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Lady Helen is going to start the small-
hospital ball rolling on Saturday, when slm
will open the Higliton Home for convales-
cent fighting-men at Balwyn. "The moral
effect of these small hospitals,” according to
a medical writer on the 600 similar 80-bed
places established in Great Britain since the
war, “is quite satisfactory. There is no
doubt that the mental attitude of the soldiers
in the small hospital is better than that of
those in the large hospital.” Higliton (lent
by the George Doerys) is to be kept going
on a 20-bed basis by the local Red Cross and
the municipality of Camberwell; and the
opening is in the nature of an advertisement
of the fact that a dozen or more beds can be
put in it' the cash is forthcoming.
The Madden dame was lately presiding
over an Edith Cavell meeting, when some
of those present ventured to revive the silly
statue project. Thereupon the lady-presklent
rose and swung her peggy-bag violently—a
characteristic action when she’s agitated.
“Statue!” she remarked explosively.
“Statue! There are statues everywhere!—
crowds of the hideous things—all over Mel-
bourne!” The meeting breathed hard
through its nose, and, duly chastened, tried
to think of something else.
Vice-regal Malvern is now holding smell-
ing salts to'its nose and trying to think hard.
The new mayoress is president of the local
Red Cross Sewing society. At her meetings
you can have tea if you like to pay—-which
is a hateful way of getting anything. When-
ever her predecessor called the girls together
for sewing, there were cakes and straw-
berries and not hi »{/ to pay. The benefac-
tress gave the tea out of her own bag,
y’know, and always had a pretty collection
of Society’s first line to help with the eating.
The cream-puffs these women nibbled proved
good investments, because money for the
good cause fell out of their clothes every
time they reached for more. Of course
directly the free food stopped—at any rate,
the Malvernites don’t seem to get the same
thrill out of their Red Crossings now.
The Hennessy dame is starting an ener-
getic she-campaign against German goods.
She is contemplating a pledge-roll in which
women will promise on their best hats to
give first preference to Australian and second
lo British goods in all their shopping. Hip,
hip, hooray; but wouldn’t a serviceable
tariff save a lot of .well-meant fussing?
When Mrs. John Sanderson started her
series of musical parties in aid of Lady
Stanley’s Fund, the black-panelled mush-
room at Brie Brie was filled with Toorak’s
hest and baldest. The hostess warbled
sweetly to a Harold Elvins accompaniment,
in a white frock, scrambled over with black
lace, and with a red rose tucked
in her belt. Amongst the audience
were Mrs. Fritz Hart, snuggled up
in furs, and handsome Eva Robert-
son, her dark hair well dressed and some
good diamonds peppered over the corsage.
But Mrs. Ronald Murray was smartest of
all, in a- black-and-white arrangement.
Towards the end of the show, friend Cornish,
ihe host’s brother-clubman, expressed a few
tactful regrets for the host’s absence, ex-
plaining that he was away in the mountains
struggling with whooping cough, or croup,
or something absurd.
On Saturday afternoon Mr. and ; Mrs,
Rutter Clarke surrendered Merriwa,
L’oorak, for a fete in aid of the French Red
Dross funds. Tlve word was passed round
that one of the French hospitals had been
abandoned for lack of money, and all the
good Australians present got out their
eheque-hoolcs to keep the disease from
spreading. The Merriwa gardens are beau-
tiful, and tramloads of people answered the
call. The Bells, Crevellis, Langdons and
Ccwpers peddled things to eat; Mrs. Tren-
ery had charge of the raffles; the Alstons
and the Milnes got rid of bags and baskets;
and the Davis girls ran a parcels delivery
—a brilliant idea designed to save people
from being seen with bundles of merchandise.
The show raged till far into the evening
with concerts and theatrical showettes.
The two Norman Armytage boys are on a
military sick-list issued from Malta. Mrs.
Norman and daughter Edna, who had just
returned from a Bananaland trip, are hur-
riedly repacking their steamer-trunks. One
of the invalids only knew that a commission
had fallen on him when he woke up in the
officers’ end of the hospital.
Mrs. Artie Staughton has brought her
little family down from the country for in-
spection by Gran’ma Winter-Irving. The
little group consists of three small girls and
an infant sister. Mrs. Winter-Irving’s two
younger daughters, Florence and Laura, are
in Germany, and there must be little com-
fort in the war news for their mother.
Admiral Bridges will be looked after by
daughter Olive while his wife escorts daugh-
ters Alys and Marjorie to London to see
brother Jack off to the fighting ditches in
France. Marjorie fluttered about Melbourne
Hospital for a while and picked up some
useful knowledge; but her nursing education
stopped short when she contracted tonsilitis.
The widow of barrister Purves is closing
down her Toorak home and going to London,
accompanied by the only unmarried daughter.
One of the Purves sons has beeu killed, an-
other is missing, and a third has been
wounded.
Willie McCulloch was fa rewelled by liis
sporting friends the other night, before going
in search of military glory. Willie held a
commission some years ago in a smart
British regiment, and only gave up soldier-
ing for pastoral pleasures when he married
Edie Smith, one of the erstwhile belles of
Melbourne.
Melba’s son, George Armstrong, and bis
attractive wife left by the Makura to join
mother in California, where she has taken
a house for the summer and whoops the
praises of her surroundings. Meantime.
Coombe Cottage reposes somnolently in its
holland covers.
And here is little sister Adelaide: —
The Mawson collection of Antarctic and Javanese
pictures, which has been on view at the Society of
Arts Booms, has been purchased for £250 by the
Public Schools Floral and Industrial Society. The
Society is going- to exhibit them, of course.
(*. 11. Angas returned by the Mooltan. Mrs.
Angus is still in England. One of her two sons is
in the artillery,, tic. other in\the Naval Flying Corps.
The Edward...l law kers are in Sydney, for the mar-
riage of their .eldest daughter, Frances, to one of the
officers,' of Deniliquin.
Mrs. Herbert Woodham, who was a Beresford and
has been here for a month or so, returns to Rangoon
in early December. . Her husband’s people were
booked to go hack to England at about the same
time, but their plans are altered and they stay here
for the early part of the summer.
The widow of the late John Darling died at Nor-
wood during the week. She was a daughter of the
late Alexander Dowie, and one of her daughters is
Mrs. F. W. Young, wife of our Agent-General.
Colonel David Fulton, who left here with the 3rd
Light Horse, and was wounded early in the day at
the Dardanelles, has convalesced in England and is
on his way hack to Gallipoli.
The sad news has come through from London that
Mrs. Philip Robin, whose husband was killed at the
Dardanelles, has died, and with her the infant son.
Adelaide knew her as Miss Nellie Honevwill, and her
marriage only took place after the arrival of the
Australian Expeditionary Forces in Egypt.
The Lancelot Stirlings’ eldest son has a commis-
sion in the Grenadier Guards. Of his two brothers
one enlisted as a trooper in the 3rd Light Horse,
was invalided with enteric to England, and, later,
received a commission in the artillery. The other
left for Egypt a few weeks ago with the Ist Aus-
tralian Remount Unit. His uncle served under
Botha in the South-West African campaign ; a
cousin, Miss Mary Stirling, has been Working at a
soldiers’ canteen at Rouen ; and a sister, whose, hus-
band is at the Front, is busy in a London military
hospital.
Tattersall’s races at Victoria Park on Saturday
were, from a feminine standpoint, a very drab affair.
Muriel Starr and some of her co. provided the little
excitement we got.
The Elder Scholarship for pianoforte-study has
gone to Merle Robertson, of Rose Park. She takes
her 17 years off to the London College of Music.
Yet another “Button Day” occurred on Friday,
when the Y.M.C.A. made an appeal to the public for
help and got £lOOO. The amount of Y.M.C.A. note-
paper the boys send home shows that the organisa-
tion reaches right up tb the -firing line.
Sandbag Day- and Nurses’ Day are the next fix-
tures —the latter in connection with the Edith
Cavell Army Nurses’ Fund, which was launched at
a meeting at the Victoria Hall on Thursday after-
noon, with Dr. London in the chair, and Lady Gal-
way as chief speaker.
Blanche Francis and her pupils were responsible'
for a good exhibition of wood-carving during the
week. The exhibits ranged from a three-piece bed-
room suite to mere carved stands for the umbrella
father forgot to return.
Exit from Adelaide streets “Patsy” Weldon, a
crippled beggar, who has long been one of the city’s
best-known figures. He is getting old, and the
police, finding him “not a fit subject to be at large,”
withdrew the toleration extended up till now to his
proffered box of matches and his rattled tin. Patsy
declined to go to “the Destitute,” so the S.M. thought
two months’ gaol and .“some other institution” to
follow would be the kindest thing.
The balance-sheet of the three-day Government
House fete, published promptly, showed total receipts
£.8772, with a little more yet to come in.
Expenses £522 —just under 6 per cent.; and that
mostly went in the erection of the Old English
t illage, which drew £l2OO at the gate. The net .
profit,-well over £BOOO, is to be divided between the
Red Cross and the Belgian Fund.
Andrew Fisher, who goes to take Reid’s
chair at the dinner-tables of Empire, lias
handed over Oakleigh Hall to the military
authorities for use as a convalescent war-
rior’s home. It is very good of him; but
somehow the convalescing Australian war-
rior isn’t rushing these homes. If he hasn’t
a home and a mate can possibly push him
in anywhere, that’s where he goes nine times
out of ten.
George Chirnside’s Audrey, who has been
hurrying about Cairo entertaining wounded
soldiers with motor-rides, book-reading and
so forth, is now in hospital herself. She
has gone down with measles, and is in a
private hospital at Port Said. Mother is in
Australia, but Dad is somewhere handy, so
she won’t be neglected.
There; is not too much neutrality about
some Swedes. The son of Dickson W aern,
the Swedish Consul in Melbourne, has been
studying in Dad’s land, but is coming back
to enlist with the Kangaroos.
A note from “Bosna.” in London: —
Travelling on the Continent is a desperate enter-
prise nowadays. Mrs. Langdon Parsons and daughter
Muriel, of Adelaide, who have been marooned here
since the war began, decided that they would like
to look at Europe on the way home, and set about
the intricate business of hunting passports. The
French Consul didn’t object to their glancing at
France, but when they mentioned Switzerland his
hair stood on end and his collar nearly burst with
anxiety. Further, he put his official foot down with
a firm hand, so to speak, and said “Xo" many times.
As a favor they were allowed to go on to Italy,
with many warnings as to the fate of those who
will travel in war time. But as their quarters in
London had been in the very centre of the Hun’s
favorite bomb-lrurling grounds the warning fell
slightly flat. You may expect them in a fortnight
or so after you get this.
Katherine Prichard, the prize-novelist damsel,
goes home to Melbourne and mother a week after
this. Her book “Pioneers” is going to be specially
filmed for Australian production, and ought to make
a good thriller, as it has plenty of indent and
good Australian color. Its author has spent
five busy and successful years journalising on this
side and . lias earned her holiday.
The second Rupert Clarke daughter Aimee was
married to Lieut. Charles -Gerardy" of the Grenadier
Guards, on October 19. The benedict is the only
son of the people who own Wriglitington Hall,
Wigan, and is a cousin of Lord Gerard. Further-
more, the family owns a private chaplain who
coerced Hymen for the occasion. The bride wore
a white chiffon frilly garment festooned with small
pink and white rosebuds, and her step-sister, the
Grey-Eger ton maiden, aided her through the cere-
mony. A formal reception was not held, but some
of the peerage and a sprinkling of Australians
witnessed the show.
That was a beautiful' entertainment at
the recent Exhibition carnival when the
Ambulance Gup was decided. There was
an overflowing entry and the thing had to
be run off in several divisions, the nags
being draught-horses, requiring plenty of
room for rolling; while the jocks were
mostly social first-flighters. Such excite-
ment —whip and spur and language ; whoops
and howls and screams from the multitude;
crash of hoofs upon the unyielding turf of
the well-worn Oval; the climacteric roar as
old Blossom lollops past the winning-post,
a good top-lip in front of the field. And
then the moans of the dying punter who has
placed his all upon the second horse- —and
lost.
Hundreds of the hest (and least employed)
damsels attend daily at the Wirtlis’ Park
Rest House to do two-hour housemaid shifts
for the dear wounded soldiers. The chief
want of the establishment is soldiers to rest
in it; and the sweet volunteers stifle their
yawns with pale hands that smell of yellow
soap. Can’t the wretched Government pass
a regulation compelling all soldiers to rest
for an hour every day?
The Repertory Club has been devoting
itself to drama in national compartments.
Russia, Italy, France and Belgium having
been artistically- worked, the clubbers pro-
pose to hold an Australian Day on December
3. Th& m.usic will be Australian, someone will
sing Louise Clarke’s of secretary
Ethel Castilla’s “Summer,” an Australian
plaflet will be run off, and some home-grown
verSe declaimed.
The volunteer lady-helps who were given
jobs in the various Rest Homes are getting
rather out of hand. The matrons are train-
ing them at present—putting the soft-pawed
pets through a course of scrubbing, dish-
washing and dusting, so that they may be
doinestic.-yA.B.’s when the expected rush
comes. Slime of the damsels think it “just
too horrid" to scrub and sweep and sweep
and scrub, and have never a wounded hero
to, set their dainty housemaid caps at. A
lot of them volunteered under the impression
that they would soon be posing in uniform,
putting a few flowers in delicate specimen
glasses, reading poems to suitably attentive
V.C.’s and taking them for motor drives.
Cory few of them are enthusiastic about
the practical work, and some of the feather-
heads openly declare that matron should
not ask twice girls to do such things.
The Bateses of Balwyn lent their house
and grounds for the usual purpose last week.
This time the Victorian Neglected Children’s
Home was bracketed with the Lady Stanley
apiig'M. .Tollh .Madden, vice-regal understudy,
made - thn usual complimentary noises. The
mayoral Mcßeatlis were helping, also the
neighboring Best dame and the Tom Hogans
from over the White Horse-road. Mrs.
Hogan held up the populace to the extent of
£6O for personally baked or burgled cakes :
and fie Best lady and a tribe of pretty girls
(it’s sno use collecting grandmothers for
these jobs) ran a work-stall very success-
fully. Mrs. McEacharn, Mrs. Baxter and
the Bates daughter managed the flower
shop. The result of it all was £7OO in good
hard coin and overworked bank-notes.
The Repertory Theatre on Saturday night
gave Elizabeth Baker’s “The Price of Thomas
Scott”—which ought to be called “What
Price Thomas Scott?” The audience
v.as composed of Highbrows aud yearn-
ing young things with nice* complexions
and dimpled shoulders. But the fine
performance of McMahon enthused them
all. One business man, however, aud-
ibly criticised the whole business. “Scott
couldn’t cry off bis deal!” objected
this expert. He didn’t know what Privy
Council powers a dramatist has.
A Harper batch and some Grimwades
blessed the show. The Tooraketty Langfords
sat in a bare-backed and well-groomed rank
Miss Cochrane and Miss Grey looked with
professional eyes at the millinery of the
draper’s daughter. Eardley Turner was
somewhere about, and the spectacles of Archi-
bald Strong gleamed in the front row. Hugh
McCrae, who occasionally fills a part him-
self, was another onlooker. A wowserian
touch was heroically attended to by a
woman-player. In the Chief M owser’s ab-
sence, the Scott girl and her young men
clear the living-room for a romping dance
on the carpet. As the girl whirled in a
waltz she showed nondescript stockings, and
dull, flat-heeled footwear below old-fashioned
petticoats trimmed with ugly braid.
Fritz Hart made a considerable hit on
Saturday afternoon with his production of
Henry Purcell’s opera “Dido and /Eneas.’’
Hart wanted to show what a power there is
in English music, and preparations had been
going on for months to that end. Sir Hubert
Parry, of the London College of Music, lent
the full score and orchestral parts, and added
that he was violently interested in the Aus-
tralian experiment. The precious scores
were sent out by different ships, so that even
if the U-boats got a chance, there would still
be hope of some of the music reaching here
safely. A large orchestra was called
together for the treat at Her Majesty’s
Theatre, and Melba’s special pupils sang the
leading roles. The chorus of students num-
bered quite GO, and, as they are nearly all
the musical pets of rich families, the big
audience was a full-furnished one.
Mrs. Oswald Syme and sister Miss Rowe
occupied one box. Mrs. Geoff. Syme. with
white furs about her black mourning garl*
had another. Mrs. Sanderson, in blue, was in
an adjacent pen, and Mrs. Fritz Hart
divided her time between a box and behind-
the-scenes. The John Maddens made a pro-
minent row, and Mrs. Dwyer entertained
a party of 80 people. Vera Bedford, Ran-
dolph’s daughter, was the Queen, and was
magnificent in voice and costume. She
was "in Grecian draperies of gold and amber
with a close-fitting head-dress swathed in a
hanging veil. You are, all going well, going
to hear a lot about this young Australian.
Mrs. Withers is now showing some of
daughter Marjory’s clever work in Collins
House. Surrounding it is a line collection
of the last paintings of the late Walter
Withers. He worked to within two days of
his death, and the stuff he left behind is
well worth looking at. Radcliffe, a man
with an exquisite collection of paintings,
has persuaded the widow to part with her
husband’s last landscape, which was done in
defiance of the doctor. “Complete rest’’ had
been ordered ; but one day the invalid was
missing from his couch in the Hltham coun-
try house. After a long, resultless search
they found him in a patch, of gorse
bushes with a finished sketch —the. last he
was ever to do. Marjory is going to
achieve something as a portrait painter. At
the show two pictures have been bought by
people unacquainted with the sitters, and
merely for their excellent technique.
A thoroughgoing patriotic club recently
had to purchase a piano, and, in pursuance
of its recognised policy of giving preference
to Australian-made goods, and failing them
to the manufactures of the British Empire,
it bought one wearing the stamp of a Leipzig
maker. Several English-made pianos were
offered, and fine instruments are produced
iu Australia, but the Hog article was £5 or
sd. or something less money.
On a recent Saturday afternoon one of
a band of flappers who have been carrying
on the white-feather foolishness in a sea-
side suburb saw a sturdy-looking young man
sunning himself in a deck-chair on a front
verandah. He had a rug wrapped round
his knees, a cigarette in his mouth and a
magazine in his hands. The damsel strode
on to the verandah and thrust a white
feather into the youth’s hand, with the brief
salutation, “Cold feet!” The sun-bather
grinned. “Well, one of them is, I guess—-
it’s buried in Gallipoli,” he observed, and
from beneath the rug thrust the stump of a
leg. The maiden begged the youth’s pardon
and a glass of water almost in the one
breath.
One of the Wi'rths is building some tall flats
in St. Kilda-road, in which no tenant will
be allowed to keep bears. A sedate and
long-established villa which occupies the
ground next to the soaring pile has reared
a green lattice fence nearly 80ft. high. It
makes me think of an old maiden lady blush-
ing behind a fan.
A big chiffon store is selling straps for the
correction of double and treble chins. The
harness is disguised with ribbons and
attached to iace boudoir caps. Last
week a young assistant, intent on mak-
ing a sale to an over-nourished matron,
remarked innocently, “The wife of our
managing director wears one.” Pro-
bably she does; but I felt glad for the
young man’s sake that the loyal shopwalker
was out of range for the moment.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

MELBOURNE.
BALLARAT, BENDIGO, GEELONG.
Clay.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THERE was a liew-made mound of clay.
For the man was dead and done.
And his wife stood by at the close
of day—
His wife and another one.
They talked to each other about him there.
Of the things which they now would do.
Since he had gone where he could not care —
And the man had believed her true!
She said she had “better put up a stone”
To make “some sort of a show.”
And the dead man lay in the dark alone,
And shivered with hate below.
The time went by, and she came once more.
“I am sick of it all!” she said.
“Life is a cell with a bolted door.
And I long to be with you—dead.”
Many a fierce and broken word
She spoke then bitterly.
And the dead man’s bones lay there, and
heard,
And {dickered about with glee.
VICTORIA.
E. M. H.
Caste.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“fll HAT’S another young feller as has
I done himself in,” said the barman, as
the young man who had hurriedly
entered the pub went out again with a bottle
of ale.
“I used to notice him here having a
drink,” I said ; “but he only seems to come
in for bottled stuff now.”
“That’s it,” assented the barman. “He got
married last week.”
Fred was an ancient friend of mine. lie
had been for years at a club I frequented
until a disagreement with one of the officials
terminated his long service. A little too
much of the liquor he was supposed to serve
was the cause of the . disagreement, and as
I was responsible for at least a portion of
the liquor I felt I ought to get him another
billet. On my advice he called at the Angel
of Hope, which, being the last licensed pre-
mises in my district; held me in some
esteem : and I arranged the interview so
that, about three minutes after he entered, I
followed, and was surprised to see him. The
surprise enabled me to comment on his dis-
appearance from the Club, and gave me the
opportunity to express my anger at the
shabby treatment accorded an old and valu-
able servant at the instigation of an official
whom I grossly libelled as a man lacking in
judgment. Then I asked him to have a
drink, which gave him an opportunity of
ordering something soft, and the trick was
done. So Fred got the job, for which he
always entertained a feeling of gratitude,
displayed mainly by filling my glass to the
brim after the foam had receded, and giving
me the news of the neighborhood.
“And that’s why you say he’s done him-
self in, eh?”
“Well," said Fred, “that’s my way of put-
ting it. But he kids himself everything in
the garden’s lovely. It was a sort of
romance, too.”
“What, married his boss's daughter?” I
asked.
“Well, not exactly,” answered Fred. “That
there young fellow’s wife is the daughter
of a grocer!”
“The daughter of a grocer!” I repeated.
“Jusso,” he said. “A pretty tinpot
grocer, too. Still, ihat doesn’t prevent him
having his class pride. You see. I happen
to know all the parties, through my daughter
giving music lessons to her younger sister,
and through him and his mother living next
door to us, and through him coming in here
sometimes, when he was pretty dog-sick at
the way things were going, and drinking
a bit, and talking, lie's only a greengrocer
with a run, you see.”
“Have one,” I said, putting down a coin;
and Fred filled the mugs.
“Simkins—that's liis name: Joe Simkins
—he’s a good bit struck on dances, and once
or twice he meets the . girl—Thelma Brown,
as her name was then, She’s a fine bit of
goods; and Simkins shows what he thinks
of her by taking all the dances he can get.
Of course he can’t take too many, because
another chap’s bought her ticket and it ain’t
etiquette: so finally he buys a ticket for
her himself, and dances all the evening with
her.
“Then she takes him home and intro-
duces him and he hangs up his hat for her.”
“They became engaged?” I ventured.
“No, they don’t —he just hangs up his
hat. That’s a sort of preliminary, so to
speak, to see if anything is doing. After
that thev get courtin’. Then, if the family
approves they start to keep company—
they’re engaged, if you like it better. You
see, several chaps can hang up their hats for
a girl; but once the courtin’ starts it’s the
square thing to let a fellow have a clear
field.
“Of course, until it’s courtin’ serious, the
family don't worry much; and Simkins,
having had the word from Thelma, keeps
quiet about the greengrocery; but at last
he has to explain just what lie is and all
about himself; and he ups an’ says that he
wonts to keep company with the girl.
A COMMON REFUGE.
HE: “Mrs. Verifast says the war has robbed her of everyone .”
SHE: “Yes, poor thing! Her husband went to get away from her, and the other man
went to get away from the scandal .”
“Joe said afterwards that he noticed they
turned kinder cold, but it didn’t strike him
so much just then. They said it was a seri-
ous step, and they would have to consider
the matter carefully, but they’d let him know
next evening. Joe comes along here simply
oczing happiness, shouts drinks, and tells
me he’s going to be married.
“But when he goes along the next night
he gets a surprise. There’s no Thelma, but
there’s pa, ma and auntie waiting to explain
matters. Pa looks uncomfortable, and the
fat seems to be oozing out in perspiration;
but lie’s pompous and self-important. Ma
is cold and dignified; and auntie is there to
sustain the family honor, and inclined to be
indignant that a greengrocer should aspire
to marry into a family that’s been grocers
for generations.
“ ‘Of course,’ says the old man, ‘I regard
you as a most estimable young man—a most
estimable young man’
“ ‘But.’ adds auntie, ‘a greengrocer!’
“ ‘You must realise that it would be a
come-down for my daughter!’
“‘I dunno,’ mumbles Joe. ‘I make good
money, and I make it myself. There’d be no
need for Thelma to look after a shop.’
” ‘That’s just where it is,’ says the old ’un.
(I’ve no doubt the hint, which Joe didn’t
mean, of course, about the way his women
had to help in the shop, cut a bit.) ‘You
have no standing, young man.’
“ ‘lf you really loved my daughter.’ said
his missus
" ‘You would not ask her to make the sac-
rifice,’ finishes auntie.
“So it went on. Joe says it was surpris-
ing what they'd got 'to know about his people.
They knew that his sister worked in a jam
factory; and though Thelma herself went to
toil, she was a machinist in a hat factory,
which is ,a different thing. It wouldn’t have
been so bad if the sister'd been in a shirt fac-
tory or something like that; a girl in the
hat business miglit, with a bit of condescen-
sion, have a sister-in-law in that! But in
a jam factory! It’s only a degree above
pickles!
“Joe finally asks to see Thelma, and learns
that ‘she’s listened to reason’ and gone away
with relatives, an’ in a huff he leaves and
comes down here to get drunk and talk
about what lie’s goin’ to do with her old
man.
“Just as he’s going on like this he
gets a letter from herself which makes all
the difference. She’s heart-broken, she says.
She had to stand the entreaties of her family
for a whole night before she yielded and
went to stay with her auntie; and she only
yielded because she felt he could win them
over. Anyway, she had been expecting to
see him down at the factory day after day,
and as he hadn’t come she felt he must be
trying to forget her.
“Joe did what most young ’uus would do:
he rushed his run through next day and
was down at the factory waiting to meet
Thelma when work finished. You can under-
stand what the meeting was like: but when
they sobered down a bit, she won’t listen to
his idea that they should marry in spite of
all her people. She’s under twenty-one, any-
way; but Joe’s willing to marry her and
take the risk of her people going sore, n
she’ll take the risk of swearing she’s, of age.
But she can’t bear the thought of breaking
with her people and showing ingratitude;
and finally it’s arranged that he must culti-
vate her parents, and try to bring them
round.
“So he calls and tries to make himself
agreeable; but a cold time he has. First, he
tries to win favor by taking along something
special in the way of asparagus; but he
cottons to the idea that that reminds ’em
he’s a greengrocer. Then he takes a pipe for
the old man, or a cushion for the old lady:
but always, just when they seem to be get-
ting to tolerate him. they think of carrots
and cabbages and the social gulf there is
between them. So lie gets no forarder; but
as Thelma’s not about, and they don’t know
he’s meeting her, they don’t mind him being
at the house now and then.
“Old Brown, who never had much of a busi-
ness, was hard hit when butter got scarce.
The people he bought his butter from had
short supplies, and, to get every penny of the
price allowed, they sold for cash, and so
scooped the usual discount. Brown, having
a credit custom, found it difficult to scrape
up the cash; and a rival grocer who got
plenty of butter used it as a draw, and that
made matters worse.
“That’s where Joe comes in. He has a
good few quid stuck away, and he knows of
a chap in the country he has fruit deals with
who has a few cows, and is making butter
and selling it retail, joe buys the whole lot,
paying outsize prices for his fruit to bring
the deal within the law —and hawks the lot
up to Brown’s.
“ ‘There y’are, Mr. Brown,’ says he.
“Joe says he’d just as soon make a present
of it; but Brown insists on paying. Directly
the notice boards about the butter are out
Brown’s old custom flocks back, with a lot
more, and he has whip's of money coming in.
“‘Hey, Mr. Brown,’ says Joe, after he'd
dumped the butter ip to the shop. ‘What
about Thelma?’
“ ‘My dear Simkins,’ says Brown. ‘l’m
sorry, of course, about that; but I really
thought that you had realised the insuper-
able objections.’
“Still, though he was up against caste, Joe
had hopes ; and when Brown comes to him
with the cash for the first lot, and says:
‘.Joe, my boy : do you think you could manage
to get me another lot of butter?’ he says
again :
“ ‘How about Thelma ?’
“ ‘This is a matter of business —pure busi-
ness. Joe,’ says Brown. ‘About the other
we might talk some other time.’
“ ‘But,’ says Joe, ‘butter’s not my -business.
It’s out of my line: and it takes a lot o’
trouble to get. I’ll let you know to-morrow
night. Seven. I’ll be here. A man wouldn’t
mind taking trouble for anybody who’s going
to be his father-in-law,’ he added, as lie went
off.
“When he meets Thelma next evening
after five she tells him she’s got a telegram
that she’s to go home; and she’s at the gate
when he arrives. They go in together, and
Joe says: ‘Mr. Brown. T’l] have that butter
for you to-morrow.’
“ ‘Thanks. Joe,’ says Brown. Then he
comes over like a heavy father in a play and
takes Thelma’s hand and passes it over to
Joe. ‘There’s another thing, Joe —this ! I’ve
thought very seriously over matters, Joe. We
live in a democratic age when a man can rise
and make himself —anything. I know your
heart is gold. Let us remember —
Kind hearts are more Ilian coronets,
And simple faith than Norman blood.’ ”
I laughed. “So they married, eh?”
“Oh, yes; they married. And he’ll get
more’n he wanted,” said the Prophet. “Every
man who marries does. When one of the
ordinary bust-ups occurs, his Thelma’ll make
him happy by telling him how she came down
in the world by marrying him!”
ILAMER.
The Door.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
lOYE shut the door and laughed at me:
i Love shut the door, the golden door.
Shut out the song of leaf and bee.
And silken footfalls on the floor;
Love shut the door of Hope on me:
“Not any more....not any more”....
Love placed the cover on the draught,
And weighed it down with lead and stone.
Oh, then ’twas I who turned and laughed—
Love cannot take his wine alone!
For by my lips across the draught
The bubble of a kiss was blown!
Love shut the door and laughed at me....
Ah, foolish boy ! He has forgot
'Twas I who piped the honey-bee
To snare his music for the spot;
'Twas I who set the sheltering tree,
And tilled the garden plot.
Love shut the pearly door of dreams,
The door that once swung fair and wide.
To show a fountain’s rainbow-gleams,
A lily like a bride.
Love’s empty arms clasp only dreams —
’Tis I who laugh. . . .outside!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

THE HAT TRICK.
FIRST COUNTRY BLOKE: “You look out, Bill. W’en I was in Sydney I lost me noo
hat! Some cove took it an’ left an old ’tin in its place.”
ŚECOND C.B .: “ Them coves needn’t think they’ll beat me. I’d know me ’at anywheres.
’cos I put me intrails in the linin’.”
“8.M.H.”: I know the blood-sucking flies
that “Jaques” (B. 4/11/’l5) curses, and this is
how I balked their attacks on my dog’s ears: I
thinly smeared the flaps with Stockholm tar. As
for the eyes, try an outward application of castor
oil. Mallee mothers have a good word for it.
“Cleveland”: Hold my hat while I get
into this flying-fish argument. I was feeling my
way from Chittagong to Akyab (Amman Coast,
Burmah) in the S.E. monsoon season, and any sailor
who has been there will know what that means.
For the benefit of those who don’t know I may say
that it was as thick as pea-soup, and the only
guide the mariner had was that ascertained by
periodical casts of the patent lead. I had been
taking these every 15 minutes, and was just getting
ready for another cast, when the Lascar who was
holding an electric torch gave a yelp and spread-
eagled on deck. The torch broke, and the deep-sea
sinker bolted with about 500 fathoms of wire line.
In the darkness I was unable to find the brake, but
in fumbling round I connected up the winding-in
gear without knowing it, and when I came to again
it was daylight. It appears that the “admi” who
had been holding the glim had been smitten under
the left eye by a large flying-fish, and I 11 bet that
nig. carries the mark to this day if he has not been
gathered to the breast of Mohammed. The cause of
the trouble was picked up under the wheel gratings,
and measured 9Jin. ■
“Karoo” : The fellah of Egypt uses very
similar methods to those of our Binghi. He goes
after water-fowl by swimming slowly, with his beau-
teous countenance hidden by a bundle of rushes, and
grabs the unsuspecting ducks by the legs, and after
basely drowning them, puts them in a dill.v-bag
worn at the waist. He climbs date palms by walking
up the trunk supported by a vine rope under his
shoulders, and, further, uses the early ’Gyptian boom-
erang, the nep’ta, for bringing down birds on the
wing; but this boom, does not come back. Also, he
catches birds by net throwing.
* * * *
“Hamer”: The turn-to bell must ring
pretty early for the ant. I made up my mind to
catch him going (6 work once, when 1 was in-
habiting a seaside camp, and chose for observation a
scrap of food upon which a gang had been engaged
the previous day. Half an hour before dawn I
inspected the fragment, and saw that work had not
commenced, but when, on the very second of dawn-
ing, I had another look, two or three gangs were
busy on the job. Obviously the Working Ants’
Union hasn’t yet struck the idea of demanding that
travelling shall be done in the boss's hours. By the
way, if you stand a tin of jam or anything similar
that has been attacked by the little pests in a
saucer of water the ants get off in a way that to
me was mysterious until I saw some of the insects
make a bridge of themselves across the water. Over
this their brethren passed, the units in the bridge
then breaking up one by one and lighting out for
home and mother. Only one ant was drowned in the
“Palmerston”: Does a blackfellow ever
get bitten by a snake? Other experienced bushmen
agree that they have never heard of Binghi suffering
that way. All the same, although he eats the original
co-re., the abo. is more frightened of him than of
debil-debil.
NOT A SEA FISH.
“Come overland?”
“Don’t suspect me of coming by submarine, do you?"
“T.F.”: The Ma Slate is not a laud of
milk and honey, but there are spots where fruit and
vegetables thrive without the watering-can. At
Trial Bay (the old prison on the mouth of the
Mrcleay River) passion vines cover the SOft. myrtle
trees, and the ripened fruit can be gathered by the
bagful; further up the river common lemons provide
shelter and a thirst-quencher for the traveller by the
wayside. On the Richmond the roadways are
fringed with yellow' guavas and cape gooseberries,
while further inland gardens planted in the long
past, and forgotten, are a blessing to surveyors and
bushmen. In good seasons, on the upper reaches
of most of the North Coast rivers, there is fruit in
abundance growing wild for the plucking thereof. But
the most remarkable of all the Ma State’s vegetable
plots occur far back in the (sometimes) arid West —-
not “the West” of semi-suburban Bathurst that the
Sydney daily papers talk of, but the real West beyond
the Darling - . In there, on favorable sand-patches,
early explorers dropped melon and pumpkin seeds,
and even to this day you can find fruit, though the
over-stocking of the country with rabbits and sheep
wiped out the best of the wild gardens 20 years ago.
* * * *
“Hay”: Out West-of-Darling the blacks
were never worried very much about food supplies,
there being, generally speaking, both variety and
sufficiency, especially when the Darling overflowed
and tilled the wide lakes. Ducks abounded, and this
v'as one method of making a catch. In the early
morning when the birds were up the creeks feeding,
all hands would start out taking with them a large
net made of shredded sinews. This they would fix
across the creek where it entered the lake. One or
two expert hunters would then be left on each side
of the stream while the rest would get behind the
ducks and drive them down towards the open water.
When the flock reached the mouth of the creek the
Binghis stationed by the net would throw a curious
boomerang-shaped piece of bark, which, when hurled
upwards, had the appearance of a hawk about to
snoop. Then the startled birds, diving headlong
into the water, were enveloped in the net and shortly
afterwards were enveloped in Binghi. The recipe
for cooking them was to encase each bird in a soft
clay plaster, and bake the pie in hot ashes.
“Mujik”: Charlie, who kept the stables
in that queer little country town in Maoriland, once
reckoned the time was ripe to make a forward move.
So he bought a second-hand hearse on time-payment
in the city and added it to his rolling stock, all
burials in the district having previously been con-
ducted with the help of an elongated waggonette
and a black table-cover. He told me about it one
night when Harrows passed us or. the road. “See
that blighter?” he said. “Yer wouldn’t think he
was worth twenty thousand, eh?” No, I wouldn’t.
He didn’t look worth taking notice of. Where did
he get it? “Get it!” said Charles, disgustedly.
“Why, from his ole man, of course. And, take it
from me, he won’t drop any by extravagance. Look
’ere! The day the ole man died, round he comes to
the stables and tells me about it. ‘We’ll ’ave to
bury ’im, Charlie,’ he says. I said I thought he
might as well. ‘I want to do the right thing,
Charlie,’ he says. So I says, ‘Ri’ buck.’ And then
he wants to know how to do it. So I tells him to
go round to Plane the carpenter and get a coffin.
‘Oh,’ he says, ‘l’ll make one in the mornin’. There’s
some kauri boards on the place.’ ‘Oh,’ I says.
‘Yes,’ he says, ‘but how about drivin’ it?’ ‘Well,’ I
says, ‘yer can ’ave wotever yer like now—there’s
the ole waggonette, and I’ve a new ’earse.’ ‘ ’Earse!
Wot, a real ’earse?’ he asks. ‘Yes.’ ‘And ’ow much
will it be, Charlie?’ he asks. ‘Well,’ I says, ‘it’ll
cost five and twenty bob, ’cos I’ve got ter put a
couple o’ ’orses in.’ ‘And ’ow much is the waggon-
ette?’ ‘Oh, that’ll be ’arf a quid.’ He stood there
thinkin’ it over for about 10 minutes, and then he
says: ‘Well, Charlie, that ’earse is dear. Yer know
the ole man was always pretty careful, and I don’t
think he’d like to think I was doing it too gay, so
if yer send the waggonette up after dinner, I’ll
’ave him ready.’ And so ’e did —and I never ’ad a
customer for that ’earse for six months after. The
worst spec. I ever made in me life. It’s no good
trying to send things ahead with people like ‘Arrers
about.”
* * * *
“Dry Shave”: I hate having to court-
martial “Panhandle Pete,” but the first three of his
“bright new expressions”—“old Mighty,” “cushy”
and “jammy” (B. 28/10/’l5) —have got senile decay.
They may be just out of the refrigerator to Billjim.
but Mr. Thomas Atkins has long included them in his
vocabulary. Maybe “Panhandle Pete” has run across
the following: “Square-pushing,” to signify the Sun-
day best, and applied generally to a damsel or a new
uniform: “dekko,” meaning “have a look” or “let
me see,” according to the tone of voice used; “poul-
tice-walloper” for an Army Medical man; “walla.”
tacked to every species of male, such as “the ser-
geant-major walla,” “the ammunition walla '; “work-
ing a ticket,” to express shamming sickness in order
to get discharged unfit; “teek,” which is to say O.K.
To the average British Tommy, especially if he had
seen service on Indian or Egyptian stations, the fact
of these expressions not appearing in Webster would
condemn that venerable and learned gentleman as
“puggled,” or deficient ’■ the mental department.

“Myall”: Can any Abo. correspondent
trot along a horse to beat this performance? Belar.
a stock horse bred on Weribone station (Q.), trav-
elled from Talavera woolshed to Roma and back
again, a distance of 114 miles, in a bushman’s day;
that is, from sunup to sundown. The old fellow
humped a mere 12st. of bone and muscle on this occa-
sion, and finished the trip apparently none the worse
for his exertions. 'This spurt was all the more meri-
torious as Belar had never known the luxury of corn
and chaff. He was a crack camp horse in his time,
and there were few, if any, that could give him a
lead in cutting out a beast.

ARCHITECT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I don’t know if ’twas Bill, or Jim, or Tom who
started it,
Or if the Boss was in the swim—it matters not a
bit.
They all were there at Stiffner’s pub, the Dry Hole
Creek elect,
When Bill said it was “Archie Tect,” and Jim said
“Arky Teat l”
There was no Daniel Webster there to put the matter
through
(And Daniel used to drink himself, if all accounts
are true) ;
But, had the Unabridged been there for all hands to
inspect,
James would have had it “Arky Tect” and William
“Archie Tect.”
Tom said—l think he started it, I’m pretty sure he
did—•
That “Arky” was a nickname that this chap had
when a kid ;
But still those two mates argued it and gave the lie
direct;
And some took sides with “Archie Tect” and some
with “Arky Tect.”
They' fought it out by Dry Hole Creek, where
nothing comes amiss;
And men have fought on redder fields for lesser
things than this.
The constable was referee —and liked it, I expect—-
(His name was Archie lirossington, it wasn’t Arky
Tect).
They fought it out by Dry Hole Creek, like bushmen
of (lie land,
Until they both were blind; and then the seconds
took a hand
And fought until the constable called on them to
reflect—
And none knew if ’twas “Archie Tect” or if ’twas
“Arky Tect.”
K.s.w.
HENRY LAWSON.

Hotel Australia,
November, 10 1915.
Dearest Mother,
Father and I called at Pianola Hall to-day, to change our Pianola
rolls, and arrange for a fresh supply to be sent up to Goulburn. The
Manager invited us to hear the new musical instrument the Pianola
Company have recently put on the Australian market the Aeolian-
Vocalion. Mr. Brown—the Vocalion Manager put several disc records on
the instrument; the tone is wonderful! during a flute solo called The
Wind in the Trees,” it was so realistic, that 1 got quite excited and
caught father by the shoulder. Dad bought one of the £35 models, and a
lot of beautiful disc records. It will be home with us on T hursday, so
there is a treat in store for you all.
f
Your loving daughter, Meg.

“Condamine”: What is the hardest tree to
kill? I reckon two Queenslanders would take beat-
ing. One is known locally as rosewood and the other
is the pomegranate or Taroom tree, a beautiful vege-
table resembling an orange, and an excellent shade.
I have ringbarked," fallen and burnt down rosewoods,
and have not yet succeeded in killing one in five
years. The other specimen I have cut out a foot
below ground (there were no roots to this depth),
and green shoots promptly appeared next year.

“Jimmie Pannikin”: On the road to Gun-
dag-ai from Nangos Creek (N.S.W.) a most estimable
clergyman met a bullock-driver with his heavily-
loaded waggon bogged fair in the middle
of the road. The b.d.’s vocabulary was vile.
The clergyman was shocked. “My friend,”
said he, “you should not use such loose
and lurid language. Prayer is better than pro-
fanity. Providence could easily shift that team for
you.” The bulloclcy drawled: “That’s orlrite. Any
fool could shift th’ bullocks. What I’ve bin tryun
to do fer the larst four bowers is to shift the
bullocks and th’ waggon.”
* * * *
“Private Gumboil, J.P.”: Talkin’ about
flies in Gallipoli, Red Tracey and me live in the
same “wazza” or dug-out. Next door is Pip Dailey
and two others. The wall between us is clay half-
way ; an’ the top is made of sanu' ags. The clay
part projects a foot forward in front. At evening,
when the “strays” and shrapnel are quiet, and we
dine outside, we often use the clay ledge for a
resting-place for one of the courses until we’re
ready. It’s nice an’ high and nothing can get
knocked over. Now, someone had handed Red a
tin of golden syrup which was much yearned after
by our neighbors on either side. In the first open-
ing the tin got well mucked over with stickiness;
and when Red had helped himself, he stood it up on
the ledge between the wazzias. During the usual
contest between us and the flies over each mouthful
of our meal, neither of us noticed anything. But
later, on looking up, we found the tin of syrup had
gone. We begrudgingly had to suspect that Pip
Dailey and his cobbers had pinched it, most likely.
Red got up and stepped round to question ’em.
Pip’s cobbers were sitting there in the twilight,
talking. They told Red to go and get his head read
again—they hadn’t started tea yet. Also, they told
him what to do with his blanky syrup when he found
it. But Red wasn’t satisfied, and lingered about the
front door. Suddenly he stooped down, dived his
hand into a water-tin that stood against the dividin’-
wall, and, disturbing' a million flies, triumphantly
drew forth a tin of golden syrup. Then they started
talkin’ to each other. The two blokes looked sur-
prised, and said that it must have fallen in un-
beknown. The O.C. sent word along that if they
didn’t stop the row he’d have them dealt with. Just
then Pip came back with the .stew, and passed some
remarks about the flies. Next night at tea-time,
while Red was belting into the syrup, I went down
lo score a drop more tea, and coming back walked
up with Pip Dailey who was taking the odorous
stew to his wazza. While Pip was placing his dixie
on the ground my eye caught something wrong with
the syrup tin on our ledge. It was moving, slowly,
slowly. It raised itself, and began to move towards
Pip’s side of the partition! As soon as it passed the
boundary it slowly descended into the water tin.
ONCE BIT, TWICE SHY.
AGENT : “Then we’ll consider it settled.”
ACTOR: “But — ,er —what about the con-
tract?”
AGENT : “Oh, that’s all right. A verbal con-
tract ’ll do.”
ACTOR: “ Laddie, listen. The last time I
had a verbal contract I drew a verbal
salary!”
]' or about 12 seconds I was too flabbergasted to
move. At last 1 went to tlie tin. A million flies
rose sluggishly up as I put my arm in. Standing
there in water just high enough to lap the lid was
our golden syrup pot. As I drew it out I was
speechless; but I heard Pip talking to the , flies
that suddenly swooped down on his stew. From
the inquiry that followed it was evident that when
the flies smelt Pip’s stew they thought it was time
to quit the syrup. Deliberately moving off the ledge
they descended into the water tin—which they knew
was always near empty by that hour of the day—
and the gentle lap of the -water sufficiently loosened
their sticky foothold to allow them to tget free and
ready for Pip’s stew.

“Quidam” : When I was about eight years
old my father had a cattle-station on the MacKenzie,
back from Rockhampton. This was far inland then ;
in fact, when we went there the railway was only out
to Dingo, but had crawled as far as Blackwater
when we returned. Fever, droughts and bankruptcy
seem to have been the usual lot of the squatter then,
with fat bullocks at 30s. or £2 each on the stations.
Tucker was very rough, and almost every old bush-
man out that way suffered from dyspepsia. One
other circumstance sticks in my mind. There was a
tree growing in or near the brigalow scrubs, and
cattle used to eat the leaves and grow fat on them.
It was then known as the “cattle-tree,” and it was
an acknowledged fact in the district that whereas
horses were doomed if the grass supply ran out,
cattle could always weather a drought by retiring
into the scrubs, where they would keep mud-fat on
these leaves. I remember there was one near the
head-station; my brothers and I used to chop
branches off it and feed calves on them, and all round
that tree at night you could see the white waist-
coats of hundreds of scrub wallabies trying to take a
little higher bite. They had it pruned as smoothly
as the goats prune a poinciana tree in N.Q. What
tree was it, and would it grow elsewhere? As far
as I can remember, the leaves were slightly boomer-
ang-shaped and dark green, having a varnished
appearance, and when crushed up a sticky, glutinous
juice was set free. Can any Abo.-expert place it ?
* * * *
“Tiaki”: An ancient convention of the
Maori was the kaihaukai. This was a summons to
partake of and admire the stacks of kumara, cabbage,
taro, koko, kereru and other tucker. A certain
amount of potted homo was also on hand for the nobs,
said homo being preserved in his or her own fat and
canned in a taha or gourd. A series of these picnics
ended in Henare’s then usual pastime—battle and
cannibalism. Twenty-five years ago, in the vicinity
of Te Ivaha and other out-of-the-way East Cape
places, the chiefs of the Ngatiporou would ask the
crowd to “haw-a trink.” “How many rum you kot
’ini?” he would inquire of the publican. “Oh, 40
gallons.” “I take-a te rot,” was the answer.
Henare te Poroporo once bought out the whole of
the stock of the Grand Hotel; he also appropriated
a parcel of liquor just landed on the beach. Then
the picnic began. The shouts of some of the gold-
fields days were mere whispers compared with
Henare’s vociferation.
“Bad Australian”: When the Outback
Australian kills a bullock or cow he uses up
a few pounds in the camp-oven or frypan
and dumps the remainder into pickle; con-
sequently he is living the greater part of his
time on salt-junk, and between the junk and
the soda-and-aeid damper
he soon finds himself pro-
specting for a remedy for
indigestion, or worse. The
South American works on
a different plan. He doesn’t
have any harness cask, but
rips the animal into strips
like saddle-straps,
sprinkles the strips
with just enough
pepper and salt to
make them palat-
able, slings them
over a fence or a
sunbeam or any-
thing that comes
handy in an al-
most treeless land,
and in a day or two he
is the possessor of several
sheaves of “eharqui,”
which can be eaten as it
is with a handful of
parched maize or “gal-
leta,” or boiled to make
soup or stew. Flesh dried
in this way loses very
little of its nutritive
qualities, and I have
known men to live and
thrive for weeks on straight
meat of this kind, with
nothing to counteract the
effects of an exclusively
flesh diet except an occa-
sional pot of “yerba
mate,” the South Ameri-
can substitute for tea.
During many years in
South America I have
never seen a cask of corned
beef. A small supply is
kept in some of the pubs,
but it is of Amurrikan ex-
traction and lives in a
“calm.” The gaucho al-
ways uses unleavened
bread, very similar to the
true Australian damper,
and when he cooks freshly-
killed meat he sticks a
“sprit” through it and
toasts it before or over the
kind of hot ash-heap that
Billjim uses to bake his
•damper; but I have never
known him to include a “banjo” in his kitchen.
Sydney Zoo has bought a pair of mallee-
fowl from Bellchambers, of Humbug Scrub (S.A.),
who reckons he is the only Australian keeping this
bird alive by active breeding work. For years they
would not breed in captivity ; but now-, by reproduc-
ing their natural scrub surroundings, he has per-
suaded them to abandon race-suicide and go through
the. whole performance of pit-digging, hotbed-filling,
laying, mound-building, and keeping enemies off dur-
ing the two months that the buried half-pound eggs
take to hatch.

■ “Wandi”: One of the first things taught
young Binghi is how to swim. At first mother
takes him out under her arm, or clinging to her
neck, letting him go in shallow water; or she swims
out with him on her back, now and again diving
from under him or shaking him off. By-and-bye she
throws him far out and leaves him struggling as
long as he can keep his head up ; then she plunges
to the rescue. When he is able to swim he is
taught to dive for mussels; then -to locate distant
objects on the surface .while swimming under water;
and to acquire the duck habit of escaping observa-
tion while in water. Diving, in, he swims carefully
under a bunch of weeds, where he remains with just
his nose above the surface, without making ripples
or bubbles. Tie has also to learn to enter and get
ouc of water without leaving tracks behind him.
The art is specially necessary when there is a hostile
person close in the rear. A white man would jump
in and swim away as quickly as possible, leaving a
track on the water that would be visible to the
trained eye for minutes afterwards. If there were
reeds or lilies near the shore the track would remain
for a day or a week. The aborigine usually dives a
long way down or up stream, rarely leaving directly
opposite the spot where he entered ; not infrequently
he gets out on the same side. Water-tracking enters
very much into the children’s games, and they soon
develop cunning. One will swim across a lagoon
leaving a perceptible track on the water, then dive
stealthily back, and, whilst the pursuer swims over,
creep out behind some screening' reeds or rushes.
Others will walk backwards to the water and walk
out of it at another place in the same way. The
expert pursuer detects the ruse by the difference in
the impressions of the toe and heel. Diving from
trees, where there is time for the resultant marks to
disappear from the surface of the water, and climb-
ing out by means of an overhanging limb of another
tree, are also practised, the object being to break
the track and so gain valuable time. The
boy must familiarise himself with the foot-
prints of all living things, and be able
to tell a fresh track from an old one. The
convergence of footprints indicates the position of
a waterhole, and the age of the tracks tells whether
water is still there or has dried up.
* * * *
“McNorth”: Asa most unpleasant place
to live in, and an equally unpleasant spot to die in,
let me nominate the village of Biota (Papua).
15 is situated on a mangrove- and nipa-fringed
tributary of the Ufafa River, and its curse is mos-
quitoes. The winged torments are there in millions.
Food must be eaten under a net, and one cannot
remain in one position for any length of time. The
wretched things keep one’s arms going like flails.
Now and then that little black acrobat, the anopheles
—the disease-carrying mosquito—comes along and
carefully selects a likely spot to inject bacilli of
malaria. The natives are more or less immune, but
whites never become accustomed to the pest. At
Biota there is a gaunt, fever-ridden priest of the
Mission of the Sacred Heart. Ali day long he
flicks himself with a horse-tail swish in the endeavor
to gain a little respite from the flying torture. He
has been there for years, and has suffered like this
all the time. Yet he sticks to what he believes to
be the good work of enlightening our brown relatives.
It is not necessary for a hero to be a soldier.
“F.H.R.” : I saw a bushman covering his
hut with stringy bark t’other day, and learned some-
thing new from him. He had the sheets turned
with the sap towards the sky, and explained that
moisture in the bark soaked down to the sap for days
after rain, thus tending to rot the sheet. But
when the sap side was exposed to the sun it took on
a hard, polished surface that would turn a bullet, let
alone rain or hail.
* * * *
“Sry Fluke” : The wallaby has made him-
self at home in half a dozen of Maoriland’s East
Coast islands. The lava slopes of Rangitoto, the
round volcanic island at the entrance to the Waite-
mata, are all a-hop with the Australian, and on
Kawau Island, llori Kerei's old home, now a week-
end trippist’s resort, he competes for a crust with
the owner’s sheep. Hori Grey introduced the animal
to Kawau back in the ’6o’s. The old man used to
chuckle wickedly over an Exeter Hall yarn concern-
ing him and liis wallabies. The tale went that a
reverend brother, holding forth in a tabernacle,
described in heartrending accents the piteous condi-
tion of the Maoriland heathen, brown and white, and
brought groans of horror from his audience when he
announced, “And most frightful of all, my brethren,
I am informed on the most reliable authority that
an ex-Governor of the colony is living in sin on an
island near Auckland city with a whole harem of
Australian wallabies!” Present narrator will not
brook contradiction, for Hori lierei told him that
tale.

“6 & 8”: Is not “Uloola” (B. 2S/10/M5)
misled by the similarity of the flower, confusing the
variegated with the Scotch thistle? The former is
eagerly eaten by stock, and many a good bullock
has thrown the seven from a bellyful of it —“burst”
is the colloquial way of describing the cause of
death. I have never known stock to touch the
spiky Scotch plant, which is far more uncommon than
the variegated variety.
A SKINNER!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now, skinnin’ rabbits was the,theme;
Bill held the ears of all the throng
As he told of a wond'rous scheme
Of his to make a tally long—
He skited how his matchless power
Peeled ninety bunnies in an hour!
“Bah!” sniffed the ancient of the crowd.
“Up on the run where I was bred
I knew a chap who did us proud—
He skinned an’ threw ’em o’er his head ;
So slick he was —to this I’ll swear—
A skin was always in the air!”
The ancient took the monster scone
The cook had proffered as the prize,
And bit and chewed till all was gone.
Then said: “I tliort that would surprise
Yous blokes!....’Ow did ’e work th’ thing?
’E hep’ one ’angin’ on a string!”
victoria.
RITA SUNYASEE.

The Inky Way.
“T. 0.1.”: The London Observer, now in
ita 124th year, is the oldest Sunday paper
in the world. Its price in 1815 was CM.;
to-day it is 1C pages Id. It was the Ob-
server, started in 1791, that made the first
important development in publishing illus-
trated news. The murder of William
Weare, by John Thurtell and Hunt (the lat-
ter, coming to N. S. Wales for life, lived and
died at Bathurst in a Government billet),
and the Cato-street Conspiracy gave it an
extraordinary chance. It continued to illus-
trate with success until the business was
divided in 1842 by the establishment of the
Illustrated London News.
* * *- *
“D.F.” : Robert L. Murray, who ran the
old-time Tasmanian publication, the Aus-
tral-Asiatic Review (B. 28/10/T5), was a
man of many parts. He was one of Tas-
mania’s first lawyers, and was among those
licensed to plead when a Supreme Court was
set up in Hobart about 1825. He was a
vigorous and forceful writer, but his Re-
view was rather too ambitious for those
times, and did not last long. Murray got a
grant of a good lump of land near Hobart.

Will Dyson lias brought out in London a
new collection of cartoons under the title of
“Conscript ’Em.” The Star in reviewing it
refers to Dyson as “one of the most vigorous
and certainly the most gigantic of our modern
cartoonists,” and it advises those English-
men who think to “circulate this splendid
arsenal of satire and scorn, of argument and
exposure, as widely as possible.”
