“Sam O’Var”: The trouble that Ivan is
giving the Huns is nothing to the anxiety he
brings to the cable sub-editors. There is a
Russian General by the name of Russky,
alias Ruszki. alias Russki. who is busy
putting a head on bull-necked Von
Ilindenburg, and there is a kind of
Government Gazette in Petrograd
known as (he Russky Invalid, which
at intervals bucks up the Bear. Evidently
the Argus has confused the two, or else
thinks that the Russky Invalid, now that
the Turk is sitting up and taking notice, is
the real Sick Man of Europe. Anyway, in
its cable columns on 16/11’15, the Argus
says:—•
The "Russky Invalid” regards the Balkans cam-
paign »ns only "an episode.” “It is not there,” he
remarked, "that the fate of the nations will be
decided.”
The Age quotes the same remarks, but attri-
butes them to “General Russky, in the course
of a statement to press representatives,”
and heads them “General Russky Confident.”
So are the worried subs. —I don’t think.
* * * *
Jack Whear Roberts, an old Hansard
man, writes: —
My versatile friend George Meudell says (B.
18/11/’l5) that as a shorthand writer he was
"crumpled up” by Boh Clark (Bendigo), who
rattled off 200 words a minute. The genial Bob
was certainly a glib talker, but the terror of those
days in point of speed was John Mclntyre. Angus
Mackay, though much slower, was the most trouble-
some man to report by reason of his vocal defects
and his didactic diction. Doubtless for a sustained
effort Deakin is the fastest talker in Australia, but
I question whether he, in his “finest frenzy,” can
clearly and disconnectedly articulate more than 200
words a minute for 10 consecutive minutes. The
trained shorthand writer is not deterred by his
flamboyant flapdoodle, but the shorthand reporter is
often puzzled to extract any sense from the grandilo-
quence.
I vote Edmund Barton the most difficult man to
report accurately—that is, in his quiet, unim-
passioned, semi-conversational mood, as distin-
guished from the oratorical-fireworks mood. Not
only is he rapid but he avoids conventional phrases;
you dare not anticipate the terms in which he will
complete a sentence; you have to keep in close touch
with him lest he crumple you with some unex-
pected, unusual phrase or get you hopeless!}' befogged
with parenthesis within parenthesis. If, in the mood
which I describe, Barton were to talk 200 words a
minute for, say, 10 minutes, lie would have the most
expert Pitmanite tied in a knot. I have a vivid
recollection of a “turn” which I had when he was
Speaker in the N.S.W. Assembly. It was a ruling on
an important point of procedure which Barton had
had a day to consider; he had it all in the “back of
his head”; it was all legal abstruseness, and he
rattled it off without a pause for 20 minutes.
Curiosity prompted us to count the words in Han-
sard, and they worked out an average of 185 per
minute. It is not long since that the then “Chief,”
Chas. Robinson, told me that this “turn” still held
the record.
Of curious “turns” the best that I remember is
one of half an hour in the N.S.W. Upper House, in
which that delightful old bookworm, Charles Camp-
bell, hurled chunks of six different languages at me;
as type was non-existent for two quotations they had
to be cut out in wood by an engraver. My most
amusing experience was associated with Dan
O’Connor. He had prepared for a great oration on
the Stuai’t Land Bill. My “turn” should have been
half an hour; but the Chief, knowing Dan’s abso-
lute ignorance of the subject, his penchant for repe-
tition, and for piles of words “all sound and fury
signifying nothing,” asked me to do the whole
speech. It lasted four hours, and all of it that was
intelligible and to the point was conscientiously
recorded. On the day of publication Dan rushed
into the office in a most irate mood and demanded to
know why his speech had been so “ruthlessly
slaughtered.” lie had turned up a speech of I’arkes’s
of an hour, and had calculated the space which his
speech of four hours should occupy in proportion;
but of course instead of being eight times as much it
was only a trifle over three times. He had forgotten
that he had sent us the MS. of the speech for verifica-
tion of quotations. It was not until this was pro-
duced and he was shown that it would not have
occupied twice the space of Parkes’s speech that lie
was appeased. No one can be harmed now by the
statement that the MS. of Dan’s speech was in the
free, flowing handwriting of W. B. Dailey. Had the
“speech” been published in that form it would have
been a masterpiece of clearness, conciseness, and com-
prehensiveness to Dan’s credit.
‘•s T.S.” : Eighty last week, John VS at-
scn, of the Border Watch, at Mount ham-
bier (S.A. t. Any other Australian news-
paper-proprietor carrying on active personal
business supervision at that age?

Herald, with becoming modesty, printed
(on the leader page) an interview with a
returned professor who said that the three
greatest paper's in the world’ were lin-.
Times, the New York Herald and the > ■
M. Herald! On November 19, Idlo ton
the leader page), one of the three greatest
papers in the world printed this gibberish.
Regarding the franchise provisions in both
measures, which have been the subject of so mu
discussion, Mr. Griffith said that they would be
obtained in both Rills, neither of which would be
passed if the Parliamentary franchise were exeieised.
It would be really unkind to say which
paper did this; hut I may indicate it as the
groat journal which referred the other day
to certain “contortions” of Zangvvill s writ-
ings by the U.S.A. papers. (N.T».—lt scm
still can’t guess it, let me add that it is the
paper in which ships frequently run on to
“unchartered rocks.”) # #
“Sheila”: George D. Meudell (B.
18/11/’l5) thinks Deakin the speediest
speaker ho over followed. I have taken
Deakin, but did not find him as fast as the
late Engineer-in-Chief of the Melbourne
Board of Works, William Thwaites. _ The
old man would sit, martyr-like, listening to
the opposition of members to a . much-needed
proposal of his, and then, having the right
of reply, would rise and let it go. On
such occasions recording Thwaites was like
erasing Woorak. _
THE DIVINE AFFLATUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A doleful, atrabilious bard
Was William Henry Jones,
Who ground out verses by the yard
All “groans,” “alones” and “moans” ;
He’d sigh of cemeteries drear,
And blasted hopes, and wasted beer,
Till all was blue; or fleer and sneer
In sour, afflictive tones.
But, strange to say, each dismal rhyme
Found favor ev’rywliere;
Admirers called them “grand,” “sublime,”
And Jones a genius rare.
So eagerly they sought his stuff
He couldn’t write it fast enough,
And rival poets got the huff,
And, bard-like, tore their hair.
On Mrs. Jones this state of things
At last began to pall;
She cherished strange imaginings,
And begged Jones to tell “all!”
And “as he quarried out each slab
Of gloom it gave his wife a stab
To find her lord so glum and drab—
Her gladness turned to gall.
Forthwith she hired a specialist,
Who snorted, and said: “Hum!”
And sounded Jones, and squeezed his wrist
And epigastrium,
And then declared his gloom arose
From liver. “Bile, I diagnose;
An operation I propose,
Or else he must succumb 1”
They chloroformed the moody man,
And with precision then
They altered his digestive plan,
And sewed him up again.
Galvanic batteries o’ nights
His torpid liver put to rights,
And gave Instead electric' lights
To guide his fervid pen 1
Again at verse did Jones perspire,
But vainly “sigh’d” and “died” ;
He couldn’t twang the mournful lyre
No matter how he tried I
And so the public turned him down,
And editors began to frown,
And beg him not to play the clown—
They read his jokes and cried 1
But though they might delete bis stuff,
They couldn’t stop his pen,
l or unabashed at this rebuff,
Jones started off again.
Now all the dailies print his lays,
His readers smile at ev’ry phrase—
He writes the rhyming ads that praise
Blue Blobs for Bilious Men!
qUKENSLAND.
NORMAN CAMPBELL.
“Bill Bowyang” : A line from my dug-out.
Our battalion (ISth) quickly made a name
for itself. It’s grand to light beside our
boys and the Maorilanders. They are as
full of punch as a swarm of Hayed devils.
I saw M.L. artist Booth’s 'grave the other
day. It is marked with a wooden cross.
Yesterday I heard that Bat McGill, _ the
navvy poet, is in Gallipoli, but put little
faith in the rumor, as he was in Flanders
some months back. An Inky Way battalion
could easily be formed here. Every second
Cornstalk you meet has some story he “must
tell The Bulletin.” There’s a busy time
ahead for the sub-editors.
PRESSURE OF OTHER MATTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A copious writer for the press,
A freelance flaneur fine am I.
What splendid things those words express—
A scribe who has no master nigh ;
A genius who with his pen
Dissects the souls of living men!
’Tis said my style is quite unique.
For verse I have a happy turn;
My prose displays a caustic streak;
I write the words that breathe and burn.
I'm printed often in the dents
Between display advertisements!
Sometimes I half a column get,
Sometimes a poem from my pen
Five inches long is really set,
And printed, too; but this is when
The tame photographer has grace
To leave the scribe a little space.
More oft the prose that is my pride,
The clever verse, the epigrams,
For process prints are cast aside,
For football crowds, and pens of rams,
And dames that to a wedding flocked.
Behold how genius is blocked 1
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

foundation fox’ future health and happiness. Ordinary
cow’s milk is totally unsuitable as a food for young
infants; it is acid, contains indigestible curd and is
frequently swarming with dangeroxis germs, especially
in summer time. FarinaceousFobdsmustnotbegiven,as

POLITICAL
POINTS
ON THE HOLY MISSION OP COLD FEET.
[Fob The Bulletin.]
The chairman declared that conscription was a
serious menace to trade-unionists. There was one
objection which all members shared with intense
fervor —namely, their belief in the sanctity of
human life.- — Cable.
The sanctity of human life
Is such we dare not strike a blow
Or march to fields of doubtful strife
Against the crimson-handed foe.
Our flag of peace still stands unfurled,
Conscription earns our blackest curse;
Though murder, redden half the world
To slay the slayer would be worse.
We take this high and holy stand
Against all threats of march and drill,
While calm and leisure are , on hand
We are not going out to kill.
The women wail, the children cry,
Loud ruin works through smoke and flame—
Well, let the tortured wretches die,
The friends of peace are not to blame 1
The battle surges to our door,
The nation’s needs grow more acute.
Our various failures' we deplore,
But still we. must decline to shoot.
Our high religious sense remains—
Let who will pay the painful price—
No blood the snowy emblem stains
On our loud shield of cowardice!
n.s.w. GEORGE STREET.
Some inquisitive member of the Vic.
Assembly lately asked the value of goods
bought outside the Commonwealth by the
Treasurer’s department last year, and found
it was £15,325, against £10,000 spent on
Australian goods. For this defiance of Par-
liamentary instructions the usual excuse
was offered —the goods weren’t obtainable in
Australia, though among them were such
articles as paper, ink, timber, etc. What
this country can’t produce, apparently, is a
patriotic Government. When it can, the
other things will follow all right.
SO IRRITATING.
GLADYS: “ Muriel had a horrible experience
this morning .”
GARRY: “Indeed! In what way?”
GLADYS : “She was rescued from drowning
by another girl.”
“No economies of the individual are of
any value unless they are compensated for
by corresponding expenditure by the Gov-
ernment,” says the amazing Holman. So,
unless you have a spendthrift Government, it
won’t do you any good to save up for
thunderstorms, earthquakes and other acci-
dents ; and to cut down the butcher’s bills
in order that you may presently give your
child something extra in education, or your-
self a shelter in old age, is all folly and
futility. What an economist to have at the
Treasury!
“Gratuity’s” refieetion: —
The N. S. Wales Government generously votes
large sums of money to purchase annuities for the
widows of J. L. Treflg and J. R. Dacey, who died
while holding Ministerial office. This action is in
startling contrast to the usual Government attitude
towards its servants. There are hundreds of cases
of N.S.W. public servants who have been denied
compensation after years aud years of service—worse
than denied, for in dozens of cases where aged offi-
cers have established a claim at law to the con-
sideration of the Government, the “Crown” has
chivvied them from Supreme Court to Full Court,
from Full Court to High Court, and from High
Court to Privy Council. Remembering dozens of
cases where the Government has thus exhausted the
resources of devoted servants, it is no wonder that
the Public Service should resent the special treat-
ment accorded in these two cases. Apart from any-
thing else, if a Cabinet Minister drawing £l5OO a
year deserves Parliamentary assistance in providing
for his dependents, how can the Government expect
the ill-paid rank and file to do without it?
N. S. Wales Works Department made an-
other champion under-estimate in reckoning
that the cost of the Litligow sewerage system
would be £20,000. When the dawdling was
all paid for the bill mounted up to £53,000;
and instead of the township paying £1062
per annum and working itself out of debt in
00 years, it will have to pay £2170 every
year and wait 100 years before the mort-
gage is lifted.
The erection of Hobart’s new railway
station lias been postponed on account of
early closing at the pawnshop. Of course
the existing kennel ought to have been burnt
down long ago; but imagine the demented
condition of a country which knows only
one way of getting the cash to put up a
place to get into a train from! The pre-
sent station was secured by a loan which
has never been paid off, and now the Speck
has to cadge some more to put things wrong
again.
William is shifting the world. At the
annual conference of Victorian benevolent
societies the other day these two really
epoch-marking things happened: (1) The
president, Mrs. George Henderson, officially
reported an urgent need of practical meas-
ures to keep the Australian infant alive in
greater numbers. (2) A resolution was
passed urging the Government to start a
foundling hospital or subsidise similar coiv
cerns, and in either case to “recognise the
existence of a class of irresponsible mothers
by providing that children be received into
such institutions without their mothers and
without questions.” If the benevolent ladies
will now do the necessary Minister-pestering,
instead of sitting comfortably back on their
excellent resolution, something may yet be
done by the State to save an army corps
that will otherwise be buried in the dead of
night in back-yards occupied by “kind
ladies.”
From the annual report of the President
of the N. S. Wales State Children’s Relief
Board
One point to which he desired to give prominence
was the continuance of the grave danger to infant
life owing l to the insertion of advertisements in the
daily press by persons offering children for adop-
tion, and by others expressing their willingness to
take such infants. He could not too strongly
emphasise the necessity of taking prompt measures
to remedy this defect of the Act.
This matter is, of course, a scandal, aud
the “respectable dailies” deserve all that
may come to them through a tightening-up
of the law. All the same, if the “kind
motherly person” is shut out of the daily
paper, the Law may merely multiply
another harpy, the abortionist, unless it
grasps the social nettle by founding State
institutions where the “unwanted” infant
may be left and no questions asked. The
healthy infant who isn’t wanted is liable to
be wanted very badly in the time that is
coming.
From a responsible Tasmanian :
It becomes clearer than ever that this policy of
“Hush!” is being used to save the incompetents (and
maybe worse) of the Defence Department. The other
day Whitsitt made charges in the Assembly here
about the treatment of returned soldiers. Pearce
forthwith sent Senator Jim Long to Hobart to hold
an inquiry. Long came down, held a three-hours’
Bitting (from which the press and the public were
excluded), and went back again, observing that he
would make a report to the Defence Department
some old time. Either Wliitsitt's charges were not
worth bothering about or they deserved a proper
inquiry, According to Whitsitt himself the whole
thing was a farce; Long just heard the statements,
not given on oath, of the people who chose to come
along, and closed the thing down as soon as he
could, refusing to hear a word about anything but
the specific cases Whitsitt had brought out in
Parliament on November 17. Since then Whitsitt
claims to have collected a batch of other eases in
which the hungry families of soldiers have had to
wait months for their pay, and where men who have
enlisted have not got a fair deal. But these he
couldn’t go into. Long’s instructions appear to hive
been, “Go into nothing that you can find any excuse
for not going into, and don’t go into anything a
step farther than they drive you.”
N.S.W. Premier Holman has a pile
of important business to attend to these
strenuous times. For example, the other
night he was compelled to complain that
John Haynes had “made offensive refer-
ences” to two Government supporters by
calling them “Labor members.” Said Hol-
man : “In Parliament the parties are known
as Ministerialists and Oppositionists, and it
is out of order to describe members as of
a certain party.” The Speaker promised
to “keep the matter in mind, and if neces-
sary rectify it.” So it’s “offensive” to be
called a Labor member now. is it? Well,
perhaps it is. But it wasn’t before Holman
was the Labor party’s leader.
OBVIOUS TACTICS.
“The Turks are as thick as peas! What
shall we do?”
“Shell them , you idiot, shell them!”
Sydney Telegraph lately focussed its best
intelligence on the position caused by the
war in Britain, and saw it this way :
The boom in the munition-making trades and in
all trades connected with the equipment and trans-
port of troops has enormously increased the incomes
of certain sections. By the re-spending of this
money on an extravagant scale other businesses are
boomed and wages and profits forced up. But the
money is not earned in the economic sense of the
word. Real wealth production, instead of being
increased, suffers a serious decrease.
Then the Telegraph solemnly warned Aus-
tralia that it was drifting the same way,
and told it how to save itself. By a High
Tariff that would encourage real wealth
production, of course ? Oh, no! By “eco-
nomy”—that is to say, by putting your
hands in your pockets and keeping them
there. How that is going to produce any-
thing “in the economic sense of the word”
naturally wasn’t explained.
Peacock has intimated that next
session sport will be specially
taxed to raise revenue in Victoria,
but his Government still refuses to
legalise the totalisator. Opportuni-
ties for raising revenue from sport,
apart from racing, are not promis-
ing. Football was very sick last
season, and is likely to be sicker
this, inter-State cricket is at a
standstill, and ping-pong, poker,
croquet and badminton do not seem
to be weighed down with financial
possibilities. Of course, a surcharge
on race tickets would realise a fair
sum, but at the expense of patriotic
funds, to which most big clubs are
giving all their profits as long as
the war lasts. Certainly such a
tax would not realise anything like
the sum that could be raised by the
State from the tote. It is a fat-
headed idea to tax horse-racing,
which we are constantly told is a
noble sport “marred by the objec-
tionable element of gambling,”
while treating the said element as sacrosanct.
The attitude of the wowser, from whom in
this matter Peacock takes his cue, is as illogi-
cal as anything could be. While he demands
that other forms of alleged “vice”—beer,
wine, spirits and narcotics —shall be taxed
heavily, he lifts his hands in horror at the
bare suggestion of a tax on gambling.
From where the p.n. grows among the
kowhai and raupo:—
There would not be many howls in Maoriland if
the Government decided in favor of conscription.
The call for compulsory service is growing each week,
and is particularly strong in Dunedin, because the
boys of the oatmeal breed are very low
down in the district recruiting figures. Wel-
lington district is providing half the recruits,
and there the opinion against one area being
called upon to make up for the slouchi*
ness of another is accumulating. Massey, the half
Premier, refuses to commit himself until the result
of National Registration is known, but is filled with
a great windiness about what he will do “if neces-
sary” ; from which it appears he is ready to hitch his
waggon to anything.
The Leather Trades Review has printed
“the remarks of an entirely disinterested
person in regard to the splendid service
given by Australian boots.” Here is one ot
the remarks:—
The boots supplied by the Commonwealth arc a
revelation.... Some idea of the quality of the Aus-
tralian soldiers’ boots may be gathered from the
fact that the English “Tommies” only too glad
to exchange their boots, which cost 17s. 3d., for the
Australian article, the contract price for which was
11s. 3d.
Britain, of course, has “banged, barred and
bolted the door” on tariff reform; and the
young man who thus declared England s
policy has gone off to the Front. Yet it is
possible that some of the old fellows left
behind may get a crowbar at the bolts aud
bars, after all. Six bob a pair extra on
each pair of boots is a frantically high price
to pay even for the luxury of Frectrade.
As soon as the intention of certain Vic-
torian legislators regarding the introduction
of a Totalisator Bill was made public, re-
presentatives of three or four churches
hastened to start an opposition campaign,
and the same is now being conducted with
feverish energy. According to the daily
press, a deputation has been arranged to
wait on the Premier for the purpose of urg-
ing him to come to the assistance of its pal,
the bookmaker. The Pencillers’ Association
is helping Archbishop Clarke in bis good
work, the Fielders’ (undenomina-
tional) is lending its immoral support to
the Presbyterians, while the Bagmen’s
League (more or less affiliated with the
Council of Churches) is doing its little bit
to show Methodism how the anti-tote trick
should be worked so as to look really
righteous.
The most dreadful facts disclosed in
Winston Churchill’s account of his steward-
ship were (1) that, when Belgium was
pleading .for help to defend Antwerp, he
(Churchill) “took the responsibility of send-
ing the naval brigade, despite its want of
training, because the situation was des-
perate, and they were the only men avail-
ahle”; and (2) that on November 30, 1914,
he “offered Kitchener facilities for trans-
porting 40,000 men, but was informed that
no army teas available.” That was the
nation which had accepted Germany’s chal-
lenge and was fighting for its life against
trained millions! And when it is all over,
how is Derby’s scheme, followed in Aus-
tralia, going to improve matters? That
plan will only produce an army during a
war, whereas it is obvious that the need is
for an army ready to throw instantly
against any person who may begin trouble
in the future.
FREEZING.
“Bill, do yer ever find readin' the war news
gives yer cold feet?"
Something will have to be clone to sup-
press these people who are spoiling our shib-
boleths. Not only are shells being made
in Australia, but an engineering firm at
Castlemaine (Vic.) is now turning out the
plant to make them with.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Despite war, mourning, drought, high cost
ot' living, and the lamentations at the closed door
of Uncle’s pawnshop, entries for important summer
handicaps in Sydney show a considerable increase,
Viiliers Stakes enticed 78 against 65 in 1914 :
Summer Cup, 04 against 39, and Carrington Stakes,
70 against 63. Tatt.’s Cup shows the only drop—4 2
against last year’s entry of 48.
A. J. Noud. dead at Brisbane, was one of
the most widely known Queensland trainers. Among
other events lie won Q. Derbies of 1913 (Signal Lamp)
and 1914 (Fcgbell), Brisbane Cups (llaidee and
FightawavA and three Queensland Cups (Drake, Signal
Lamp and Fra sea). lie knew enough about the
business to get a job as trainer of the winged steeds
in Peter's chariot or the prads of Revelation that
are to have hair like women and tails like bald-
headed scorpions.
“Wanderer” : Things had not been going
too well with him, but lie had to get to Tamworth
meeting somehow, and Tamworth was a long way off.
So lie stood on the railway station and pondered, and
was pondering hard when a Salvationist came and
stood near-by. Good enough ! Lord knows what sort
of tale ho told, but lie borrowed the cap of glory
for ’arf a mo' in exchange for his own cady, and as
the train pulled up got to work —quick. Sighting
a brace of Chows in a compartment lie pushed his
head in. “Tickets, please!” Both were for Tam-
worth. All-li’. Later on two Chows were having
a violent argument with Red Tape. When he got to
Tamworth he borrowed a quid, hacked a couple of
winners, and felt that things were coming right at
last. A man has to get round somehow.
A peculiar incident occurred at Menangle
Park (N.S.W.) race meeting on November 25,
owing to the starter sending away the field in the
Flying Welter, a six-furlong event, from the seven-
furlong- post. When the horses returned to scale
every owner but the winner’s entered a protest, and
the stewards ordered another rare with -all hots
to stand. It was a triumphant entry that the winner
of the first flutter made when it again landed home
with its nose well in front and its tail waving three
cheers.
* * * *
“481” : While in camp in Ma’adi, my mate,
Boh llodd, and myself were order <1 to patrol the
rifle range. We had for our section a flat, sandy
stretch behind a long, low hill. Now, Boh was
always under the impression that his chestnut was
faster than my brown, an opinion from whhh l
differed. We argued the matter until Bob
offered to race me to an old stone hut
a quarter-mile away, for 50 piastres aside. We
got away to a good start, and after a very close
finish I reckoned 1 had just about won. Bob thought
otherwise. We rode slowly back, arguing the matter,
and as we approached the hut Bob gasped suddenly:
“Holy wars! Here’s the Adjutant.” Out from the
hut he came and cantered up to us. “What are you
two men doing- here?” he snapped. “Patrolling the
rifle range, sir.” replied Bob. “Patrolling he
damned,” roared his Nibs; “when were you ever
taught to patrol at full gallop?” Then, turning to
me: “What were you racing along like that for?”
“For 50 piastres, sir,” I said, trying him on a new
tack. “Oh. that’s it, is it? Very well. I’ll send
for you both after lunch. Carry on now, and don’t
he so damned enthusiastic in your patrol work.” He
then rode a wav, turning, when about 30 yards off, to
call out, “The brown horse just won.” AA'e were
never sent for and T pocketed the piastres.
* # * *
Desert Gold. T. IT. Lowry’s brilliant All
Black —Aurarius filly put another Maori land record
against her name at Riccarton the other day by
bolting with the Oaks in 2min. 35 2-ssee. The
previous best was Martine’s 2min. 36 4-ssec. in
1910. Desert Gold is proving herself a wonderful
speed horse. Last season, as a two-year-old, she
started 12 times, got six firsts, was not once un-
placed, and bagged £3665 in stakes, which is the
most ever collected in Loan Land by a nag of that
age. This season as a three-year-old she has, within
four weeks, started five times and scored five
firsts. She won the Hawke* Bay Guineas in a cantor,
the Wellington Champion Plate, the M.L. Derbv in
record time, the M.L. Oaks in ditto, and the Stead
Memorial Gpld Cup, beating AVarstep at weight-for-
age.
“.Twosp”: People who feed gold to the
tote have been wondering who pays the war tax
which Ward of Maoriland has imposed on dividends.
After a great -de il of pen-chewing-nnd-'learned dis-
mission it has been decided that the backer of a
loser contributes nothing, because his money is irre-
vocably gone, and onlv the dividend-catcher sub-
scribes. But actually, of course, the position is
that the winner pays the tax with the loser's money.
The winner doesn't pay at all, but) is merely a collector.
After you’ve thought that out get on to this: At
the Canterbury J. C. gailops and the Metropolitan
Cluli’s trots the total tote investments were £302,358,
which easily beat the previous year’s £280,8254.
Out of this figure the State collared as its ordinary
rake-off £7558 155., and for its war tax on dividends
£6BlB 15s. So that the punters had £13,377 10s.
clipped off their collective weekly wages. A smile
here from Mr. Isaac Odds. At the same time the
owners, who grabbed £28.100 in stakes, of which
the punter provided £22.678 10s. through the club’s
percentage, handed over to the war funds £ 291. This
shows us what a fine game 'racing is for the punter
and also for the gentleman who can lay the field
without depriving himself of any percentages or war
taxes.

“Moana”: It’s not often that a stable
goes the whole bank account in a big handicap with
a rank outsider and scoops the pool, but the crowd
behind Sweet/I’ipperarv did it in the Welcome Stakes
at Riccarton (M.L.) the other week. This filly hails
from Hawke* Bay, and consequently little was known
about her in the City of the Plains; but the stable
knew what she could do and staked a pile of cash
on her c-hance. The boy who rode her, C. Oarmont.
threw every penny he had on to the tote. In spite
of this the prad went out as only eighth favorite.
The jockey took her well to. the front at the start
and stayed thef-e. The stable must have collected
nearly all the cash in the machine.

“F.II.”: Another argument for the tote.
At Epsom (A’ic.) on November 20 the country horse
Moidart won the Second Division Handicap. Moidart.
was “nominally quoted at 50 to 4 in the ring.”
which is to say that nobody backed the nag. But
where the tote is in action no horse ever goes out
without a few supporters. The racing public is wise
to the fact that the tote does something quite extra-
ordinary in divvy-paying-; lienee there is always a
section willing to subscribe a bob or two towards a
syndicate ticket on even the most shaggy outsider.
“F.II.”: A horse which was left on the
board at a recent Williamstown meeting was not
started. In a par. (15. 18/11, 'I-3) I remarked that
‘‘the books bagged a nice bit.” Whereupon
“H.W.IV’ wrote thus in his haste (15. 25/11/’l5) :
“Was ‘F.II.’ serious when he referred to punters
losing their money ? If ‘F.II.’ doesn’t know lie ought
to that if tlie horse a man backs dots not start the
money is returned.” My critic's testimony will never
be supported by that of the more ripely-experienced
sporting fraternity. If I back a horse at a stated
price, the bookmaker simply bets that my nag won't
win. The animal may get paralysis before the race
(six cheers from Isaac), or break his neck on a piece
of banana-peel when coming out of the birdcage (yells
of joy from Tim O’Solomon) ; but that won’t affect
my contract with the odds-layer, except that the
contract is at once determined to my detriment.
There are, however, two conditions under which the
bookmaker has to refund on a scratched horse: (1)
If the bet is made at starting-price, since there
c-an’t be a s.p. without a start; and (2) if it can
lie shown that the horse backed at a stated price was
actually' scratched prior to the bet having been
made.

After McGoorty had operated on Mick
King at Sydney Stadium on Saturday for the tenth
time of smiting, the police inspector held up his
hand; then Arthur Scott, the referee, leaped into
fhe line of fire and the towel fluttered gently to
the floor. Mick was done to a turn; his hands
were down ; his wits had been knocked a mile or so
away by that last swinging hit he took on the point.
It was a painfully one-sided affair. King is, of
course, an immeasurably better boxer than McGoorty,
but. as his hits are never more dangerous than those
of a housemaid armed with a feather-duster,
McGoorty simply treated him with indifference. And
the punches that Mick got in return for his mos-
quito-shifters were hard and direful and many, and
kept him on the run. The man who cannot exchange
smites with McGoorty is merely turning himself
into cold meat by getting into the ring in his
company.
EDDIE McGOORTY chews gam.
Another sorry hiding was distributed to
the ancient Ilughie Mehegan when Frank O’Connor
took off his shirt at Melbourne Stadium on Saturday
night. As early as the second skirmish O’Connor’s
right began to lob home regularly, Mehegan’s
smother being as effective as a mosquito-net would
he against whales. The unpleasant performance
continued until the seventh, when the has-been, bat-
tered to a standstill, dropped on the stroke of the
gong and a courageous second heaved in the towel.
When ilughie realised that the flag he usually nails
to the mast had been dipped, heavy blue smoke
curled up from his corner and the air was laden
with the smell of burning sulphur. O’Connor looks
as if he will have his pockets bulging with cham-
pionships before he is much older.
Mick Paul on signs of the times: Thoug'i
present-day champions seem to prefer to look for
fight in the peace-loving U.S.A., some of the old-
timers are off to show the Turk that they still have
a punch about the premises. Jim Barr, of Sydney,
for one, is training for the real thing. His last go
was against Casey at the old National (Sydney) 10
years ago. He had previously weathered two 20-
round disturbances with Boh Turner, then light-
weight champion and at his best. James lost the
first on points, while the second was called a draw
by the referee and a darned shame by some of the
crowd.

“Nyreir’ : When Jack Lester came to
Australia in 1911, under the wing of Tahmmy Burns,
he, was heralded as the White Hope. He proved a
black frost. He was a flash fellow, and, had a mania
for motor-cars. He was also a whale on breaking
nails with his teeth and straightening out horse-
shoes. A “prominent person” who hoard of this
made arrangements for Lester to give an exhibition
in Sydney, to which several other important person-
ages were invited. A well-known pressman bought
the horseshoe. Lester arrived, and, after being-
introduced, proceeded to get busy. The veins
stood out in his forehead like rope, and he
turned the color of an incandescent lobster. Pres-
ently he let loose a string of hot-cross prayers, where-
upon two or three visitors left the room. The pug
had a rest and then renewed the tug-of-war. Foiled
again, he prayed fervently, whereupon the remainder
of the visitors fled. Said the pressman, “Say, Jack,
you’re not as strong as a goosethat’s only a thin
shoe.’’ “Don’t know what’s the matter with me
to-day,” replied Lester. “I do,” replied the scrib-
bler; “you’ve been trying to straighten out a shoe
made of steel.” “Hell!” yapped Lester, as he
bounced out of the room furiously. There was
“nothing- doing” for the photographer who was
engaged to take Johnny in his feat-of-strength act
that day.
»- * * *
Charleville to Sydney pigeon race (600
miles) this year was most disastrous. Altogether
i 00 birds were freed, and of these only eight reached
the lofts, and only one within schedule race-time.
I lie loss is chiefly due to the foolishness of holding
the event while bush fires obscured everything with
smoke and haze. Strong wtsterly winds, which blew
birds into strange surroundings, did the rest.
“D.”: When the Rev. F. F. Waddy’s team
toured Ceylon two years ago they had several funny
experiences. In the second match against the Cey-
lonese XI, Armath, the latter’s best batsman, had
only scored a few runs when, going out to a. googlie
from “Biscuit” Arnott, ho missed it and was so
easily stumped by Ted Long that the latter simply
threw up the ball without appealing. The batsman
scrambled back to liis crease and stood there, where-
upon Long appealed. “Not out,” said the umpire
(a big tea-broker). The batsman continued, made
59, and won the match for his side. When button-
holed by Long subsequently the umpire admitted that
the batsman was out when the bails were knocked
off. “But when you appealed his foot was behind
the c-rease.” Teddy did not say his prayers that
night. That umpire was “off” and there were
several applicants for the job. One fellow declared
that he knew all points of the game and had acted
in all the best matches. “But,” lie said, “there is
one thing 1 must tell you—l never no-ball a man
when he makes: his first break; I only warn him.”
“Is that so?” said Long (Arnott tells the yarn).
“Well, what would you do if the bowler howled a
batsman with his first no-ball ?” The umpire scratched
his head, and replied, “I’m damned if I thought of
that.’ By the way, it took Teddy Long three weeks
to make four runs during that Ceylon tour. He is
now in the ranks, and scoring much better.

“Coot’ : In ono of Iho matches W. G.
Grace played against the great r diversities, he hap-
pened to fail badly in each innings. In the evening
lie was the guest of one of the dons, who invited the
cricketer to attend service in the University chapel.
Bad did so, and, as it happened, the concluding
hymn was No. 28 in “Hymns Ancient and Modern.”
The great stress that the choir laid on the lino, “The
scanty triumphs grace hath won,” made the irrever-
ent undergrads grin, and even the great batsman
forced a smile. That’s the yarn, anyhow, and if
it isn’t a fact, the following is. Upon W.C.'s visit
to Australia with Lord Sheffield’s XI, Melbourne
youths disapproved of several of his actions in the
field, and showed their disapprobation by greeting
the somewhat pot-bellied champion . with cries of
“Watery Guts,” thus playing upon the champion’s
initials as well as his corpulency.
It looks as if all the good wicket-keepers
have gone to-(lie war. “Sundries” reached double
figures in each first-grade match played in Sydney
on Saturday last. ’Varsity gave Red fern 37 (second-
top score), and Mr. S. ran up in other matches
totals of 33, 30 and 26.

“Billy” Hart, ihe Parramatta bird, is off
io join oilier Australians in the aerial business
somewhere in the war-zone. Hart some time ago
offered his services to the Defence Department to
inaugurate an aerial flotilla; but the Department
had its hands full, and the offer lapsed. Hart is a
limb of a family which lias had a footing in the
old town further hack than the oldest inhabitant
can remember, and still keeps its signboard up in
the timber business.

* * * *
Harry Green, of N. S. Wales, holder of
many amateur sculling championships, has joined
Hie artillery. His record includes Australian Singles
in 3912, and N.S.W. Singles in 1912 and 1914; a
seat in N.S.W. champion four-oared boat of 1912
and 1914, and inter-State eights of 1913 and 1914.
Last season, with Sydney Enterprise Club, Green
won the singles and stroked the fours and eights in
the State championships.
A strong nor’-easter last Saturday was
responsible for a surprise in the general handicap
of the Sydney Flying Squadron, the hitherto-uns.ic-
cessful Rosetta landing the double. Accidents” ppt
some of the best-known boats out of the race, while
several were disqualified. Despite the recent police
raid on the sailing-race bookies, the follow-the-boats
steamers were crowded as usual.
Fish sports are recording the presence in
N. S. Wales waters of large numbers of a giant
species of “toadie,” hitherto seldom met with.
Experts identify this toad-fish as a nuisance common
only in Japanese waters. It’s following the trade
flag presumably.
-* * * *
“Moana” : McLoughlin, Uncle Sam’s red-
haired tennis hurricane, has had two doses of un-
diluted disaster lately. First of all, after smiting
his way to the final, he was outed by the new
youngster Johnston in the U.S.A. championship.
Then he got through to the final in the big Newport
Invitation Tournament, only to be blown away by
Williams. Considering that the Yankee crowd made
a god of Mac after the last Davis Clip struggle and
cold-leaded Williams for having lost the utensil, the
latter is probably feeling particularly good at pre-
sent. McLoughlin is reported to be feeling the,
reverse, for his countrymen, as usual when a cham-
pion goes down, are chewing their cigars dubiously
and are beginning to remark: “Was this guy ever a
gol-derned champion, anyhow?”
“Cleido” : W. Robinson, who says lie will
run any man living from 100yds. to 800yds., gave a
further taste of liis quality the other evening at
Flemington (Vie.). In a heat of the 85yds. sprint he
started off the 4yds. mark and conveyed himself to
the tape in 81- lOscc.—exactly even-time. The track
is not a particularly fast ono, and as the season is
quite young he will probably show something special
before it terminates, lie has any number of winning
performances from 75yds. to ' 880yds. (including
Stawell Gift, 1914) io his credit in the best of
company, is a non-smoker and a close follower of the
King. He never dopes immediately before an im-
portant run, as so many topnoteliers do. Writer
remembers a prominent sprinter absorbing nearly a
pint of whisky in warming up for a. final. Veteran
Postle, who is a sort of trial horse these days,
wants to debate the question over 75yds. to 120yds.
with Robinson, and, conscription and accompanying
Urcumstances permitting, be will be given the
chance.
* * * *
.1. .T. IVoIs he is in the limelight of Sydney
amateur athletics this season. Recently the Botany
athlete won four club championships; and at the
Labor sports on Saturday showed the soles of his
feet to the field in the 440yds. handicap. From the
10yds. mark he reached the terminus in 53see.
A school with a mighty athletic record
is Marist Brothers’, Christchurch (M.L.). Its Rugby
team has annexed the North Canterbury Schools’
Championship for the sixth year in succession, and
lias done the same trick 11 times since 1900. Last
season it played nine matches, and accumulated the
staggering tally of 437 points to its opponents’
three. Needless to say, it was undefeated. In the
last three seasons it has run up 952 points against
six, and has bagged on an average 43 points per
game without losing a match. In one game it helped
itself to 100 points to nil. The school also holds
the cricket banner and the Wood Shield for athletics,
and in 1912 and 1913 it provided the Canterbury
swimming champion.

In a recent raid on some two-up premises
in Sydney, one of the 33 men arrested was a juvenile
of 72. Hope was still doing its eternal spring in
his tough old heart.
The “find” of Sydney swimming season
is A. Ifuie, a youngster from Manly. A week ago
be sprinted over 100yds. in 58 4-ssec., and on Satur-
day, from scratch, finished third over 150yds. in
Imin. 39sec. Young Huie gets his speed from an
imitation of the famous kick of Duke Ivahanamoku.
Incidentally, the Longworth family has a young bro-
ther about whom big things are prophesied. Dad
says he'll better Billy.
“Will G. Buck”: One of tlie finest beaches
in Australia is at Port Douglas (JST.Q.). It is, and
has been for years, the registered course for the
racing club there and the running track of the local
athletes. Also, it is a splendid speedway for
motor-cars. When the tide is out the sand is as
hard as an ordinary bicycle track. While you’re
trying to think of another as good will somebody
tell me the address of the best and safest surfing
beach in Australia?
“Vic.” : Great sport! Deer have largely
increased in South-East Gippsland (Vic.). Snake
Island, close to the Corner Inlet entrance, and near
Welshpool railway station, is over-run with them.
A week’s sporting trip is arranged by the Vie.
Government Tourist Bureau. Parties up to five will
be allowed from December I this year to May,
1916 —permit of £2 per party—to shoot deer on
this island. Each squad may shoot up to 10 head
of deer, and possess the carcases. So venison is
likely to be on Melbourne tables. Good
sea-fishing for schnapner, ilathead and whiting;
and fine angling in Agnes River, five miles from
Welshpool, where rainbow trout and black fish abound.
“Peri-Winkle”: There is some ill-advised
talk in Vie. about again officially opening the duck-
slaughter season early next year, now that the swamps
and natural haunts of the wild-fowl have filled after
the rains. Last year wild duck was off the menu,
and the authorities might well wait another season
or two to give the birds a chance to marry and mul-
tiply, despite the desires of the “sports,” cartridge-
retailers, and city holiday-makers. The dock is
becoming scarcer every year, and soon the species
will be a rarity. The proposal to restrict the
number of victims a shooter may bag is a wise one,
but it is almost a generation too late. Close seasons
are the true remedy.

“Rag”: The Fremantle-Sydney motoring
record of 28 days I hr. 52min., put up by Birtles
during 1912, with a little Metz car, has been blown
out. A Westralian party, P. W. Armstrong, 11.
Eraser and T. Collins, in a 6-cylinder, 40-h.p
Studebaker, has flogged over the 3000 miles in eight
day,; 23hr. lOmin, averaging 320 miles a day.
The machine, passengers, gear and petrol weighed
24 tons, and petrol consumption was a gallon for 15
miles. That’s good business. Near the top of the
Razorbaek some rubber tubing between engine and
radiator burst, and nearly all the water was lost.
Reluctantly the motorists had to fill the machine’s
guzzle with two bottles of beer and several of
gingcrale.. Naturally, she never went better. Moral:
Ncvef go motoring unarmed.

Tlie financial story of a mucli-discussed
motor-car eo., from the Canadian Monetary Times :
“Ford Company of Canada. —Net earnings of this
company for the present year are estimated at
53,000,000, or 300 per cent, on the capital stock.
Authorities in Detroit, who are in close touch with
the company*, express the opinion that a stock divi-
dend between 600 per cent, and 700 per cent, will
probably be announced this fall. The regular divi-
dend is 40 per cent, cash per annum.
“The company has a record for making distribu-
tions in stock, and its present capital, $1,000,-600,
represents a growth from 5125,000 in 1906, through
the declaration of stock dividends.
“Through agreement with Henry Ford, tlie com-
pany sells Ford products without competition in
Canada and the British colonies, which includes Aus-
tralia, India, British South Africa and Maoriland.
It does not, however, sell in the British Isles.
“In 1912 Ford Motor Company of Canada sold
6300 cars: in 1913 it sold 11,000; in 1914, 16,500;
while this year’s business, notwithstanding adverse
conditions in many parts of its field, will be close
to 25,000 cars.”

A good run was recently taken out of a
B.S.A. big 4:1 at the opening meeting‘of the Pioneer
M. C. Club, held on Brighton Beach, Christchurch
(M.L.). Tn the 20 miles open handicap F. V.
Thomas secured first place and absolute fastest time,
covering the distance on a soft sandy* beach with
four turns in 20 minutes 38 seconds. Oh the same
machine he secured first place in the 10-mile open
side-car race. In Westralia, too, in the 150-mile
T. T. Race, B.S.A. machines secured first and second
places in the side-car event, beating previous records
by over one hour.

John N. Will.vs, who builds the Overland ears, was
the second-largest, private contributor to the War
Loan floated in N’Yarlc. He took up £200,000 worth.
Corporal Adam Dobson, the well-known
Maoriland Soccer rep., was one of the first to be
called out under the new Munitions Act. He was
about to board the troopship with the Bth Reinforce-
ments when he received orders to proceed to
Dunedin, where he is to serve his country at the
Roslvn Mills, as a specialist in woollen stuffs. Of
course, Dobson was disappointed. But he set to work
to cheerfully serve bis country in the prosaic way
in which it had decided that he could serve it best.

A DYING WISH.
Inside a humble cottage stood
A family round a bed,
In sorrow gazing upon
An old man soon to be dead.
His son he callest to his side
And asketh with a sigh,
“One smoke of ‘HAPPY THOUGHTS,’ my son,
For soon I hast to die.”
“GVllan Park” has a great idea: I could stop this
war as easily as a State Premier can ask for
another loan. I’d get every available aeroplane
loaded up with “HAPPY THOUGHTS” Tobacco, to
be dropped over the enemy’s lines with the message
“More where this came front.” The enemy would
desert in thousands —surrender in millions —to get
another helping of the joyous weed. Then we could
easily mop up the few that would be left, or drop
some more tobacco.

This is from a man who can’t keep still: —•
When will some progressive railway man introduce
an express train to Australia ? From Melbourne to
Adelaide is 483 miles, and the alleged express stops
25 times between the two cities. In a run of 17
hours 26 minutes those stoppages amount to 125
minutes, Say two hours five minutes. Surely all
that time need not be wasted. I've done non-stop
runs in France, Britain and Yankeeland, where
water is sucked up while the train is moving and
mailbags are received and delivered. Several such
trains do 60 miles an hour rigid, through. But. the
Adelaide express stops at little towns about the
size of last year's peanuts, and the passenger trying
to sleep is regaled with half the local gossip and all
the local jokes. One does not advocate the sole use
of dumb porters, yet I think they ought to be " told
not to be so beastly noisy in the centre of the
night.

Even the G.-G. pokes borak at us: —•
It will be just as well to crush Germany before
the world is crushed with debt; and it will be a
new world we shall find if we cannot manage to
borrow in pursuance of our usual policy. We must
crush Germany and leave a little money to be got
at the end of the war.
Victoria’s wide-awake and strenuous
Department of Health has put up a record;
the Camberwell Council on April 5, 1911,
sent in an application concerning a nursing
home, and the cyclonic Department has just
intimated that the matter will receive
attention.
“John P.”: It’s not often that anyone is
accused of being 120 years old, but a
wrinkled Maori named Houe Puruwhare. alias
“Johnnie Bull,” who passed over in Tara-
naki (M.L.) the other day, pleaded guilty
to the charge. As usual lie had mislaid his
birth certificate, and everyone who could
make an affidavit regarding the matter be-
ing extremely dead the evidence consisted of
unreliable rumor. The ancient brown man
always declared that he had personal know-
ledge of a famous raid made by the warlike
Waikatos on Taranaki in 1820, and that
he was then a young man “with hair on
his face.” That would make him well over
110 years; hut probably what he knew about
the celebrated exploit was told to him in child-
hood years after it happened. Still, lots of
people who made a hobby of digging into the
old chap’s past believe that he was 120 years
old. Lots of people believe anything.
Chief Justice John Madden, of Melbourne,
must be rather sorry to remember that in
liis Waxcellency capacity he once journeyed
to Europe and back in a German mail boat.
At the time it was only a matter of satirical
remark. Now it is a matter for speculation
as to how a leading State official came to be
captured by a German State-subsidised
steamship company for advertisement pur-
poses.
Colonel “Spo” Smith, who has been men-
tioned in despatches and got the C. 8., took
up the command of the 12th Battalion
when Colonel Clarke was shot down shortly
after the landing on April 25. In civilian
life he had a job in the Commonwealth Bank
at Hobart and put in liis spare time coach-
ing rowing crews when he was not out with
file Field Engineers.
N.S.W, Town and Shire Clerks have as
good a record as any for jumping into
khaki. Arthur Barnes, clerk at Ulmarra.
was killed on April 25 at the landing ; and
amongst others who have gone are Captain
C. A. Cork, Casino; J. J. Hackett, Coota-
mundra; W. R. Haigh, South Grafton; L.
W. Adams, Granville; W. It. Talbot, Yan-
eo; Lieutenant W. .J. Richardson, Bulli;
W. E. O’Halloran, Yalloroi; G. G. Yeitch,
deputy, Granville; A. G. Searle, Woollahra;
and W. G. Devitt, Randwick. In one shire
there was so strong a desire to retain the
services of a valuable officer, who was
married and had children, that three
councillors offered to enlist in his stead. The
Council argued with him ; but apparently he
got the best of the discussion, for not only
did he enlist but he took the three aldermen
with him.
“Ripley”: “Boris” (B. 11/11/’ls), who
quotes chapter and verse to show where the
Kink gets his O’Malleyisms from, reminds
me what a fine, typical Australian this Min-
ister is. On a Tasmanian political cam-
paign, just before the Big Blaze started, In 1
worked off yards of “The Great Illusion.”
He soaked his audience in the Angell’s logic
—but never a word of where he got it from.
Then he went on to suggest that the Aus-
tralian navy should be either “commercial-
ised” or put on the scrapheap. A few months
afterwards the Sydney sinks the Emden!
“Cape Y.”: Away up on the summit of
Australia is a well-to-do Chinese who would
rather be mistaken for an Irishman than be
made a mandarin of the first grade. When-
ever he has worked himself up by recalling
the glorious end of Robert Emmet or wants
to impress some visitor, he directs his
almond-eyed Eileen to “play ‘The Wearing
of the Green,’ and make your poor old
father’s heart bleed.” This provided the
little port with a good stock joke, till a
proud relative of one of the Irish kings came
ashore to buy a few odds and ends and over-
heard what he considered was a foul insult
directed at himself. Forthwith a long
fathom of genuine wild Irishman steeple-
chased over the store counter and made poor
old father’s nose bleed.
At the bidding of local Australian Society
the available handful of Australians and
Maorilanders lately rallied at the Manhattan
Hotel, New York, shook their pocket-books
and gathered several hatfuls of money for
the wounded Kangaroo. Mrs. J. C. William-
son and Mrs. Hugh Mclntosh were on hand,
and Premier Holman’s wife had a speaking
part like P. E. Quinn, who does something
or other for the Holman Government in the
land where the Notes come from.
What a tenderness the hottest Laborites
develop for everything vice-regal when they
get into office! The Tasmanian Labor Gov-
ernment shoved into this year’s estimates
the following new little item: “Maintenance,
upkeep, repairs and supervision, Govern-
ment House grounds, £160.” It was left for
Liberal members to point out that this was
a bad time to give an indirect boost to the
Gov.’s salary, and that if his Ex. was too
poverty-stricken to keep his grounds in
decent order Ministers should say so straight
out. Then the item, blushing to be dis-
covered, hurriedly fled.
“Harrison O.”: Those who talked of Pri-
vate Enterprise being in as good a position
as the Government to provide, vessels in
which to ship the Australian wheat harvest
were promptly silenced by Prime Minister
Hughes. When the wheat-pooling scheme
was decided on Hughes got into touch with
the British Government and pointed out
that unless Australia could realise on its
harvest it might be hqrd put to it to finance
its share of Armageddon. The result was
that 14 transports were at onye released to
carry wheat, and the Admiralty appointed
two of the best-known shippers in the Cold
Country to go into the matter and supply
as many more vessels as can be spared for
the job. One can See the bigwigs of the
Admiralty doing this for Pirate Enterprise,
I don’t think!

BEFORE THE MIRROR.
By “JEANNETTE.”
“Jeannette,” the well-known writer on Beauty Culture and author of “The Book of Beauty,”
will be pleased to help and advise any reader on matters of the toilet. Queries should be accom-
panied by a stamped addressed envelope, and directed to “Jeannette,” c/o “Before the Mirror,”
43 Gray’s Inn Road, London, W.C., England.
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and all of
us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow over the
lives of many of us women, and while, perhaps, a few —the more stoical amongst
us—can avoid worrying over past and prospective events, we may all, with a little
care and foresight, succeed in preventing the results of worry from being notice-
able in our appearance. To these fortunate few this little chat will, however, be
as interesting and instructive as to those who stand in real need of help in toilet
matters, and may be the means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown
method of retaining, or regaining, that beauty of face and figure which is the birth-
right of every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present,
not generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Wrinkles, and How to Remove them. —Worry,
late hours, or the passing of old Father Time
may cause those ugly little lines which so
detract from the freshness and charm of a
woman’s face; but the cure is always the
same. Feed the tissues beneath the skin, and
massage them with a reliable skin food,
which, while nourishing the fatty tissues,
will not coarsen the skin nor grow hairs upon
the face, as so many inferior creams do. To
remove the wrinkles bathe the face with hot
water, wipe it dry; then, while it is still
warm, smear a little mercolised wax over the
whole face, carrying it well down on the neck,
and, with the tips of the lingers, rub it gently
into the pores, working in an upward and
outward direction. Wipe off any superfluous
wax, leaving a little on the skin all night.
In the morning wash off with warm watel-
and Pilenta soap. The use of the wax not
only feeds the skin but removes the dead,
outer cuticle, giving the fresh, young skin
beneath an opportunity to breathe and show
itself.
Beautiful Hair—How to Shampoo. —Whether
the hair is luxuriant and glossy, or thin and
lanky, depends very largely upon the care
bestowed upon it. To keep the.hair in good
condition it should be shampooed once, in
every two or three weeks. Before shampooing
massage a little pure olive oil or almond oil
into the scalp, then mix one teaspoonful of
stallax granules in a cup of hot water, fill a
basin with warm water, into which the ends
of the hair can fall, and shampoo the stallax
mixture into the scalp in the usual way.
Rinse, and dry by fanning with a palm fan,
or in the open air, if the weather permits.
Should the hair be naturally very greasy, the
oil massage should be omitted. Stallax can
be bought at any chemist’s in quarter-pound
sealed packages; it keeps indefinitely, and one
one package contains sufficient for twenty-five
or thirty shampoos.
The Question of Powder.— Powder should
never be used by anyone with a tendency to
wrinkle, for it fills up the lines on the face,
and tends to deepen and accentuate the wrin-
kles. In the place of powder, get about' one
ounce of eleminite from any chemist, and. dis-
solve it in four tablespoons of warm water.
Place it in a bottle, and apply to the skin with
the finger tips, allowing it to dry on the face.
This gives the skin a soft, peach-like bloom,
and prevents that unbecoming, greasy appear-
ance which is the bane of many a woman’s
life.
A Tonic for the Hair. —During the spring and
early summer, the hair generally needs a little
stimulant to its growth, in the form of a
tonic. To make a simple and inexpensive one,
at home, take four ounces of bay rum, and
add to it one ounce of boranium. Shake thor-
oughly, and leave for thirty minutes, then
strain, and add sufficient water to make half
a pint. At night, massage the scalp for ten
minutes, then dab the mixture into the roots
with a soft sponge. Five minutes’ brushing
in the morning will he found beneficial in giv-
ing an added gloss to the hair
To Wave the Hair.—Hot irons, when employed
to wave the hair, pot only make it harsh and
brittle, spoiling color of fair or auburn hair,
but induce a tendency towards greyness. To
give a wave to straight hair, damp it with
silmerine and either plait, tying the ends, or
arrange in waving pins for a short time. A
couple of ounces will last quite a long time.
A Greasy Skin and Blackheads. —Blackheads
are absolutely fatal to beauty, for they give a
coarse, dirty look to the face which no cos-
metics can ever hide. The way to remove
blackheads quickly and effectively has only
recently been discovered. For many years
those who suffered in this way were restricted
in tlieir diet, denied sweets, and had to suffer
other privations. Modern science lias, how-
ever, provided a simple, effective and quick
remedy. Dissolve one stymol tablet in a glass
of hot. water, and when the effervescence has
subsided, dip a small, soft sponge into the
liquid, and bathe the face. Leave for a few
minutes, then dry with a towel, and the black-
heads will come off. For a greasy skin, bathe
the face three times a week with stymol. and
spray with cold water every morning, using a
vulcanite throat spray.
To Reduce the Figure.—With the present
fashions, even more than with the tight skirts,
it is imperative that the woman who wishes to
look smart should be slim. To reduce one's
weight without injuring one's health is no
easy matter, and can only be done by taking
some specific which will counteract the natural
tendency towards putting on flesh. A few
clynol berries, eaten regularly, will speedily
transform the fat, ungainly figure into one of
slim and' graceful proportions, without any in-
convenience whatever, and in a very short
time. Clynol berries are quite pleasant to the
taste, having a marked peppermint flavor.
To Restore Grey Hair to its Original Color. —
Streaks of grey, whether in fair or dark hair,
are always unbecoming, and prove a cruel tell
tale of age. IF is not generally known that
an ounce of tammalite, which can be bought
at any chemist’s, mixed with four ounces ».
bay rum, makes a most satisfactory lotion for
restoring grey hair to its natural color. Apply
the lotion night and morning with a soft
toothbrush, and, after a few days, the hair
will be found to be gradually returning to it-
original shade.
Superfluous Hair—its Cause and Cure. —Super
fluous hair is invariably the result of using
inferior soap or bad face creams. To remove
the hairs, apply a little powdered pheminol
mixed to a paste with cold water and applied
to the skin with a bone knife. The skin
should be dusted with powdered alum before
applying the pheminol, and the paste removed
after being left on for two minutes. This
treatment is recommended not only to remove
the growth temporarily, but to eventually kill
the roots. [2

“Two Seas" wants a State Glioulery De-
partment
The politicians seem to have overlooked the high
cost of dying, especially in the country districts. A
private ghoul when summoned to put the dead out
of sight charges as much as he thinks his client can
pay without having to sell his boots. One can argue
with any other tradesman about his bill, but for
some reason or other an argument with a ghoul is
not considered respectable. In any case, the be-
reaved is not in a fit state of mind to hold his end
up in a barney. -The other day I asked a man in
the business to explain his awful charges, and he'
did ■ it by accusing the deceased of clashing
with the annual picnic of the Amalgamated Society
of Ghouls. It seems to me there ought to be a law
or something to compel these people to rejoice in sec-
tions. Anyhow, what is the matter with making the
waving feathers and the crawling plate-glass show-
room and the polished cedar (silver mounts) a
State or municipal monopoly? Incidentally the
writing of obituary verse and in-memoriam notices
could be turned into a sideline. This would provide
a job for the chaps who were lately composing
pamphlets about the irrigation settlement at Yanco.
One of the men who have scented war
profits in Sydney met with a most unfor-
tunate accident the other day. He wired
to Blayney (N.S.W.), to the mother of a
soldier, in the soldier’s name, urgently re-
questing that £6 should be wired him at
once. It was only his rotten luck that the
man he was supposed to be was at home on
leave. Naturally when the man who should
have been away, but wasn’t, inspected the
telegram from himself, lie asked the police to
clear up the mystery, and a detective
attended to the rest of the story. Hints
dropped at the hearing of the case suggest
that the defrauding of soldiers’ relatives has
grown into a fairly large thing—almost a
iiysiness, in fact. The easy plan to defeat
the rogues is for every soldier to fix up a
simple telegraphic code with his people before
he leaves home. Then when the young
man finds himself pushed for money, and
wires “Parsnips.’’ or something of the sort,
mother knows that. £?> will greatly oblige.

The ADJUSTMENT ELEMENTS
OF THE TYPE 1-14 METER.
01
The regulating or adjustment
elements of a Watthour Meter
control the speed of the meter so
that it will register the load
correctly under widely-varying
load conditions.
The continued accuracy of a
meter depends more upon the
permanence of the damping mag-
nets than upon any other one
thing. The two C-shaped magnets
used in the Type i-t4 Meter are
manufactured from the highest
cr>
grade of steel, specially hardened,
magnetised and aged. The perfection of
the process by which these magnets are
produced has been developed in the com-
mercial' production of ten million magnets.
In ordinary service these magnets are
absol utclv perm ane n t.
KHjOWOTT HOURS
The full load speed of the
meter is regulated by moving
these magnets nearer to or further
from the edge of the moving disc,
O O 7
thus increasing or decreasing the
magnetic drag. This adjustment
is made by a micrometer screw,
which gives a fine regulation with
a wide range of adjustment.
The friction compensation or
light load adjustment is regulated
by the “ light load plate” which
carries a small copper punching
under the potential pole. The
plate is moved laterally by a micrometer screw
to secure the desired compensation.
The permanence of magnets and the
simplicity and convenience of adjustments
are two of the many good points of Type
I-14 Meters.

IMPORTANT
Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as ‘‘the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free 1 /?,. Free sample posted.

try this Recipe

ICE CREAM
"JD each large tin of NESTLES
Sweetened "Condensed Milk add two
(2) Milk tins of cold water previously
sterilized by boiling (i.e., two (2) parts
water to one of Nestles Milk). To
this mixture add 17ozs. Nestles
Pure Thick Cream. Colouring to suit
(about 4 drops) and one or two (2)
teaspoonsful of best quality Vanilla
Essence (according to strength), pinch
of Table Salt.
For convenience of adding salt mix
1 oz. fine salt with 1 lb. Icing Sugar ; add
14 teaspoonful of this mixture to each
tin of Milk.
Place the whole of ingredients in
Freezer, pack closely with Ice and Salt
in usual manner, and churn until frozen.
Time.—Twenty minutes. Churn quickly
for first 7to 10 minutes. This makes
2 Quarts of ICE CREAM.

IN BANANA LAND
Bananaland Treasurer lias crashed in with
a heavy State income tax on top of the Fed-
eral ditto and a very proper land tax. The
exemption runs up to £2OO a year; after
that taxable income is divided into five
classes—•
(Vi Individual's .income from personal exertion.
(2) Individual’s income from property.
(3) Income by a company in ordinary business.
(4) Ditto by a company controlling" a monopoly
or working a public utility.
(5) Income of absentees.
The bigger the income the stiffer the tax.
So far as cos. working public utilities are
concerned a new principle is adopted—there
will be a tax of Is. in the £ up to G per
cent., and a super-tax running up to 3s. in
the £ on dividends of 17 per cent, or more.
\\ hy a co. having a “monopoly” should be
taxed more heavily than an indivi-
dual with one isn’t at all clear. It
is far better, as in N. S. Wales
not to . tax the co. at all, but to tax
the individual shareholder. A. co. may be
made up of 100 people without a Fatman
among them ; on the other hand, the biggest
batman in the country may escape stiff taxa-
tion by always playing a lone hand and
never subscribing any capital to companies
wnoso object is to mine or manufacture
something, or otherwise develop the country.
The Ryan Government has its own notions
of what is a decent thing. It handed over
the Government boat Lucinda to the Adela
Panklmrst crowd for a picnic down the
Bay, whereat a lot of stop-the-war ideas
weie broached and a Yankee poem was dis-
tributed e.ntitled : “I didn’t raise my son to
be a soldier.” Brisbane boasts a good few
score mothers who did raise their sons to be
soldiers, and if there are any picnics about
these are the women for a loyal Government
to entertain —not the Pankhursts. What
have these women done to deserve any public
attention —except from the police?
. Apropos the demand of Queensland
judges to have an open account for expenses,
this letter was once received by a Northern
boniface:—-
