do.
Justice Real threatened the other day, as
•1 warning to the Bar, to cut off two days’
fees from counsel because the case appeared
to liim to have been unnecessarily prolonged.
All right. And now what about the time
that chattering Judges waste—more of it in
Queensland than in any other State of the
Commonwealth ?
Andrew Hunter, who, with Professor
fckertchley, is credited with having sug-
gested the famous Channel-net scheme to the
naval authorities, is seriously ill.
Wren drops out of the Daily Mail, and
ssnarpe, M.H.R., founder of the Standard.
comes in. There is also to be a new editor
—Alf Buchanan, of the Age— and most
likely a new name.
Itan ’The drought has found a new use
tor the prickly-pear curse. In some districts
the settlers, driven to desperate straits for
green vegetables, are using the young leaves
as food. They cut them into strips, and
after boiling serve with salt and pepper. It
is declared that in one district there are
hard-up settlers who are forced to make the
vegetable the chief item on the menu.
Bananaland’s military authorities have
been given a lesson in thoroughness by the
women of Brisbane. A letter was received
at headquarters to say that 200 sandbags
would arrive during the day. While the
typiste was writing a brief thanks two
lorries came groaning to the barracks. They
carried the sandbags and each bag teas filled
with good Australian earth.
NIGGER THEOLOGY.
MAGISTRATE: “You Christian, Toly?"
TOBY: “Dunno."
MAGISTRATE : “Where you go when you die?"
TOBY: “Me go ’longa Mission Station, mine thinkil."
Wrote old Dr. Lang in the Empire 50
years ago :
It is somewhat remarkable that the only natural
harbor of first-rate character in Queensland should
have been virtually ignored by the Government and
people of that colony. Such, however, is the fact.
For whoever hears of Port Curtis in connection with
Queensland? Both Government and people seem to
say in the words of the song, “Oh, no! We never
mention it.’ We are willing to do anything to
compel Nature to give us harbors at Moreton Bay
or Keppel Bay where Nature never intended to give l '
us one. But Port Curtis —a harbor confessedly only
second to Port Jackson—we make no account of it
whatever. We almost wish we had never heard of it.
And Queensland hasn’t got over that in-
sanity yet.
Queensland Commissioner for Health
strongly recommends the enforcing of
the State’s admirable legislation for the
prevention of venereal disease. Dr. Elking-
ton goes further and advises compulsory
insurance against the red plague so as to
insure free treatment at night clinics. But
Bananaland is stuffed, chock-a-block, with
admirable Acts that every Government in-
sists on dodging.
Advt. from a northern paper:—
Caution is hereby- given that persons found shoot-
ing trapping or otherwise destroying wallabies,
bandicoots, bears or carpet snakes over two feet
long in my paddock will be prosecuted, for since
the rise in beef and the closing of butcher’s-
shops, I require all the said stock for my own
use. .
If lie can only get some English pickles and
Indian chutney and Yankee asparagus,
things won’t be so bad as they are in Servia,
anyhow.
-Llie railwaymen have been doirg their
little bit. To date 856 of them have gone
into camp and those who have remained at
home have parted with £15,698 to various
funds.
The incredible has again occurred in
Queensland. In the ballot for the Alton
Downs railway 92 persons voted for the
scheme and 93 against, and the line was
therefore- rejected. Israel never heard of
such a thing before.
Brisbane is not far from a water-famine,
llie supply of the liquid that some people
drink neat is almost down to the 1902
drought-level, but with this unpleasant dif-
ference, that now there is 40 per cent, more
population to supply.
Bananaland is making a great mistake: it
is just about to limit the appointment of its
Auditor-General to a term of seven years.
Thus a man’s appointment is to be made
contingent upon the favor with which he can
get_ himself regarded by Ministers and other
politicians; whereas the Auditor-General’s
present job ought to he to get himself
thoroughly disliked among politicians by
telling the truth about their finances.
From a Brisbane Courier 50 years old
this November :—■
We were shown a nice sample of sugar made from
yellow canes grown by Captain Hope on his Cleve-
land plantation. The crushing machinery consisted
of a blacksmith’s vice, a saucepan, a billy and a
small perforated box for drying the juice. Value
of sample shown about £32 a ton.
The crudeness, of the apparatus was as con-
spicuous as tthe pluck of the operators.
Bananaland’s Labor Government didn’t
take long to make up its mind to jettison
the futile Guaranteed Railway system. In
the nine years that the plan' led a miser-
able life, 87 lines were built. Of these six
paid the required 3 per cent., 18 squeezed
? ut a fraction of interest, and the remain-
ing 13 failed to yield even working expenses.
The outstanding amounts against these lines
total £168,000, and the guarantors will have
to unload the burden with as much piety as
they have about them.
“Politicus”: Meddling with food stocks
and prices is a ticklish and. risky business
for any Government. Queensland bakers
are compelled by the Government to use
very inferior flour at £l9 7s. Gd. per ton,
while the price of good flour down South
lias been £ll 17s. Gd. per ton. Best Adelaide
wheat can be landed in Brisbane at £lO 10s
per ton c.i.f. ; but if the State Ministry
tai s importation it stands to lose
£20,000 on the stocks of the wretched Bar-
latto-Russo wheat still in hand. Hence dear
wheat, clear bread, and a fine object-lesson
iu State muddling.
The Substitute.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Offices are being filled with girls in place of males
who have gone to the Front. —News item.
She sits before her ledger, sedate and sweet and
wise;
She takes no heed of anything but what’s beneath
her eyes;
Her brother’s on the Tiger, her cousin’s off the
Nore,
Her dad is at the Dardanelles, her lover’s at the fore.
So free and independent she trips along the street,
Intent to earn her dollars she stirs her pretty feet;
No more the household handmaid or toy of tyrant
Man,
She’s out to show the carpers how much a Woman
can.
The ink is on her fingers, her pen’s behind her ear;
And in her eye, I fancy, I sometimes see a tear;
Hut level is her headpiece, and in her bosom true
She wears a lasting image of one who’s said adieu.
Her mind is on the business, her heart’s away in
Fra nee,
And ev’ry morn it’s further off with French’s last
advance ;
And if the shrapnel strike him who heads a gallant
crew,
Alas ! I sadly fear me, ’twill strike a lassie, too.
Her brother’s on the Tiger, her cousin’s off the Nore;
Her dad is at the Dardanelles, her lover’s at the
fore;
She sits before her ledger, sedate and sweet and
wise,
Hut ciphers aren't the only things that swim before
her eyes.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, November 27, 1915.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
The hospital ship Ivanowna, due to-mor-
row, has Capt. Melbourne, of the 9th Bat-
talion, on board. A soldier who lost his
arm at Gallipoli told me that “every man of
the 9th who could walk” would be on the
wharf to make the captain feel at home
even although there were no trenches handy.
Killed in action, Lieut. C. H. W. Thom,
youngest son of a Maryborough family. The
young man used to belong to the Water Sup-
ply Department’s staff of engineers.
Noel Marlay, recently manager of Ara-
bella station, who is off to pot Turks, has
placed the half-hoop on the acquiescent finger
of Stella Richmond, daughter of Mr. Rich-
mond, of the Westbrook Experiment Farm.
Major Macfarlane is back again on mili-
tary business, and, incidentally, recovering
from a wound.
Ernest Simkin, formerly of the Courier
writing staff, has got into dungarees, which
is the first stage on the road to khaki. His
two brothers are already somewhere in the
great disturbance.
Bananalanders generally, and Health
Commissioner Dr. Moore in particular, are
filled with unbrotherly love over the in-
effective measures taken in N. S. Wales to
prevent smallpox spreading from Newcastle
to the Northern State. Dr. Moore visited
the port that has coal dust in its ears and
curses in 50-odd languages to make a f- '
inquiries, and discovered that patients had
not been isolated, but were merely urged i,.
a, friendly sort of way to stay at home.
The Brisbane Women’s Club held its
annual meeting on Thursday. After a most
exciting ballot, in which Miss Martha Burns
and Miss Freda Bage looked like making a
dead heat for the presidency, the former
sprinted and won by a single vote. But
every woman present firmly refused to be
made secretary. Perhaps the length of the
annual report frightened them.
The Fiveways branch of the Crftche and
Kindergarten held a sale of work to-day.
which Mayoress Davey, wearing dove grey
with touches of shell pink, blessed before
going mi to a Red Cross affair at East Bris-
bane.
.The Christmas toy fund, run by the Patri-1
otic committee, grows apace. At least eight
Christmas _ trees are to be laden for the
youngsters pleasure, and if there is any sign
of a shortage in their satisfaction VI vege-
tables wfill be torn from the virgin bush and
decorated with Japanese and other imported
toys. Talking of Christmas trees, there’s
another that mustn’t be forgotten—the big-
one for the Children’s Hospital.
The Brisbane Amateur Operatic Co. pro-
duced “The Cingalee” to-night, and will
keep it going for a week in aid of various
funds. Mrs. E. R. B. Jordan as Nanoya.
George Webster as Chambuddy Ram, Mrs.
Harris and little Miss Osborne were the best
o! a. very good cast. The costumes were a
ciedit to Mrs. Hunter, of Sydney, who stage-
managed the show. Mr. E. R. B. Jordan
put the orchestra through the customary
evolutions.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
One of Labor’s first jobs when it assumed
control was ta *eell_off some “permanent and
reproductive” derelicts. The other day
tenders for the sale of the dredge Groper
were opened. The out-of-date mud-raker was
not rushed,! although a couple of extra boilers
• a m set of compound engines were thrown
m. The first two tenders read £35 and £5O
respectively. Restoratives were called for
and the other tenders opened. The dread-
nought finally went at £370. Heaven only
knows how-jnuch of Cohen’s good money it
represents.
The butchers of Brisbane the other day set
out after the blood of Minister for Lands
Hunter. The retail price of salt meat had been
fixed at 3d. and 31d. the lb., and the butchers
saw red. Hunter listened to their expostu-
lations and their threats to close the shops.
Ihen he had his say. The Government had
obliged the meatworks (much against their
will) to sell frozen meat to the retail shops
at between 2d. and 3d. The retail shops
bought it up by the ton, yet the public could
not buy frozen meat anywhere. (Here two
or three of the butchers nearest the door
slipped out.) Prime fresh briskets, how-
ever, were exceedingly plentiful at 7d. to
'VI. The deputation shuffled its hat, mut-
tered something that sounded like “Wasn’t
me, and adjourned sine die.

HAVE YOU GOT
CATARRH ?
If you have a dropping of phlegm into the throat,
If your nose is stopped up,
If crusts form in your nose,
If you are easily susceptible to coldn,
If you suffer from frontal - headacheo,
If you have noises in the head,
If your hearing is affected,
If you have to clear your throat and expectorate often,
If a change in the weather affects you,
If you are a victim to shortness of breath,
If you sneeze frequently,
If your sense of smell is affected,
If you have a heavy, tired feeling on awaking,
If you suffer from a husky throat,
If you are affected with any of the above symptoms,
the chances are that you are suffering from
Catarrh. It is an exceedingly dangerous com-
plaint, and is so insidious that the sufferer hardly
realises how serious is his or her condition until
matters have reached an almost chronic stage.
Catarrh often begins with a cold in the head, but it never st °P s ' tller ,®;
The simple cold becomes a protracted one, while tne discharge from the
nose grows more profuse and uncomfortable.
The inflammation extends to the throat and bronchial tubes causing
hoarseness, a tickling sensation, and an aggravating cough. Itie matter
that is continually dropping hack into the throat finds us. wa in to the
stomach, resulting in a distressing form of dyspepsia, nausea, and loss of
appetite and strength.
The catarrhal poisons are absorbed into the blood, and all the membranes
of the body become infected. What was supposed to be a purely local
disease has become constitutional, deep-seated and chronic, and may
eventually result in Deafness, Bronchitis, Asthma, Neuritis, Catarrh of
the Stomach, or that dread scourge of the human race, Consumption.

ESTABLISHED OVER 30 YEARS.
Business. Bobbery, etc.
“Glen”: “Hori” (B. 11/11/T5) alleges
that the Edina has “retired from business.
There is, on the contrary, many a kick m
the old girl yet. A few days ago she was
at Portarlington, which is a neat, little
farming and boarding-house seaport near
Geelong. It was the intention of the cap-
tain to leave behind him on the wharf a
waggon-load of furniture (£280 worth, in-
cluding the waggon) which had just been
taken off: the vessel. The old girl decided
differently. With one of her mast-stays she
caught tiie waggon in an affectionate grip,
pulling the whole caboodle off: the wharf
into 12ft. of water, amid the ohs of almost
the entire population—a noteworthy effort
for an old sea-lady of 61.
“Y.Z.”: What class gets it hardest as a
result of the war? I nominate the keepers
of popular boarding-houses. The distressed
dame engineering a hash emporium and
bedding-down place catches it coming and
going. To begin with prices have gone up
to such a preposterous altitude that keeping
boarders is an expensive hobby; and, to
follow on. the war has made such extensive
demands on the ranks of young men that
there are scarcely any boarders left to
keep. Mrs. Ashley has opened her heart to
me. “I dunno what I’m goin’ t’ do, I’m
sure. The war’s taken all my young men
with any profit in ’em. Them that’s left is
all the hungriest, which eat a body out of
house and home. Mrs. Spicer and Mrs.
Brown, keepin’ boarders, ha’ noticed the
same thing. It's the light eaters is goin’
to the Front, the big feeders you couldn’t
shift with a cart ’orse.”
* * * *
“Eko”: Some men do make sacrifices in
the cause. In connection with the Aus-
tralian War Loan, the cash can be paid by
numerous instalments, but interest for the
first half-year will be allowed on the total
amount. This patriot placed his total in
the Savings Bank and got interest on his
balance there; paying it by instalments to
the War Loan he got interest for the whole
amount there. So for part of his capital
he got a 7i per cent, return. And in face of
this, he waved his paunch and boasted that
he was doing his little bit for his country.
I told him he was doing his country for his
little bit.
“Mulga”: I’ve earned a crust for years
with the axe. and I can assure “Austin S.”
(B. 2S/10/T5) that the bushwhacker’s im-
plement to-day is much superior to what it
was not so very long back. The old favor-
ites, the “Kelly” and the “Collie King,” have
been superseded by the “Collins” and the
“Plum,” the latter undoubtedly the best
tempered and best balanced axe of the lot.
The whole trouble is caused by a great
number of men who take on scrub falling
not knowing how to grind an axe according
to the timber. An experienced axeman has
three methods of doing up his “Douglas. ’
For hardwood he grinds it “short” ; for soft
wood, “long”; for medium timber the
“round” or “half-moon” grind from corner
to corner, this being the favorite in brigalow
and belah scrub. Also, no axe will stand if
not smoothed off with an oilstone after
embracing the grindstone. I say "hear,
hear” to manufacturing axes in Australia,
but the tariff fence will want raising to offer
any encouragement.

upon which was painted: “And what’s for
you?” Of course, that left the rider or
driver no option but to climb down and_
AN ADVERTISEMENT-WRITER'S SPRING SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They tell me it is Dryad-time amid the mossy
glades,
That Zimmeroons are frisking in the fern,
That all the pipes of Spring are fluting down the
cool arcades,
And in the calm lagoon the planets burn.
I'm a prophet of the practical, and dreams are not
for me,
Two sonnets and a rose don t make a Spiing!
She comes in wooden cases from the markets oveisea,
A tangible and lovely offering.
My banjo bids her welcome with its twankey,
Yankee twang;
The lute's been “tinned" a century or less;
But stanzas dearer far than ever worthy Homer sang
To ladies is my “Iliad of Dress ;
Nor do I sing of Truth, for Truth's been naked far
too long—
The doom of vulgar nudity is sealed.
The fount of all my fancy and the tenor of my
song
Is the sweet lure of the less-than-half revealed.
The lure of filmy blouses that are cut decollete—
Oh, pretty neck to kiss as white as milk !
A dainty skirt light-lifted as she trips across the
way,
Betraying ankles wonderful in silk.
A yard or two of voile can give a glory to a thigh ;
A lacy petticoat can play its part:
Whilst a pair of satin slippers lightly, lightly
stepping by,
Can tap a wireless message to the heart.
So as High Priest of Appearance on the altar steps
of Town
I chant the mystic Offices of Dress,
And make a daily preachment from the gospel of
the gown,
And give an offertory to the press ;
All npidens are Madonnas whilst the corset keeps
its trust —
Remember, ’tis the shell which shapes the egg ;
A yoke of lace insertion gives enchantment to the
bust,
And oftentimes the stocking makes the leg,
To the country-side its cattle, 0 my children of the
street—
’Tis mostly muddy where the grass is green!
So here are pretty carpets, and my labor is
complete,
For all the world is gay with crepe de chine.
The breast is in the bodice, and the arm is in the
sleeve,
Yet the bodice and the sleeve are everything;
Man likes to seek his woman in the land of Make-
believe,
So the bodice makes her Goddess of the Spring.
MAORI LAND.

When Prime Minister Hughes publicly
appealed to Australians to be sparing with
the sugar he only succeeded in flustering
consumers. As a result, suburban grocers
have been swamped with orders from house-
keepers who only saw in the appeal a hint
of shortage. In turn the grocers snowed-
under the wholesalers with orders, and
already the distributing trade is disorganised.
It is this sort of thing that explains the Ger-
man “ticket” system. The adoption of the
system did not necessarily mean a dearth of
meat, bread, etc., but it was the means taken
by the careful Hun to put a stop to the
prodigal waste threatened when every citi-
zen looked after himself by doubling his
ordinary stocks. It’s a pity (he German
takes things so seriously. Why cant lie
adopt Australia’s happy-go-dammit methods
and get blown-out at an early date?
Among the 37 candidates who want to be
Senators and are scrambling for the N.S.W.
Labor nomination, is J. F. Coates, who ran
against Miller for Bathurst in 1910 and then
earned from The Bulletin the recommen-
dation of being “a good Australian and a
capable platform orator.” For a man who
has never been in Parliament, Coates has
done a lot of things for his party. He and
the late J. L. Trefle framed its land policy,
so far as N. S. Wales is concerned, and it
was he who, in the face of J. C. Watson’s
opposition, pushed through a motion in 1911
requiring the selection of State Ministers by
Caucus, Coates’s idea being that this was
at least the half-way house to a completely-
elective Ministry.
“Two Seas”: In our N. S. Wales town-
ship the other evening Alderman Splodger
was addressing the brethren who had
assembled to decide whether the Sabbath
banana should be allowed to decay until
Monday:—
I have no doubt at all that this terrible war was
sent as punishment for our Sunday-trading sins.
That settles the question in one act. Aus-
tralians now know what they are fighting
for ; so On with the Scrap !

Medical.
THE USES OF TONICS.
Tonics are remedies which gradually re-
store tire various bodily organs, muscles and
nerye cells from a lax and sluggish state to
one making them capable of normal activity.
Tonics impart a sense of increased well-being
and strength. They are not to be confused
with stimulants which may give an immedi-
ate false strength. There are tonics for the
nerves, blood and stomach, but undoubtedly
the most useful is one having a combined
effect and generally benefiting the sys-
tem. Tonics are the most widely usefr' of all
medicines. In all forms of weakness, when
energy and vitality are needed; when the
blood is thin and weak and when the nerves
are run down and have lost their tone, a
tonic is prescribed. Amongst tonics the best
knrrwn is the much spoken of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. These pills have established a
high reputation as a blood-builder and nerve
tonic. They make the thin blood rich and
red, carry nourishment to weak and inflamed
nerves, increase the appetite and make diges-
tion normal, correct sleeplessness and most
forms of headaches. Try them for any
trouble caused by thin blood or weak nerves,
such as anaemia, rheumatism, neuralgia, sci-
atica, lumbago, indigestion, sick headache or
St. Vitus's dance.

who sends a request to the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Sydney.—Advt.

value in the treatment of chronic constipa-

normal activity, so that the use of an open-
ing medicine will not be a constant necessity.

STOPS FALLING HAIR.
This Home-made Mixture Stops Dandruff
and Falling Hair and Aids Its Growth.
To a half-pint of water add: —
Bay Rum loz.
Orlex Compound A small box.
Glycerine loz.
These are all simple ingredients that you
can buy from any chemist at very little cost,
and mix them yourself. Apply to the scalp
once a day for two weeks, then once every
other week until all the mixture is used. A
half-pint should be enough to rid the head of
dandruff and kill the dandruff germs. It
stops the hair from falling out, and relieves
itching and scalp diseases.
Although it is not a dye it acts upon the
hair roots and will darken streaked, faded
grey hair in 10 or 15 days. It promotes the
growth of the hair, and makes harsh hair soft
and glossy. [2

THE woman who tries Bostock shoes for the first
time realises, as soon as her foot slides home,
that here is something different, something
easier and more flexible, than anything she
has yet dreamed of. Then, when she steps out in
them, what an exultant feeling she has of lightness,
buoyancy and freedom. On the top of this, too, there
is that delightful confidence and assurance that conic
of being well-dressed and knowing it. For Bostock
shoes are always designed with one eye on the
fashions of the day and one eye on the ultimate sat-
isfaction of the wearer. The result is shoes so well
fitting and so easy that they feel just like one’s best-
loved old pair, yet so dainty, so original, so
thoroughly well made that they look and. after quite
a lot of wear, continue to look the newest and smart-
est things imaginable.

“Mid-Channel”: “Private Boxer” (B.
18/11/’l5) fears that it will surprise readers
to learn that J. C. Williamson rejected Ethel
Turner’s “The Sundowner” on the plea that
“Australians don’t care for Australian plays.”
With an isolated exception or two there
isn’t any doubt about it. They don’t. A
great proportion of present-day playgoers
belong to the stinking-fish brigade. Pass
them out something stuffed with cheap
Yankee slang and they’ll throw their arms
around it. They seem to make the cult of
the Yank their special worship. They wear
baggy trousers and square-shouldered coats,
have their hair cut d la Connecticut,
don bunion-shaped boots —and then imagine
themselves just it. They talk of “going
some,” of such and such a lady being “some
girl,” and allude to the half-crown as an
“ ’arf doller.” These are the chaps who roll
up at the box-office with their cash. Put
on “good stuff” and they stay away with
great emphasis.
“J.F.H.”: Every revue in London must
have a “Beauty Chorus” —an indispensable
adjunct, even though its component parts
don’t always suggest loveliness at first sight.
The Comedy Theatre in deserting the legiti-
mate drama for the all-pervading revue craze
has gone one better with a “Super-Beauty
Chorus.” But it discloses just the same old.
old collection of recruits —obvious shop-girls,
and parlor-maids, and domestic servants,
mostly, like those who supply the ordinary
beauty choruses, and who would be far
better off in their old billets.
Aggie Thorne, whom the Fred Niblos took
back with them to the U.S.A., is said to have
been booked for a part in the Blossom-
Ilerbert comic opera, “The Princess Pat.”
Old man Thorne ran a hotel at Shepparton
(Vic.) at the time his little Aggie was quali-
fying for entrance to stage life. She learned
her letters at the Windsor Convent and won
an Ormond piano-scholarship which took her
to the University Conservatorium, where she
chose singing as her second study. It was
this that presently obtained for her an open-
ing with the Royal Comics, not long before
her unfortunate marriage.
“Harrison O.” : While the Benighted States
have bred some first-class short-story writers
—E. A. Poe, Bret Harte, Ambrose Bierce
and O. Henry—it has failed to produce a
really great novelist, if we except Henry
James, a naturalised Britisher. Similarly,
looking back over years of suffering, I can-
not recall an Amurkan play which has risen
above' the ordinary, although there have been
dozens that would have made capital one-
acters. “A Pair of Sixes” is a case in point.
The author had material for a snappy short
story or curtain-raiser, and put it into a
three-act farce which, even with the enthusi-
astic assistance of a couple of intervals, lasts
only from 8.20 to 10.20, and drags a good
deal at that. The home of ,the quick lunch
would seem to be the natural breeding ground
for the one-act play; but the Yankee drama-
tist insists upon sandwiching one act of
drama between a couple of acts of padding.
Instead of basing short plays on short
stories, he jumbles together several short
stories—as in “Potash and I’erlmutter” and
“Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford”-—or else takes
a single short story, as in “Alias Jimmy
Valentine,” and hammers and stretches it
into three acts of thin melodrama. The
result is that American drama counts for
about as much as Woodrow Wilson’s Notes.
“Christian B.”: I have never been to
Poland, but I once recited in public about the
decline, slump and thud of the country. It
was at a big schools’ demonstration in Mel-
bourne Exhibition, and one of the awful star
turns was the recital —by 3000 small boys
shrieking in unison—of that stormy and
heart-rending poem, “The Downfall of
Poland.” Our conductor was a tall, severe
clerical person, and lie directed our united
yelp with a long white wand. After the lapse
of years, the only line I remember is, “And
Freedom shrieked when Kosciusko fell.”
After five hundred thousand rehearsals the
brighter spirits got into the way of render-
ing it thus: “And Free (damn!) shrieked
when Kosciusko (HellI))’ There was such
a lot of howling left over and so much be-
lated shouting that no one was ever caught.
Never before or since have I heard “Hell!”
yelled as loudly as it was on that occasion,
though I have heard the revivalist parson
bellow it frequently, and have also been pre-
sent when it was alluded to by the funny
man on the stage.
“Yarra Ben”: Australian playgoers had
much to thank the late George Edwardes
for; but the remembrance of one undoubted
“bloomer” still lingers. He foolishly billed
the crowd which arrived here something like
11 years ago, playing “Three Little Maids”
and “The Girl from Kay’s,” as a “Gaiety
company.” It was incorrect to start with, and
unfair to the public. The pieces were light
and slight (very slight) musical comedies,
or rather “comedies with music,” which had
never been done at the Gaiety, and the
“star,” George Huntley, had, so far as I
know, never appeared at the world-famous
Strand house. And the attenuated chorus
was far under Gaiety form. Of course
the company badly disappointed patrons,
and it was hardly to be wondered at that
the trip was not a booming success. Which
was rough luck for Huntley, one of the best
character comedians that ever set foot on
these (or any other) shores.
DULY QUALIFIED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The picture-play “producer” puffed
A large cigar and spoke.
“You may,” he said, “be, as you say,
The finest Hamlet of the day”—-
He blew a cloud of smoke—
“ But movie-mumming, you will find,
Needs gifts of quite another kind.”
“Just so,” Adolphus boomed; “but I,
With confidence, aver
This moving-picture work to be
Less strange than you might think to me;
For I of acting, sir,
Without an audience, have had
A long experience, by gad!
VICTORIA.

A Song of Sydney.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OLEAR-CUT, tile bills are lifting
Blue peaks to evening skies,
And, grey and soft as twilight,
The dreaming harbor lies.
Far up the misty valley
The snow-clad mountains loom ;
The hillsides brown are royal
With rangiora bloom.
And, warm and slow, the west wind
Blows in from sunset seas ;
The sounds of swaying cordage
It makes among the trees —
Aye, o’er the western ridges
The sweet sea-wind wends down
To croon of other waters,
To sing of Sydney Town.
It sets the senses dreaming
Of lights beyond the bars
That sunset’s gleams are shaping
To screen the jealous stars.
Oh! dreams are only shadows,
Yet, like a dream come true
Are thoughts of you, O Sydney ;
Of happy thoughts of you,
Resting beside calm waters,
Or, with a woman’s whims,
Bidding the ocean breakers
Sing you their evening hymns!
In twilight’s gloom, you falter
To swoon in night’s embrace,
While fairies deck with jewels
Your gown of filmy lace.
Oh, Sydney in the starlight’s
A dream no clamor mars ;
Her myriad lamps flash heavenward
Their challenge to the stars ;
And o’er the tide her ferries
Like radiant shuttles go;
With threads of gold they flutter,
For ever to and fro.
Lights, red and blue and yellow—
And green and white and gold—
Her hands, that scatter jewels,
What largesse must they hold !
A-down the west wind lonely
The Manly boat-bells chime,
And shoreward sounds the cadence
Of bells that chant of Time.
The dawn-lights, shining seaward,
That start so far away !
The path of golden glory
That ends in Coogee Bay !
The sheen of copper gleaming,
As Day, with brazen hoofs,
Marches through Night’s dominions,
Gleams on a thousand roofs !
And, like an organ rolling.
The surf’s hoarse undertone
Tells of the night of wonder
When it. sang on alone.
There is no sunburned desert,
Nor lonely ocean swell,
Where Sydney’s call is heard not
By those who love her well.
The blue-crest of the ranges
A beauty rare reveals ;
The snow-clad mountains beckon ;
The misty vale appeals.
The royal rangiora,
The fragrant gorse and broom
Have filled my air with sweetness,
Yet there is always room
For that warm wind that whispers
And sings among the trees,
Because it tells of people
I love across the seas;
Of dreams that are but shadows,
Yet are as dreams come true;
Such as I dreamed in Sydney,
With you, and you, and you.
MAOKILAND.
WILL LAWSON.
The Mysterious Lodger.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ABELL rang violently on the gloomy
landing, and a loud, imperious voice
intoned the words: “Send up the
attendant, somebody! Tell Mrs. Blatters to
send up the attendant!”
There was a scuffling far down in the
basement, and a badly-rattled servant with
her cap over one ear darted out of the
kitchen and clattered upstairs, wiping her
hands on her apron as she ran.
At the head of the stairs the landlady
in black silk interrupted her headlong flight
and sternly demanded the reason of her
hurry.
“It’s Mr. Carsback, Mrs. Martin,” gasped
the girl. “ ’E’s singin’ out f r th’ ’tendant!”
“What does he want nowf"
“I dunno,” muttered the girl.
“Has he been calling me Mrs. Blatters
again?” persisted the sombre landlady.
“Yes, ’e ’ as ,” replied the girl; “’e called
yer that not two minutes ago.”
“Did you tell him that my name was Mrs.
Martin?”
“Yes, I’ve told ’im three times now, mum.
But ’e don’t seem t’ take no notice.”
The bell on the second landing rang again.
“Send up Mrs. Blatters!” commanded the
voice of the lodger.
Mrs. Martin seized hold of her funereal
skirts with both hands and went slowly up
the stairs with a dreadful air of deliberation.
The awe-stricken maid followed reverently
in her wake. , ~ _
Arrived at the lodger s door the landlady
tapped smartly with her beringed fingers and
remarked in a deadly cold voice, “Will you
be good enough to open the door, Mr. Cars-
bftek
There was the sound of a chair being
pushed back, then four slow, creaky foot-
steps. The door opened and a slim, studious-
looking man of fifty, dressed in black, with
a rather unkempt grey beard, stood bowing
stiffly. .
“Mrs. Blatters, I see you have arrived at
‘‘Before we go any further, Mr. Carsback,
T desire to inform you that my name is not
Blatters.! .It is Martin.” .........
Carsback stared angrily through his gold-
rimmed glasses. “That doesn't matter! I
always call my landladies ‘Mrs. Blatters’ —
ii saves time!”
"If you will be good enough, sir”-
“My lirst landlady was called Mrs. Blat-
ters, and I adopted it as convenient, and I
cannot be bothered learning the names of
people I may never see again.”
“I refuse to be treated in this way ! If
you can’t call me by my proper name,
then”
“One moment, madam ! My name for all
grocers is Dadley—l don’t like it as a name,
but my lirst grocer was called that!”
“Mr. Carsback! I will not be insulted in
this way! Either you call me by my proper
name or else you must go!”
“Hush!” thundered the lodger command-
ingly.
The landlady sprang back smartly.
“What’s the matter?”
-/(-/” hissed the lodger, finger on lips,
and eyes goggling.
The landlady sh-h’d.
“The cranes are about to nest!” whis-
pered Carsback confidentially, and shut the
door in her face.
The rattled landlady thought it over for a
minute and then, plucking up her courage,
knocked smartly on the door.
“Mr. Carsback ! Open, if you. please ! I
want you to explain what you said !”
The door opened explosively and Cars-
back stuck his head out for long enough to
say: “Impossible! Mrs. Blatters, I never
explain anything I have said —it always
sounds stupid!” Then lie shut the door.
The landlady turned about and went
downstairs, where she found a group ol
scared-looking lodgers waiting for particu
lars. ,
“And what do you think of that? she
asked when she had done. “Fancy .lm
allowing a man of that sort to come into
the house!”
"I think his mind must be affected," said
the pale young woman with spectacles.
“Overstudy or something, you know.”
“But he spoke quite rationally when he
was engaging the room—couldn’t have been
more gentlemanly.”
“I suppose it comes and goes,” murmured
the girl drearily. .
“We’ll just have to humor him, ’ said the
landlady miserably. “Don’t tell any of the
others at present—that Mr. Bodger, for
instance. I’m sure he would lose his tem-
per. Sh-h ! Here’s Mr. Carsback now ! ’
As the grave-looking man came down the
stairs the group melted away; but almost
immediately the drone of the dinner-gong
called them from their refuges to assemble
in the dining-room, where ten small tables
were already laid. Mrs. Martin hovered
anxiously about the doorway as the lodgers
came in, and sighed with relief when Cars-
back obediently took the chair she pulled
out for him at a small table in the far
corner, Presently Bodger, the uncrowned
but full-whiskereu king of the house, arrived,
and the landlady rustled to her place, where
a plate of soup was rapidly getting cold.
The diners had just settled down, when a
deafening spoon-and-plate clatter broke out
at the table in the corner. The startled
lodgers, wheeling round on their chairs,
found the ncw-comer on his feet. “Am I
to be left here like a dog?” he roared, glar-
ing round about him.
The agitated landlady rose in a wave of
black satin.
“Mr. Carsback! You must not make these
stupid remarks!”
“Am I to be left here like a dog ?” thun-
dered the embarrassing diner, striking a
sort of Budget-speech attitude.
Mr. Bodger rose in his place, beard
bristling and fury in his eye. “Go outside,
sir!” he thundered back with a lordly wave
of his hand.
The effect was instant and startling. With
a low howl the new lodger dropped to table-
level, and, using only finger-tips and toes,
scampered out of the place like a monkey,
his bouncing coat-tails waving a flippant
adieu as he went down the steps into the
warden.
The sympathetic girl gave a little shriek
and fainted in her chair. As the boarders
nearest her started to render first-aid, Bod-
ger looked angrily about him. “Where did
that fellow come from?”
Mrs. Martin looked at him appealingly.
“He arrived this morning, Mr. Bodger! He
seemed quite gentlemanly then —I hadn’t the
least idea he would be so rude.”
“Rude!” echoed Bodger. “God bless my
soul, the man’s as mad as a March hare!
You’ve onlv got to look at him to see he’s
mad! I tell you what! I'm not going to
sleep in the same house as a lunatic! I
don’t want to have my throat cut in my
sleep.”
A startled murmur ran round the room.
“Oh, Mr. Bodger, now! I don’t think
he’s as bad as all that! Perhaps he’s only
joking!”
“Yes, yes!” chattered the boarders. No
need to be alarmed, Bodger. The chances
are he’s only joking.”
Something utterly mad, dressed in a frock-
coat and pants, but otherwise dog-like, ran
in the door, and in and out amongst the
tables. “Onlv joking!” repeated the face
of it. “Just doing it for exercise, you know.
When it had arrived at the vacant chair
it straightened up and became a human
being again ; then, sitting down, it spooned
up the almost cold soup with great relish,
while the other lodgers giggled and con-
ferred together.
Mr. Bodger for his part sat with solemn
eyes protruding—he was obviously at burst-
ing point. “D —d impertinence!” he kept
muttering to himself. “Pitch him out neck
and crop! Mad as a March hare!”
As soon as he had finished his soup the
amazing Carsback wiped his lips, looked
Bodger in the eye, and remarked in an easy,
conversational way: “And what do you
think of the weather, sir?”
Bodger’s only reply was a snort.
The inquiry was carefully repeated—in a
venomously polite tone this time. “Pardon
me. sir, but I asked you a ouestion. W hat
do vou think of the weather?”
Bodger stared insolently at the questioner
and then carefully drew a paper from the
tail-pocket of his coat and unfurled it before
his face. He hadn’t read more than two
lines when a flying loaf of bread tore the
/Continued on next page.)

flashed with it into the garden beyond.
“The weather!” shouted the thrower to
the unmasked reader. “What do you think
of it? Tell me at once!” His right hand
with a plate in it went up and back.
“Don’t you throw that at me!” shouted
Bodger, terrified and holding a tea-cosy
before his purple face. “Drop that, I tell
you!”
“Oli-h!” wailed the lady boarders. “Tell
him what you think!”
“Yes, tell him, Mr. Bodger!”—this from
the landlady.
“Somebody take that plate from him !” in-
sisted Bodger in a tremendous voice. The
roomful shrieked at him to tell and get it
over.
“ Hush!” It was the lunatic demanding
silence with imperious gestures. lie had
put the plate down, and seemed to have only
one desire in the world —to restore peace
and quiet.
“Confound you!” stormed Bodger, whose
courage was returning. “Why should we
‘hush’ when you tell us?”
“Because,” answered Carsback, sweetly
reasonable, like one who humors fools,
“because the cranes are about to nest!”
Then he sat down.
Bodger shook a hairy fist at him. “Don’t
you try any more of your d—d nonsense on
me!”
“You know,” said the now-seated Cars-
back, leaning his bead on bis elbow and
addressing the room generally, and ignoring
Bodger, “my people intended me for the min-
istry, but I walked away to sea and was
never heard of again. 11 hut is that gentle-
man there standing up for? What is he
muttering about?”
“This is all humbug and play-acting!”
trumpeted Bodger. “All these monkey tricks
and nonsense—they’re all play acting!”
“I don’t know what be is talking about!
Does anybody know what he is talking
about?”
“More of it!”
The landlady rose making semaphore signs
at Bodger. “Oh, please don’t excite him. Mr.
Bodger! I’m sure he would be all right if
you didn’t excite him so!”
“Yes!” yelped the boarder pack, “you
excite him too much ! Give him a chance
and he'll be all right!”
Bodger at once became apoplectic. “Well,
what am I to do? Am I to let him throw
things at me?”
“You wouldn’t answer him, Mr. Bodger !
You know you wouldn’t answer him When
lie spoke to you !”
“Yes, and you held the paper before you,
old man !”
Bodger rose to his feet. He looked as if
he couldn’t have breathed any longer in a sit-
ting position. “Am I to sit quietly here,” he
stormed, “while a man runs about on his
fingers and toes like a dog? Am I to answer
the impertinent questions of lunatics?”
“Sh-li!” (This from the terrified land-
lady.)
“Thank you, Mrs. Blatters!” boomed the
lunatic, who had been listening gravely.
“My name is not”
The lunatic waved liis hand. “I have dis-
posed of that question.” Then, settling him-
self to listen, he commanded: “Continue,
Bodger!”
Bodger choked with surprise and grasped
at his hand. “I —I—l’m d —d if I will!”
He then sat down as it' lie never intended to
get up again.
The lunatic was promptly on liis feet.
Glancing round at the speechless company,
he announced : “Would anybody else like to
say a few words before I put the motion to
the meeting?”
Mrs. Martin rang the handbell violently.
“Bring in the second course,” she com-
manded, when the scared-looking maid
appeared at the door.
“But is it allowable, Mrs. Blatters? I
have doubts whether the standing orders per-
mit the second course to be introduced at
this stage!”
“Sit down!” shouted Bodger and the two
other men-boarders who bad risen with him,
and rather to their surprise the lunatic did
so. The law clerk, with a meaning side-
glance at the crestfallen and preoccupied
figure at the side table, muttered something
about “ringing up,” and left the room. The
soup-plate collecting was then performed in
a buzz of nervously subdued conversation.
Bodger sat and gloomed ferociously alone.
The venturesome one or two who tried to
enter into conversation with him he repulsed
rudely; he considered the house had treated
him unjustly—had, in fact, tried to sacrifice
him to a homicidal maniac in the interests
of peace and quiet. So he took it out in
bad temper.
The tinkle of the telephone bell was heard
faintly from the hall.
During the distribution of second-course
orders the maid created a slight diversion by
asking hoarsely what was to be done with
the loaf tli’ gen’l’man had pitched at Mr.
Bodger.
“Where did it fall ?”
“In a rosebush, Mrs. Martin.”
“If it is quite clean,” said the landlady
with a meaning look at the thrower, “bring
it in again !”
“Bring the loaf to me!” commanded the
lunatic sharply.
Bodger pointed a fat forefinger at him,
“You’ll have a slice, my friend !”
“Bring the loaf to me, girl!”
“Sit down!”
But the lunatic didn’t sit down this time.
Instead he took out liis watch, looked at it
closely and remarked gravely. “I think the
cranes are about to nest now!”
Bodger cleared his throat ferociously and
shot a look of inquiry at the law-clerk, who
had returned unobtrusively from the tele-
phone and slipped into his seat.
“Well?” asked Bodger impatiently, after
the law-clerk had vainly tried to signal his
message silently with his eyes and lips. The
expectant boarders subdued their knife-and-
fork play to catch the reply, spoken as
quietly as possible.
“Been looking for him for hours. Attend-
ant coming down in a car now. Here in a
few minutes.”
“H —m!” growled Bodger, and went on
with his dinner. The boarders sighed their
relief and went on with theirs —quietly, as
though any sudden noise might break the
spell.
And presently when the people in the
dining-room looked and sounded just like
any other scratch crowd of feeders, the
hurried whirring of a motor was heard at
the gate and scuffling feet hurried up the
garden path.
“In there!” rang the voice of the cook;
and in through the door leading into the
garden came a humble-looking little man,
very red in the face and perspiring profusely.
“So there y’are!” was his first greeting to
the disgraced Oarsback. “I bin lookin’ for
you all over the place!”
Carsback rose from his chair, a look of
pained remembrance on his face. Putting
his hand to his brow, he muttered “I forgot!
—I completely forgot!”
“Just like you!” retorted the newcomer,
slily distributing outrageous winks to the
onlookers.
“Yes, I forgot we had an appointment to
burn down the Synagogue!”
“Well, ’ere I am, Mister James, with a
car full of hile and kindlin’! If you get
your ’at now an’ come quick we may be jest
in time!”
“Then we will go at once ! Are the cranes
nesting yet?”
“Not yet, sir. But if you come along now,
sir, I think they might settle down. They
bin missin’ you, them cranes ’a've.”
“I’m sorry! I don’t know how I came
to be detained here. Mrs. Blatters, why
have you detained me here?”
The landlady waved her hands at those
nearest the door. “Get his bag and hat, for
goodness sake! They’re in his room!”
Three people rushed to do her bidding.
“Mrs. Blatters! Why have you?”
“Never mind about that, sir i We’re off
now, any’ow!”
“Yes.” boomed Bodger, “and thank Heaven
for it!”
“Go outside!” shouted the lunatic in exact
imitation of the dog-ordering senior lodger,
who went purple in silence.
“Now, sir! Now, sir! Don’t you fret
yourself, sir! We’ll never git to the Sinner-
gog in time if you fret yourself! Thank
yer, miss!.... Now, ’ere’s your ’at an’ bag,
sir! Now we’re right!” As he led his
charge to the door the landlady drew near
expectantly.
“Th’ doctor’ll fix up with you. missus!”
he whispered over bis shoulder. The pair of
them went down the garden path slowly, con-
ferring together secretly. When they were
comfortably seated in the car and the
chauffeur had started his engine the warder
had to shout to make himself heard. So
it happened that part of his last speech
reached the watchers on the verandah.
What they heard was: “The ol’ ijit with
the _ whiskers, sir? Yes, Mister James.
Orright, we’ll come back an’ murder ’im
next Wednesday night.”
KODAK.
Feet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The city’s a-rattle with pattering feet.
On roadway and pavement, and over
The doorsteps of shops where they’re
retailing meat
Grown juicy on cowslips and clover
The feet pitter-patter; and some are so
neat
That they seem to reproach a poor sinner
Who shabbily slinks down the populous
street,
Down-at-heel, on the way to his dinner.
There are feet long and slender that coax-
ingly please,
And teasing wee feet shod in ticklesome
threes.
There are feet with high insteps and peril-
ous heels
Of a style that the mood of the wearer
reveals.
There are shoes so demure, such an arrless-
ness glows in ’em,
That the veriest cynic might kiss the pink
toes iu ’em.
There are feet spick and span, each an im-
pudent dart
Well chosen by Cupid for wounding the
heart.
There are feet move one’s naughtiest
thoughts to contrition,
And feet that would fire the worst sluggard’s
ambition.
Ah, ladies, dear ladies, have mercy ! for, oh,
Your feet pitter-pattering trouble me so,
And a maddening melody exquisite steals
To my heart with each tap of your insolent
heels!
Feet! feet! winsome slight feet,
Tippety-tappeting over the street!
Life is a vapor. The thought oddly rankles
Here, as the trim, inaccessible ankles,
Each of ’em eager to whisper and coo,
Flash all about in a naughty to-do.
Would you be sure that*She’s perfectly
sweet.
You, doubting Thomas? —Well, look at her
feet.
If they are tricky and dainty and slim,
Make no delay but get into the swim.
(Love is a flyaway. Fate is a limb.)
Feet that are weary and toilworn go hv,
Thumpety-thud !.. . .Thumpety-thud !
Uppers bent over and laces awry,
Sullenly trudging swish-swish through the
mud.
Tragic, ripped seams with pale flesh peeping
through.
Feet going— ichere? Does it matter to you?
Out of our sight, you disconsolate feet,
Blotting and spotting the prosperous street!
Tippety-tap ! Tipjiety-tap !
Love, you plump fiiscal, you are a good
chap!
Why should we care, while we’ve fire in our
blood,
For the'poor feet that go‘'thumpety-thud!
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.
